The Fourth Proof: Attributes as Relations
Author presents fourth proof for distinction between attributes and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relational nature of attributes proves their distinction from the divine essence.

Core Idea
Attributes are relations, not the essence itself.

In this proof, the author argues that attributes like knowledge are relations (nisab) between the essence and the object. Since relations depend on the essence and are not self-subsistent, they must be distinct from the essence. This passage lays out this concise yet powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's flow.
Notice the rhetorical question 'How can we not say that?'
2
Identify the key premise
The author states that attributes are relations (nisab) and additions (iḍāfāt) that are dependent on the essence. Contrast this with self-subsistent essences.
3
Follow the conclusion
Because attributes are relational and dependent, while the essence is self-subsistent, they must be distinct. The proof ends with 'All of this necessitates distinction.'
This is the main point of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إنا بينا أنه عالم تحصل النسبة المسماة بالعلم ، لم يحصل كون العالم (۱) عالماً والنسبة بين ذات العالم وبين ذات المعلوم صفة لتلك الذات ، ومفتقرة إليها ، وكيف لا نقول ذلك ، والنسب والإضافات مقبولة بالقياس إلى غيرها ، والذوات القائمة بأنفسها ليست كذلك ، وكل ذلك يوجب التغاير ؟
The fourth proof: We have explained that He is knowing; the relation called knowledge occurs, but the thing being knowing does not occur [merely] from that relation. The relation between the essence of the knower and the essence of the known is an attribute of that essence, and dependent upon it. How can we not say that? Relations and additions are acceptable in comparison to others, but self-subsistent essences are not like that. All of this necessitates distinction.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a proof (ḥujjah) for the distinction between God's attributes and His essence. The author argues that when we say God is knowing, we refer to a relation (nisbah) called knowledge between the knower and the known. This relation is an attribute (ṣifah) of the essence (dhāt), and it depends on the essence. Relations and additions (iḍāfāt) can be predicated of other things, but self-subsistent essences (dhawāt qāʾimah bi-anfusihā) are not like that—they are not mere relations. Therefore, the attribute (the relation) must be distinct from the essence. The rhetorical question 'How can we not say that?' emphasizes the clarity of the conclusion.

Key Terms
علم
عالم
نسبة
إضافة
ذات
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that since attributes are closely tied to the essence, they are identical.
Correction: The proof shows they are distinct because attributes are relational and dependent on the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why must attributes be distinct from the essence?
A) Because attributes are self-subsistent.
B) Because attributes are relational and dependent on the essence.
C) Because the essence is multiple.
D) Because knowledge is the same as power.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that divine attributes are relations distinct from the self-subsistent essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about God's attributes, do you treat them as mere labels or as real relations that depend on His unchanging essence? How does this shift your understanding of who He is?
?
If God's knowledge, as a relation, is distinct from His essence but dependent on it, how does that affect the way you relate to His omniscience? Does it make you more aware that your own knowledge is just a relation to objects, while His is rooted in His very self?
?
The proof shows that relations can be added without changing the self-subsistent essence. When you sin and then repent, does God's essence change? What does this tell you about His constancy and your need to anchor your trust in His fixed nature, not in fluctuating feelings?

The Fifth Proof: Conceptual Distinction Between Power and Knowledge
Author presents fifth proof distinguishing 'powerful' and 'knowing' conceptually.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof that the divine attributes 'power' and 'knowledge' are conceptually distinct from each other and from the essence, and why this implies their real distinction from the essence.

Core Idea
If two concepts have different definitions, they cannot both be identical to the same essence.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from the text, which argues that because the concepts of 'being powerful' and 'being knowing' are different in their quiddity and definition, they cannot be identical to the divine essence. The author uses a simple thought experiment to show that equating each attribute with the essence would lead to a contradiction. By the end, you will see how this proof establishes that the attributes are really distinct from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins by stating the conceptual difference between being powerful and being knowing.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Understand the reasoning
Follow the author's logic: the concepts differ because each can exist without the other (powerful without knowledge, knowing without power). This shows they are not identical.
3
Examine the contradiction
The author argues that if both concepts were identical to the essence, the single essence would have to be two distinct things simultaneously, which is impossible.
This contradiction reveals that the attributes cannot be identical to the essence.
4
Apply the conclusion
Recognize that this proof implies the attributes are really distinct from the essence, not just conceptually. This is a key point in kalām.
The proof is part of a larger discussion on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن المفهوم من كون الذات قادرة ، غير المفهوم من كونها عالمة . وذلك لأن القادر قد يكون عالماً ، وقد لا يكون ، كما أن العالم قد يكون قادراً وقد لا يكون . فلما كان ماهية العالمية غير ماهية القادرية ، وحد العالمية غير حد القادرية ، فلو قلنا : إنها عين الذات لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد : لا يكون شيئاً واحداً . بل يكون شيئين متغايرين . وذلك محال .
The fifth proof: Indeed, the conception of the essence being powerful is different from the conception of it being knowing. That is because the powerful may be knowing and may not be, just as the knowing may be powerful and may not be. Since the quiddity of 'knowingness' is different from the quiddity of 'powerfulness', and the definition of 'knowingness' is different from the definition of 'powerfulness', if we said that each is identical to the essence, then a single thing would not be a single thing, but would be two distinct things. That is impossible.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is the fifth proof (al-ḥujjah al-khāmisah) for the distinction between attributes and essence. It relies on the conceptual difference between being powerful (qādir) and being knowing (ʿālim). The author notes that we can conceive of a powerful being that is not knowing, and a knowing being that is not powerful; their quiddities (māhiyyāt) and definitions (ḥudūd) are different. If both 'powerful' and 'knowing' were identical to the essence (ʿayn al-dhāt), then the one essence would have to be two distinct things (shayʾayn mutaghāyirayn) simultaneously, which is impossible. Thus, the concepts must be distinct, and since they are real attributes of God, they must be distinct from the essence as well.

Key Terms
مفهوم
قادر
عالم
ماهية
حد
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceptual difference between attributes does not imply real distinction; they are merely aspects of the same essence.
Correction: The proof shows that because the concepts differ in quiddity and definition, equating each with the essence leads to a logical contradiction. Hence, they must be really distinct from the essence.

Checkpoint
Why does the fifth proof conclude that 'being powerful' and 'being knowing' are not identical to the essence?
A) Because the essence cannot have multiple attributes.
B) Because their quiddities and definitions differ, making identity impossible.
C) Because power and knowledge are both eternal.
D) Because the Quran distinguishes them.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof establishes that the divine attributes 'power' and 'knowledge' are really distinct from the essence because their quiddities and definitions differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-Qādir (The All-Powerful) versus al-ʿAlīm (The All-Knowing), do you feel a different kind of reliance? This lesson shows they are distinct attributes—does your heart truly distinguish them in your trust and supplication? Challenge: In your next prayer, consciously reflect on each attribute and ask yourself which one you need most in that moment.
?
If the attributes were identical to the essence, your understanding of God would collapse into a single abstract concept. But here, the proof forces you to recognize real distinctions. How does this affect the way you relate to God's names? Are you tempted to reduce Him to a neat package of features, or do you embrace the complexity of His attributes as distinct manifestations of His reality? Commit to memorizing one of His names this week and meditate on its unique meaning.
?
The proof uses logic to defend tawhid. But does your own mind sometimes resist such distinctions because they feel too academic? This is a matter of how you know your Lord. If you are uncomfortable with the reasoning, is it because you haven't engaged with it, or because you fear it leads to complexity? Ask yourself: Are you willing to dive deeper into Islamic theology to know God more truly, even if it challenges your previous assumptions?

The Sixth Proof: Distinction Between Knowledge and Power
Author presents sixth proof by conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof for the distinction between divine knowledge and power, based on the objects each attribute relates to.

Core Idea
Knowing something does not mean having power over it.

We now examine the sixth proof (al-ḥujjah al-sādisah), which uses a conditional argument to show that knowledge and power cannot be identical. The author first states the conditional premise, then draws an implication, and finally shows that implication is false by appealing to rational consensus about the Necessary and Impossible. This forces the conclusion that the two concepts—and hence the two attributes—are distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if... then...'. Notice the key terms: wājib li-dhātihi, mumtaniʿ li-dhātihi.
The footnote marker (٢) is part of the original text—ignore it for now.
2
Identify the conditional premise and its implication
The author says: if knowledge and power were identical, then everything known would also be an object of power. What would that lead to?
Think about what is known: the Necessary in essence and the Impossible in essence are known to Allah.
3
Understand the counterexample
The author then states that rational people agree that the Necessary and Impossible are known but not objects of power. This contradicts the implication, so the premise must be false.
This shows that the concepts of 'known' and 'an object of power' are different.
4
Conclude the distinction
Since the concepts differ, the attributes themselves—knowledge and power—must be distinct from each other and from the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : لو كان كونه عالماً ، عين كونه قادراً ، لكان كل ما صح كونه عالماً به وجب أن يصح كونه قادراً عليه ، فكان يلزم في الواجب لذاته ، وفي الممتنع لذاته أن يكون مقدوراً ، كما أنه معلوم له وحيث أطبق أهل العقل أن الواجب لذاته والممتنع لذاته معلوم ، ( وليس بمقدور علمنا أن المفهوم من كون الشيء معلوماً ، مغاير للمفهوم من كونه مقدوراً ) (٢) وذلك يقتضي حصول المغايرة بين العلم وبين القدرة .
The sixth proof: If His being knowing were identical to His being powerful, then everything that is correct for Him to be knowing of would necessarily be correct for Him to be powerful over. Thus it would be necessary that the Necessary in essence (wājib li-dhātihi) and the Impossible in essence (mumtaniʿ li-dhātihi) be objects of power (maqdūr), just as they are known to Him. But all rational people agree that the Necessary in essence and the Impossible in essence are known, and are not objects of power. So we know that the conception of a thing being known is different from the conception of it being an object of power. That necessitates the occurrence of distinction (mughāyara) between knowledge and power.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is the sixth proof (al-ḥujjah al-sādisah) for the distinction between knowledge and power as attributes distinct from the essence. The author presents a conditional argument: if knowledge and power were identical (ʿayn), then anything known would also be an object of power. This would wrongly imply that the Necessary in essence (wājib li-dhātihi, i.e., God Himself) and the Impossible in essence (mumtaniʿ li-dhātihi, e.g., a partner with God) are objects of power—but rational consensus says they are known but not within power. Therefore, the concepts of 'known' (maʿlūm) and 'power over' (maqdūr) are distinct, and consequently knowledge and power themselves are distinct attributes.

Key Terms
واجب لذاته
ممتنع لذاته
مقدور
معلوم
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's knowledge and power are co-extensive—everything known is also an object of power.
Correction: The proof shows that the Necessary and Impossible are known but not objects of power, so the scopes differ.

Checkpoint
What would follow if knowledge and power were identical according to the sixth proof?
A) The necessary and impossible would be objects of power.
B) Everything known would also be created.
C) God would not know the impossible.
D) Power would be limited.

Key Takeaway
Because the Necessary and Impossible are known but not objects of power, knowledge and power must be distinct attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that God knows the impossible — things that can never happen — does it humble you to realize that His knowledge infinitely surpasses your own? How should this affect the way you speak about God's attributes?
?
The proof shows that God knows the Necessary in essence (Himself) and the Impossible, but He has power only over what is possible. Is there an area in your life where you are expecting God to do the impossible (like change a necessary fact) instead of trusting His wisdom in what He chooses to do? How can you realign your trust?
?
If God's knowledge and power are distinct, then His knowledge of your struggles does not automatically mean He will change them. Does this truth make you rely more on His wisdom rather than demanding His intervention? What does that look like practically in your dua (supplication)?

The Seventh Proof: Power Ceases, Knowledge Remains
Seventh proof: example of created object ending power but not knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof for the distinction between divine knowledge and power, based on the impossibility of re-creating what already exists.

Core Idea
Power ends with creation; knowledge remains. They must be distinct.

This lesson examines the seventh proof from the author's series distinguishing divine attributes. The argument uses a simple observation: once a powerful agent creates something, that specific act can no longer be repeated. Yet the created thing remains known. This difference in persistence shows that knowledge and power are not identical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the conditional structure and the conclusion.
The passage is concise; focus on the logical chain.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms qādir, maqdūr, ījād, taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil, muḥāl, and ʿilm. Understand their roles.
These terms form the backbone of the proof.
3
Trace the logical steps
Map out the argument: premise → reason → deduction about power → persistence of knowledge → conclusion of distinction.
This helps internalize the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن القادر إذا خرج مقدوره إلى الوجود . لم يبق قادراً على إيجاده بعينه. لأن إيجاد الموجود وتحصيل الحاصل محال . والمحال لا قدرة عليه ، فالمقدور بعد دخوله في الوجود لم يبق مقدوراً ، لكنه بقي معلوماً بعد خروجه إلى الوجود. وصدق هذا النفي ، والإثبات ، يوجب التغاير بين العلم. وبين القدرة (۳)
The seventh proof: The powerful (qādir), when his object of power (maqdūr) comes into existence, is no longer powerful over bringing it into existence exactly as it is, because bringing into existence what already exists and obtaining what is already obtained is impossible. And the impossible is not an object of power. So the object of power, after entering into existence, is no longer an object of power, but it remains known (maʿlūm) after its coming into existence. The truth of this negation and affirmation necessitates the distinction (mughāyara) between knowledge and power.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is the seventh proof (al-ḥujjah al-sābiʿah) for the distinction between knowledge and power. The author uses a clear example: a powerful agent (qādir) who creates something loses the ability to create that same thing again, because creating what already exists (ījād al-mawjūd) is impossible. The impossible is not an object of power (lā qudrah ʿalayhi). However, that created thing remains known (maʿlūm) to the agent after its existence. So power ceases with respect to that particular act, but knowledge persists. This difference in persistence—power ceasing while knowledge continues—shows that knowledge and power are distinct attributes.

Key Terms
قادر
مقدور
إيجاد
تحصيل الحاصل
محال
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: After creating something, the creator retains the power to recreate that exact same thing.
Correction: The proof shows recreation of exactly what exists is impossible (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), so power over that specific act ends once the object exists.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh proof, what happens to an object after it comes into existence?
A) It remains an object of power.
B) It ceases to be known.
C) It ceases to be an object of power but remains known.
D) Both power and knowledge cease.

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof demonstrates that power and knowledge are distinct because power over a specific act ends once it is actualized, whereas knowledge of it persists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have accomplished things in your life that you once had power to do. Do you still think you have power over those past achievements, or do you admit that your power over them ended the moment they were realized? How does this truth humble your pride?
?
The proof shows that power over a specific act ends with its completion, but knowledge remains. Where in your life are you trying to control what is already done, holding onto the illusion of power over past mistakes or successes? What would it mean to let go and only keep knowledge of them?
?
If God's power over a created thing ends with its existence, what does that say about your own attempts to 'recreate' or 'reimagine' what has already happened? Are you fighting against the impossibility of bringing back what is past? Surrender that false power today.

The Eighth Proof: Distinction Through Definitions and Properties
Author presents eighth proof based on definitions, properties, and conceptual distinctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth proof that divine attributes are distinct from each other and from the essence based on differences in definitions, rulings, and properties.

Core Idea
Differences in definitions and properties prove real distinction among attributes.

Sanusi presents the eighth proof, which uses the diversity of quiddities (māhiyyāt) to argue that knowledge and power are distinct attributes. If they were identical, their terms would be synonymous, but definitions, rulings, and properties show otherwise. This lesson walks through the logical steps of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof structure
Notice that the proof begins by presenting two alternatives: either the quiddities of knowledge and power are identical or different. The argument then eliminates the first alternative.
The proof uses a disjunctive syllogism.
2
Examine the consequence of identity
If the quiddities were identical, the words 'knowledge' and 'power' would be synonymous. Reflect on why this would be unacceptable.
Synonymy would mean no real difference in meaning.
3
Identify the three grounds of difference
The author lists definitions (ḥudūd), rulings (aḥkām), and properties (khaṣāʾiṣ) as evidence that knowledge and power are not identical. See how each differs.
These are objective criteria for conceptual distinction.
4
Understand the conclusion
From the above, it follows that the intelligible content of 'knowing' differs from 'powerful', and both differ from 'existent'. This proves the desired distinction.
The proof thus establishes three distinct concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : إن العلم ماهية مخصوصة ، والقدرة أيضاً ماهية مخصوصة ، فإما أن تكون إحدى الماهيتين عين الأخرى أو غيرها ؟ فإن كان الأول كان لفظ العلم ولفظ القدرة لفظان مترادفان ، يفيد كل واحد منهما ما يفيده الآخر . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل ، لأن حد العلم غير حد القدرة ، وأحكام العلم غير أحكام القدرة ، وخواص كل واحد منها مغايرة لخواص الآخر . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يكون المعقول من كونه عالماً ، مغايراً للمعقول من كونه قادراً ، وأن يكون المعقول من كل واحد منهما ، مغايراً للمعقول من كونه موجوداً . وذلك يفيد المطلوب
The eighth proof: Knowledge is a specific quiddity (māhiyyah makhṣūṣah), and power is also a specific quiddity. So either one of the two quiddities is identical to the other or different from it. If the former, then the word 'knowledge' and the word 'power' would be synonymous, each conveying what the other conveys. But it is known that this is false, because the definition (ḥadd) of knowledge is different from the definition of power, the rulings (aḥkām) of knowledge are different from the rulings of power, and the properties (khaṣāʾiṣ) of each are different from the properties of the other. Since this is the case, it is necessary that the intelligible (al-maʿqūl) from His being knowing is different from the intelligible from His being powerful, and that the intelligible from each of them is different from the intelligible from His being an existent essence. That yields what is sought.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is the eighth proof (al-ḥujjah al-thāminah) for the distinction between attributes and essence. The author argues from the diversity of quiddities (māhiyyāt). Knowledge and power each have their own specific quiddity. If they were identical, the words 'knowledge' and 'power' would be synonymous, which they are not: their definitions (ḥudūd), rulings (aḥkām), and properties (khaṣāʾiṣ) differ. Similarly, the intelligible content (al-maʿqūl) of being knowing, being powerful, and being existent are all distinct. Therefore, these attributes are distinct both from each other and from the essence. This is a clear statement of the proof from conceptual diversity (ikhtilāf al-mafhūm).

Key Terms
ماهية
حد
حكم
خاصة
معقول
مترادف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that differing words always imply distinct realities, or that attributes are merely aspects of a single undifferentiated essence.
Correction: The proof does not rely on word difference alone; it grounds distinction in objective differences in definitions, rulings, and properties. It also distinguishes attributes from the essence itself, as the intelligible of each attribute differs from the intelligible of existence.

Checkpoint
What does the eighth proof use to show that knowledge and power are distinct?
A) The impossibility of synonymous words.
B) Differences in their definitions, rulings, and properties.
C) The fact that God knows all things.
D) The consensus of rational people.

Key Takeaway
The eighth proof uses differences in definitions, rulings, and properties to show that divine attributes are distinct from each other and from the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's knowledge and power are distinct quiddities, or do you unconsciously treat them as the same? How might a confusion here affect your confidence that He both knows your need and is able to answer? Commit to distinguishing these attributes in your next du'a.
?
When you reflect on the fact that definitions, rulings, and properties of God's attributes differ, which attribute do you find yourself neglecting? For example, do you dwell on His power but forget His detailed knowledge? Make a concrete intention this week to meditate on one neglected attribute daily.
?
This proof shows that the intelligible content of God being knowing is different from His being powerful. How does that challenge your tendency to reduce God to mere generalities? Write down one way you will consciously invoke His specific attributes in your worship starting today.

The Three Conceptual Distinctions: Restatement of the Argument
Author restates the argument by enumerating three possibilities for the relationship between existence, knowledge, and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author restates the argument that divine attributes are distinct from the essence by eliminating two possibilities (synonymy and separate substances) and affirming the third (attributes as qualities of one essence).

Core Idea
Three distinct concepts cannot be separate substances; they must be attributes of one essence.

The author now restates the previous argument in clearer terms, focusing on three fundamental concepts: existence, knowledge, and power. He asks: are they the same thing, three independent entities, or something else? By eliminating the first two possibilities, he leads us to the conclusion that they are attributes of one essence. This restatement reinforces the logical necessity of the distinction between essence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the restatement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the concise style of the restatement.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage lists three options: one single concept, three self-subsistent atoms, or some described and others attributes. Note how the first two are rejected.
The third is the conclusion.
3
Understand the rejection arguments
The first is rejected by intuition (words are not synonymous), the second by consensus and reason. See how the third follows as the only remaining possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنعبر عن هذا الكلام بعبارة أخرى . فنقول : المعقول من كونه عالماً ، ومن كونه قادراً ، ومن كونه ذاتاً موجودة، إما أن يكون معقولاً واحداً ، أو لا يكون كذلك . والأول باطل بالبديهة، لأنا يبديهة العقل (۱) نعلم أن هذه الألفاظ ليست مترادفة بل هي ألفاظ متباينة ، يفيد كل واحد منهما غير(٢) ما يفيد الآخر . فهذه المعاني الثلاثة إما أن تكون ذرات ثلاثة قالمة بأنفسها . وذلك باطل بالاتفاق ، وبصريح العقل ، أو يكون بعضها موصوفاً ، والبواقي صفات . وهو المطلوب
Let us express this speech in another expression. We say: The intelligible (al-maʿqūl) from His being knowing, and from His being powerful, and from His being an existent essence—either it is a single intelligible or it is not. The first is false by intuition (bi-l-badīhah), for we know by the intuition of the intellect that these words are not synonymous, but are distinct words, each conveying other than what the other conveys. So these three meanings either are three self-subsistent atoms—that is false by consensus and by clear reason—or some of them are described (mawṣūf) and the rest are attributes (ṣifāt). That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a restatement (taʿbīr bi-ʿibārah ukhrā) of the previous proofs, summarizing the conclusion. The author considers three concepts: being knowing (ʿālim), being powerful (qādir), and being an existent essence (dhāt mawjūdah). They are not a single intelligible (maʿqūl wāḥid) because the words are not synonymous—they convey different meanings. These three meanings cannot be three independent self-subsistent atoms (dharrāt thalāth qāʾimah bi-anfusihā), as that would be false by consensus and reason. Therefore, one of them (the essence) is the subject (mawṣūf) and the other two are attributes (ṣifāt). This is the desired conclusion: attributes are distinct from the essence.

بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If three concepts are distinct, they must be separate independent substances.
Correction: The passage shows that distinct concepts can be attributes of one essence, not separate atoms.

Checkpoint
What is the third possibility rejected in the restatement?
A) That the three concepts are synonyms.
B) That they are three self-subsistent atoms.
C) That they are nothing at all.
D) That they are all accidents.

Key Takeaway
The three concepts of existence, knowledge, and power are neither synonymous nor separate entities; they are attributes of one divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah knows' and 'Allah is powerful', do you truly sense them as distinct perfections, or do you lump them into a vague feeling? How would your salah change if you consciously pondered each attribute separately?
?
Have you ever unconsciously slipped into thinking that Allah's attributes are just different names for the same thing, robbing them of their specific meanings? Will you from now on intentionally reflect on the unique meaning of each attribute when you recite His names?
?
If you truly internalize that knowledge and power are two distinct attributes of Allah, how does that affect how you turn to Him in dua? Will you now call upon Him with specific needs tied to specific attributes, trusting that each attribute is a distinct source of perfection?

Philosophers' First Objection: The Dilemma of Divine Attributes
Philosophers present a dilemma against distinct attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first argument against affirming distinct attributes in God, namely that attributes must be either necessary or contingent, both of which are rationally impossible.

Core Idea
Attributes cannot be necessary or contingent without contradicting tawḥīd or dependence.

The philosophers (falāsifa) denied that God has attributes distinct from His essence. Their first argument presents a logical dilemma: any attribute must be either necessary in itself or contingent in itself. Both options, they argue, are impossible. In this passage, we examine the first horn of the dilemma and its initial justification, setting the stage for the author's later refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: an objection presented with a dilemma and two reasons for the first horn.
The passage is incomplete; focus on what is presented.
2
Identify the dilemma
The philosophers claim attributes are either necessary or contingent. Why would these be the only options? Think about the logic: anything that exists must be either necessary or caused.
3
Examine the first reason against necessity
The first reason: the Necessary Being must be one. If attributes were necessary, there would be multiple necessary beings, contradicting tawḥīd.
4
Consider the second reason (incomplete)
The second reason is cut off: attributes are not self-subsistent but subsist in the essence. This leads to dependence, which is incompatible with necessity. Reflect on why dependence undermines necessity.
The full argument will be completed later.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
This is only the objection. The author will later refute it. For now, understand the philosophers' challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج نفاة الصفات بوجوه : الحجة الأولى للفلاسفة : قالوا : هذه الصفات إما أن تكون واجبة الوجود لذواتها ، أو جائزة الوجود لذواتها . والقسمان باطلان ، فالقول بثبوتها : باطل أما بيان أنه يمتنع كونها واجبة الوجود لذواتها فلوجهين : الأول : ما ثبت أن واجب الوجود لذاته (۳) يمتنع أن يكون أكثر من واحد . والثاني : أن الصفات غير ( ) قائمة بأنفسها بل هي (1) قائمة بالذات ، والقائم بغيره مفتقر
And the deniers of attributes (nafāt al-ṣifāt) argued with several arguments: The first argument of the philosophers: They said: These attributes are either necessary in existence by themselves (wājibat al-wujūd li-dhātihā) or contingent in existence by themselves (jāʾizat al-wujūd li-dhātihā). Both divisions are false, so the statement affirming them is false. As for the explanation that it is impossible for them to be necessary in existence by themselves, it is for two reasons: The first: what has been established that the Necessary in existence by Himself (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihi) cannot be more than one. The second: that the attributes are not self-subsistent (qāʾimah bi-anfusihā) but rather are subsistent in the essence (qāʾimah bi-l-dhāt), and that which subsists in another is dependent (muftaqir).
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage begins the presentation of the philosophers' objection (nafāt al-ṣifāt) against affirming attributes distinct from the essence. The philosophers argue that attributes must be either necessary in existence by themselves (wājibah li-dhātihā) or contingent (jāʾizah li-dhātihā). Both are impossible: if necessary, there would be multiple necessary beings, contradicting tawḥīd; if contingent, they would depend on something else, but attributes of God cannot be contingent. The argument is incomplete here, cut off at the second reason, but the reader understands the intended dilemma.

Key Terms
نفاة الصفات
واجب الوجود
جائز الوجود
قائم بذاته
قائم بغيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' argument is sound because it seems logically airtight.
Correction: The author will later show that the reasoning conflates different types of necessity and dependence. Attributes are not 'necessary in themselves' but are necessitated by the essence. The dilemma misses this distinction.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' first argument, why can attributes not be necessary in existence by themselves?
A) Because they are many, but the Necessary is one.
B) Because they are not self-subsistent.
C) Because they are eternal.
D) Because they are created.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' first objection attempts to disprove distinct divine attributes by arguing they must be either necessary (leading to multiple eternals) or contingent (implying dependence on another); both are impossible according to them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think of God’s attributes as 'things' added to His essence. After reading the philosophers’ argument, does this mental picture make you uncomfortable? How might you begin to think of them instead as the very reality of God, not separate add-ons?
?
The philosophers say that anything dependent cannot be necessary. In your own life, what do you treat as 'necessary' for your happiness or identity—your job, relationships, health—that is actually dependent on other things? How does this challenge you to rely solely on the truly Necessary Being?
?
If the philosophers are wrong and attributes are real yet one with God, what does that mean for how you see God’s knowledge of you? Can you still say 'God knows me' without making that knowledge a separate addition? How does that change your sense of being personally known?

The Eighth Proof: Distinction of Attributes from Essence
Sanusi presents the eighth proof that attributes are distinct from the essence through conceptual distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that divine attributes are conceptually distinct from the divine essence, based on the differences in their definitions and properties.

Core Idea
Attributes are distinct from the essence because their concepts are not synonymous.

Sanusi presents the eighth proof that the divine attributes are real and distinct from the essence. He uses a logical argument based on conceptual distinction: if knowledge and power were identical to the essence, their terms would be synonymous, but we know they are not. This lesson walks through the steps of this proof, showing how it establishes the reality of attributes without making them separate substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once to get a sense of the argument. Note the key terms: ماهية, ترادف, حد, معقول.
The passage is dense; read the translation if needed.
2
Follow the first conditional argument
Sanusi starts by comparing knowledge and power as specific quiddities. He asks whether they are identical or different. Follow the reasoning that if identical, the words would be synonymous, which is false.
3
Understand the restatement
Sanusi restates the argument more directly: the three conceptions (knowing, powerful, existent) are not one because the words are not synonymous. Then he concludes that they must be either three independent substances or some are attributes.
Remember: attributes are not separate substances.
4
Review the commentary
Read the commentary to solidify your understanding. Pay attention to how the proof avoids making attributes separate substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : إن العلم ماهية مخصوصة ، والقدرة أيضاً ماهية مخصوصة ، فإما أن تكون إحدى الماهيتين عين الأخرى أو غيرها ؟ فإن كان الأول كان لفظ العلم ولفظ القدرة لفظان مترادفان ، يفيد كل واحد منهما ما يفيده الآخر . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل ، لأن حد العلم غير حد القدرة ، وأحكام العلم غير أحكام القدرة ، وخواص كل واحد منها مغايرة لخواص الآخر . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يكون المعقول من كونه عالماً ، مغايراً للمعقول من كونه قادراً ، وأن يكون المعقول من كل واحد منهما ، مغايراً للمعقول من كونه موجوداً . وذلك يفيد المطلوب . ولنعبر عن هذا الكلام بعبارة أخرى . فنقول : المعقول من كونه عالماً ، ومن كونه قادراً ، ومن كونه ذاتاً موجودة، إما أن يكون معقولاً واحداً ، أو لا يكون كذلك . والأول باطل بالبديهة، لأنا ببديهة العقل نعلم أن هذه الألفاظ ليست مترادفة بل هي ألفاظ متباينة ، يفيد كل واحد منهما غير ما يفيد الآخر . فهذه المعاني الثلاثة إما أن تكون ذواتاً ثلاثة قائمة بأنفسها . وذلك باطل بالاتفاق ، وبصريح العقل ، أو يكون بعضها موصوفاً ، والبواقي صفات . وهو المطلوب
The eighth proof: Knowledge is a specific quiddity, and power is also a specific quiddity. So either one of the two quiddities is the very other or is other than it? If the first, then the word 'knowledge' and the word 'power' would be synonymous terms, each conveying what the other conveys. It is known that this is false, because the definition of knowledge is other than the definition of power, the rulings of knowledge are other than the rulings of power, and the properties of each are different from the properties of the other. When this is the case, it necessarily follows that the conception of Him being knowing is different from the conception of Him being powerful, and that the conception of each of them is different from the conception of Him being existent. That yields the desired conclusion. Let us express this statement in another expression. We say: The conception of Him being knowing, and of Him being powerful, and of Him being an existent essence, either is one conception or is not so. The first is false by intuition, for we know by the intuition of the intellect that these words are not synonymous but are distinct words, each conveying other than what the other conveys. So these three meanings are either three essences subsisting by themselves—and that is false by consensus and by clear reason—or some of them are described and the rest are attributes. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof for the reality of divine attributes distinct from the essence. It is a proof by conceptual distinction. The author begins by comparing knowledge and power as two specific quiddities. If they were identical, the terms would be synonymous, but they are not—their definitions, rulings, and properties differ. Therefore, the concept of 'knowing' is different from 'powerful', and both differ from 'existent'. This establishes that attributes are not identical to the essence. The argument is restated more directly: the three conceptions (knowing, powerful, existent) are either one or multiple. They are not one because we intuitively know the words are not synonymous. The three meanings must then be either three independent substances (impossible by consensus and reason) or some are described (the essence) and the rest are attributes. Thus, attributes are real and distinct.

Key Terms
ماهية
ترادف
حد
معقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof claims that attributes are separate, independent substances alongside the essence.
Correction: The proof explicitly rejects that the three meanings are independent substances (ذوات قائمة بأنفسها) and concludes that some are described (the essence) and the rest are attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth proof, what would follow if knowledge and power were identical?
A) They would be separate substances
B) Their terms would be synonymous
C) Their definitions would differ
D) They would not be attributes

Key Takeaway
The eighth proof demonstrates that divine attributes are not identical to the essence because their concepts are distinct, though they are not separate substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-ʿAlīm (the All-Knowing) and then as al-Qadīr (the All-Powerful), do you truly feel the distinctness of these attributes, or do you mentally collapse them into one vague notion? How might consciously distinguishing His knowledge from His power change the way you ask Him for guidance versus for strength?
?
If Allah's attributes are real and distinct from His essence, then His mercy is not merely His will, nor His justice merely His power. How does the reality that each attribute has its own meaning and implications affect your trust in His forgiveness? Are you tempted to think that His mercy somehow overrides His justice, or do you see both as equally real and perfectly harmonized? Examine your heart.
?
The proof shows that the attributes are not separate beings, yet they are not identical to the essence either. Does your worship reflect this balance—neither falling into neglect of His attributes (as if they are irrelevant) nor elevating them to the level of independent deities? In your daily life, which extreme do you lean towards, and what concrete step can you take today to correct it?

First Philosophers' Argument Against Attributes: The Dilemma
Philosophers argue that attributes cannot be necessary or possible, so they are not real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' dilemma against divine attributes and why they conclude attributes cannot be real.

Core Idea
Attributes cannot be necessary or possible; therefore they are impossible.

The philosophers present a powerful objection: if attributes exist, they must be either necessary or possible. But both options lead to contradictions. This lesson unpacks their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the dilemma structure
Read the opening lines of the passage to see how the philosophers frame the argument as an exhaustive division: attributes are either necessary or possible. Then they claim both are false.
This is a classic kalam dilemma (taqsīm).
2
Examine why attributes cannot be necessary
Focus on the two reasons given: (1) only one necessary existent is possible, so multiple attributes cannot be necessary; (2) attributes depend on the essence, so they are possible, not necessary.
The second reason is more subtle: dependence implies possibility.
3
Examine why attributes cannot be possible
Look at the two absurdities when the essence is both agent and recipient, and the implication of temporal origination. Understand how the philosophers conclude that attributes cannot be caused by the essence.
They argue that if attributes were caused, they'd be originated (ḥādith), which is impossible for eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى للفلاسفة : قالوا : هذه الصفات إما أن تكون واجبة الوجود لذواتها ، أو جائزة الوجود لذواتها . والقسمان باطلان ، فالقول بثبوتها : باطل . أما بيان أنه يمتنع كونها واجبة الوجود لذواتها فلوجهين : الأول : ما ثبت أن واجب الوجود لذاته يمتنع أن يكون أكثر من واحد . والثاني : أن الصفات غير قائمة بأنفسها بل هي قائمة بالذات ، والقائم بغيره مفتقر إلى الغير، فيكون ممكناً لذاته ، لا واجباً لذاته . وأما بيان أنه يمتنع كونها ممكنة لذواتها ، فهو أن كل ممكن لذاته ، لا بد له من سبب . وذلك السبب ليس إلا تلك الذات، وذلك محال من وجهين : الأول : إنه إذا كان المؤثر هو تلك الذات والقابل أيضاً تلك الذات فيلزم كون الشيء الواحد قابلاً وفاعلاً ، وهو محال . والثاني : هو أن الاحتياج لا يحصل إلا عند زمان الحدوث، وإلا لزم تكوين الكائن ، وهو محال ، فلو كانت هذه الصفات معللة بالغير، لكانت حادثة ، لكن كونها حادثة محال ، فيمتنع كونها معللة بالذات
The first argument of the philosophers: They said: These attributes are either necessary of existence by themselves or possible of existence by themselves. Both divisions are false, so the claim of their affirmation is false. As for the explanation that they cannot be necessary of existence by themselves, it is by two aspects: The first: It is established that the Necessary of Existence by Himself cannot be more than one. The second: Attributes do not subsist by themselves but subsist by the essence, and that which subsists by another is dependent on the other, so it is possible by itself, not necessary by itself. As for the explanation that they cannot be possible by themselves, it is that every possible by itself must have a cause. That cause is nothing but that essence, and that is impossible from two aspects: The first: If the agent is that essence and the recipient is also that essence, then it follows that the one thing is recipient and agent, which is impossible. The second: Need does not occur except at the time of origination, otherwise the coming-to-be of the existent would follow, which is impossible. So if these attributes were caused by another, they would be originated, but their being originated is impossible, so it is impossible that they are caused by the essence.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the first philosophical argument against the reality of attributes. It is a dilemma: attributes must be either necessary or possible, but both are impossible. First, they cannot be necessary because (1) there cannot be more than one necessary existent, and (2) attributes are not self-subsisting but depend on the essence, so they are possible, not necessary. Second, they cannot be possible because any possible requires a cause, and the only possible cause is the essence itself. But this leads to two absurdities: (a) the essence would be both agent and recipient of the same attribute, which is impossible, and (b) need for a cause implies temporal origination, but attributes must be eternal, so they cannot be caused. Therefore, attributes cannot exist at all.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن الوجود
علة
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument disproves all attributes entirely, including those understood as aspects of God's essence.
Correction: The argument only targets attributes as separate real entities. Many theologians hold that attributes are not distinct from the essence but are relational or conceptual.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' first argument, why can't attributes be necessary of existence?
A) They are dependent on the essence, making them possible
B) They are self-subsistent
C) They are originated
D) They are identical to the essence

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' dilemma attempts to show that attributes cannot exist as real entities because neither necessity nor possibility is coherent for them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you ever mentally treat Allah's attributes as if they came into being or are 'acquired'? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you consciously affirm about His attributes today?
?
Consider the dilemma: attributes are either necessary-in-themselves or possible-in-themselves. The philosophers say both are impossible. Have you ever thought of Allah's attributes as separate 'beings' alongside Him? How does this argument expose the idolatry hidden in such a conception? What concrete step will you take to ensure your worship honours Allah as one essence with perfect attributes?
?
The philosophers conclude that attributes cannot be real. But the Ash'aris maintain they are real yet not separate. How does your heart react when faced with a logical challenge to your beliefs? Are you willing to trust the scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah even when your mind struggles? What will you do this week to deepen your reliance on revelation over mere reason?

God Must Be Absolutely Simple
Philosophers argue that God must be simple, so attributes (a composite) are denied.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God cannot have attributes distinct from His essence because composition would imply dependence and contingency.

Core Idea
If God were composite, He would depend on His parts—but God depends on nothing.

This passage presents the second philosophical argument against the reality of divine attributes. The philosophers argue that God must be either simple or composite. They then show that composition is impossible for God because every composite depends on its parts, making it contingent and in need of a cause. Since God is the Necessary Being, He cannot be composite. Finally, they note that the combination of an attribute and its subject is a composite, so God cannot have attributes distinct from His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once silently, then read the English translation. Notice how the argument proceeds step by step.
The argument uses a dichotomy: either simple or composite.
2
Identify the logical structure
Look for the key steps: (1) the dichotomy, (2) the rejection of composition, (3) the conclusion of simplicity, (4) the application to attributes.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'simple' (فرد) and 'composite' (مركب). Also note how 'attribute and described' (صفة وموصوف) is considered a composite.
These terms are central to the argument.
4
Reflect on the implication for attributes
The argument concludes that God is absolutely simple (فرد مطلق), meaning no real distinction between essence and attributes. Consider how this differs from the Ash'ari view.
This is a direct challenge to the classical Islamic doctrine of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية للفلاسفة : قالوا : إله العالم إما أن يكون فرداً أو مركباً ، ويمتنع كونه مركباً ، لأن كل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، وجزء الشيء غيره ، فكل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى غيره ، وكل مفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، فلو كان إله العالم مركباً لكان ممكناً لذاته ، وكل ممكن لذاته ، فلا بد له من سبب يوجده . فيثبت أن إله العالم لو كان مركباً لكان ممكناً ، ولو كان ممكناً افتقر إلى الفاعل ، وذلك محال في حق إله كل الممكنات ينتج أن إله كل الممكنات لا يكون مركباً، وكل ما لا يكون مركباً ، كان فرداً مطلقاً والمجموع الحاصل من الصفة والموصوف يكون مركباً ، فيثبت أن إله العالم فرد مطلق ، وليس فيه تركيب من الصفة والموصوف . وهو المطلوب
The second argument of the philosophers: They said: The God of the world is either simple or composite, and it is impossible for Him to be composite, because every composite is dependent on each one of its parts, and the part of a thing is other than it, so every composite is dependent on something other than itself, and everything dependent on another is possible by itself. So if the God of the world were composite, He would be possible by Himself, and everything possible by itself must have a cause that brings it into existence. Thus it is established that if the God of the world were composite, He would be possible, and if He were possible, He would be dependent on an agent, and that is impossible regarding the God of all possibles. This results in the conclusion that the God of all possibles is not composite, and whatever is not composite is absolutely simple. And the aggregate resulting from attribute and described is composite, so it is established that the God of the world is absolutely simple, and there is no composition in Him from attribute and described. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents the second philosophical argument against attributes, based on simplicity. It is a classification and proof. The argument states that God must be either simple or composite. Composite is impossible because every composite depends on its parts, which are other than itself, making it possible and thus in need of a cause. Since God is necessary, He cannot be composite. Then it notes that any combination of attribute and described (subject) is a composite. Therefore, God cannot have attributes distinct from His essence. Hence, He is absolutely simple, with no composition of attribute and described.

Key Terms
فرد
مركب
تركيب
صفة وموصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes are separate entities added to God's essence, like extra 'things'.
Correction: The philosophers argue that if attributes were distinct, God would be composite and thus contingent. They deny any real distinction; attributes are identical with the essence in their view.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' second argument, why is God not composite?
A) Because composite things are eternal
B) Because composites depend on parts, making them possible
C) Because God has no parts
D) Because attributes are the same as the essence

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that God cannot be composite because composition entails dependence and contingency, so God must be absolutely simple, without any real distinction between essence and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is absolutely simple, then my mental image of Him having separate 'parts' like mercy and justice is fundamentally flawed. In my daily life, do I still act as if these are separate? How can I train myself to see all of God's actions as flowing from His single, undivided essence?
?
The philosophers argue that any composition implies dependence. Have I ever subtly believed that God depends on His own attributes—that His mercy must 'activate' for me to be forgiven? If so, how does this argument show that such thinking diminishes tawheed? What concrete step can I take today to worship God as one indivisible being?

The Philosophers' Argument for Absolute Divine Simplicity
Philosophers' disjunctive syllogism for divine simplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second argument for divine simplicity, which uses a disjunctive syllogism to prove that God cannot be composite and that any composition of essence and attributes is impossible.

Core Idea
Composition implies dependence and possibility, which contradicts God's necessity.

The philosophers present a rigorous logical argument to prove that God must be absolutely simple, devoid of any composition. They begin with a disjunction: God is either simple or composite, then demonstrate that composition is impossible. This lesson walks through the steps of their reasoning, showing how each premise leads to the conclusion that God is not composed of parts, including the critical rejection of any composition of essence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: it's an argument, not just a statement.
2
Identify key terms
Identify the key terms: فرد (simple/one), مركب (composite), مفتقر (dependent), ممكن لذاته (possible in itself), فاعل (agent/cause).
3
Trace the argument step by step
Follow the logical chain: disjunction → proof that composite impossible → consequence that God is one → final point about attribute and subject.
Write down each step in your own words.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Reflect on the conclusion that God has no composition of essence and attributes. Consider how this differs from other theological views.
This is the key point the philosophers aim to prove.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية للفلاسفة : قالوا : إله العالم إما أن يكون فرداً أو مركباً ، ويمتنع كونه مركباً ، لأن كل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه ، وجزء الشيء غيره ، فكل مركب فهو مفتقر إلى غيره ، وكل مفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، فلو كان إله العالم مركباً لكان ممكناً لذاته ، وكل ممكن لذاته ، فلا بد له من سبب يوجده . فيثبت أن إله العالم لو كان مركباً لكان ممكناً ، ولو كان ممكناً افتقر إلى الفاعل ، وذلك محال في حق إله كل الممكنات ينتج أن إله كل الممكنات لا يكون مركباً، وكل ما لا يكون مركباً ، كان فرداً مطلقاً والمجموع الحاصل من الصفة والموصوف يكون مركباً ، فيثبت أن إله العالم فرد مطلق ، وليس فيه تركيب من الصفة والموصوف . وهو المطلوب
The second argument of the philosophers: They said: The God of the world is either one or composite, and it is impossible for Him to be composite, because every composite is dependent upon each of its parts, and a part of a thing is other than it; so every composite is dependent upon another, and everything dependent upon another is possible in itself. So if the God of the world were composite, He would be possible in itself, and every possible in itself necessarily has a cause that brings it into existence. Thus it is established that if the God of the world were composite, He would be possible, and if He were possible, He would depend on an agent, and that is impossible with respect to the God of all possibles. The conclusion is that the God of all possibles is not composite, and everything that is not composite is absolutely one. And the composite resulting from an attribute and its subject is composite; so it is established that the God of the world is absolutely one, and there is no composition in Him of attribute and subject. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' second argument for divine simplicity. The argument is a disjunctive syllogism: God is either simple (fard) or composite (murakkab). Composition is impossible because every composite depends on its parts, which are other than itself; so the composite is dependent on another, and thus possible in itself. If God were composite, He would be possible and in need of a cause, which contradicts His being the Creator of all possibles. Therefore, God is not composite; He is absolutely simple. The argument also targets the Ash'ari view that God is composed of essence and attributes: the combination of attribute and subject is a composition, so that is also rejected.

Key Terms

مفتقر
ممكن لذاته
فاعل
تركيب
صفة
موصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that God has any attributes.
Correction: The argument only denies a real distinction between essence and attributes, affirming that God is simple without composition of attribute and subject.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers claim that God cannot be composite?
A) Because composite things are temporal.
B) Because a composite depends on its parts, making it possible and in need of a cause.
C) Because composition implies physicality.
D) Because attributes cannot be separated.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that God must be absolutely simple, without any composition of parts or of essence and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers prove that composition implies need. In your life, do you treat God as if He needs your worship or your obedience? Or do you truly believe He is absolutely self-sufficient and simple, without any lack? How should this change how you pray today?
?
If God's attributes are not separate from His essence, then His mercy is not a mere act but His very being. Can you trust a mercy that is not just something He gives but something He is? What would it mean for you to seek refuge in His essence rather than in His actions alone?
?
This argument strips away every mental division you might make about God. Is there any part of your concept of God that you secretly think of as 'part' of Him—like a characteristic you can isolate? How can you train your heart to see God as absolutely one, without any mental partitions?

First Mu'tazilite Argument: God's Knowledge is Necessary
Mu'tazila argue that God's knowledge is necessary and uncaused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first Mu'tazilite argument that God's knowledge is necessary and therefore uncaused.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge were contingent, He would depend on a cause.

The Mu'tazila begin their case against the reality of divine attributes by arguing that God's knowledge must be necessary, not contingent. They reason that a contingent attribute would need an external agent, implying dependence on that agent—an absurdity for God. This passage presents the logical core of their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the two main claims
Find the two premises: (1) God's knowledge is necessary, (2) the necessary cannot have a cause.
3
Understand the reasoning for each claim
For the first: contingency would require an agent, making God dependent. For the second: a cause is needed only to tip the balance from non-existence, but a necessary thing exists necessarily.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Consider that this argument denies that knowledge is a distinct attribute added to God's essence, not that God knows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : قالوا : عالمية الله تعالى واجبة ، والواجب لا يعلل . أما ان عالمية الله تعالى واجبة ، فلاتها لو كانت جائزة لافتقر ذلك العالم إلى فاعل يجعله عالماً ، وحينئذ يصير إله العالم عبداً ، وهو محال . وأما أن الواجب لا يعلل. فلأن الافتقار إلى العلة، لأجل أن يترجح بسببها جانب الوجود على جانب العدم . وإذا كان ذلك الرجحان حاصلاً على سبيل الوجوب ، امتنع افتقاره إلى العلة
The first argument: They said: The omniscience of God most high is necessary, and the necessary is not caused. As for the fact that God's omniscience is necessary, because if it were contingent, that knowledge would depend on an agent to make it knowing, and then the God of the world would become a servant, which is absurd. And as for the fact that the necessary is not caused, because dependence on a cause is for the sake of tipping the balance of existence over non-existence through it. And when that preponderance occurs in a necessary manner, its dependence on a cause is impossible.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is the first of the Mu'tazilite general arguments against the reality of divine attributes. They argue that God's attribute of knowledge (al-'ilmiyya) is necessary (wajiba), i.e., it cannot be contingent (ja'iza). If it were contingent, it would need an external agent to cause it, making God dependent on that agent, which is absurd (God would become a 'servant' of that agent). Then they argue that whatever is necessary cannot have a cause, because a cause is needed to tip the balance from non-existence to existence; if existence is necessary, no tipping is needed. Thus, God's knowledge is necessary and uncaused.

Key Terms
عالمية
واجبة
جائزة
فاعل
واجب
علة
ترجيح
وجود
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite argument denies that God has knowledge.
Correction: The argument does not deny God's knowledge; it denies that knowledge is an attribute distinct from God's essence, arguing that God's knowledge is identical with His essence and thus necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the first Mu'tazilite argument, why must God's knowledge be necessary?
A) Because if it were contingent, God would need a teacher.
B) Because if it were contingent, it would need a cause, making God dependent.
C) Because all knowledge is necessary.
D) Because God's essence is knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila claim that God's knowledge is necessary and uncaused, forming the basis for their denial of distinct divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge were contingent, He would depend on something else. Do you live as if God is independent and you are dependent on Him, or do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
The argument shows that necessity excludes dependency. In your own life, are there things you treat as 'necessary' that are actually contingent and dependent? What would it mean to truly rely only on the Necessary Being?
?
This argument guards against making God need anything. Is there any part of your worship or belief that subtly assumes God needs your actions? How can you purify your intention to serve God without trying to 'complete' Him?

Second Mu'tazilite Argument: Eternal Entities Lead to Absurdity
The Mu'tazila present a dilemma against multiple eternal entities showing both outcomes absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma the Mu'tazila present against the existence of multiple eternal entities, and why both possible outcomes are considered absurd.

Core Idea
Multiple eternal entities necessarily collapse into either infinite regress or polytheism.

The Mu'tazila used rational arguments to challenge the Ash'ari view that divine attributes are eternal and distinct from God's essence. This second general argument presents a dilemma: if there are multiple eternal entities, then either they differ in some constitutive part or they do not. Both alternatives, they argue, lead to absurdities. In this lesson, we follow the logical steps of this argument and examine its implications.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the dilemma
The argument sets up two exhaustive alternatives for multiple eternal entities: either they differ in constitutive parts or they are identical in essence. Understand why these are the only possibilities.
This is a classic either/or logical structure.
2
Explore the first alternative
If the entities differ, then each is composed of two aspects: the shared element (eternity) and the distinguishing element. Since both parts are eternal, they themselves are composite, leading to infinite regress. Trace this reasoning step by step.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Explore the second alternative
If the entities do not differ in any constitutive part, they are identical in essence. This collapses the distinction between subject and attribute and implies multiple gods (if multiple eternal entities are considered). Understand why this is also absurd according to the Mu'tazila.
This outcome is considered polytheism (shirk).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both alternatives are impossible, so the original premise—that there are multiple eternal entities—must be false. Consider how this argument targets the Ash'ari view of eternal attributes.
The argument does not deny attributes per se, but only their being distinct eternal entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : القول بالقدماء يفضي إلى المحال فيكون محالاً . والدليل عليه : أنا قد دللنا على أن القدم مفهوم ثبوتي ، فالقدماء تكون متشاركة في هذا المفهوم ، وإذا كانت متشاركة فإما أن يخالف بعضها بعضاً بشيء من الأجزاء المقومة ، أو لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان الأول فما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، لأن كل واحد من أولئك القدماء مركب من هذين القيدين ، وهما قديمان ، ضرورة أن جزء ماهية القديم قديم ، فذانك الجزءان يتشاركان في القدم، ويتخالفان في الموجب الآخر ، فيكون كل واحد من ذينك الجزئين أيضاً مركباً ، فيلزم كون كل واحد منها مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني وهو أن أولئك القدماء لا يخالف بعضها بعضاً في جزء مقوم للماهية فهي أشياء متماثلة في تمام الماهية ، فيلزم كون الذات صفة ، والصفة ذاتاً ، ويلزم القول بتعدد الآلهة وهو محال
The second argument: The claim of eternal entities leads to the absurd, so it is absurd. The proof of that: We have shown that eternity is a positive concept; so eternal entities share in this concept. If they share, then either some of them differ from others in some of the constitutive parts, or they do not. If the first, then that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, because each of those eternal beings is composed of these two conditions, and they are both eternal, necessarily because a part of the essence of the eternal is eternal. So those two parts share in eternity and differ in the other positive [element]; then each of those two parts is also composite, so each of them is composed of infinite parts, which is absurd. If the second, that is, that those eternal beings do not differ from each other in a part constitutive of the essence, then they are things identical in the entirety of essence, so it follows that the subject is attribute and the attribute is subject, and the claim of multiple gods follows, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite general argument. They argue that positing multiple eternal entities (like God and His attributes) leads to two alternatives, both absurd. First, if the eternal entities differ in some constitutive parts, then they share the concept of eternity and differ in something else, making each composed of two aspects: the shared (eternity) and the distinguishing. This composition must itself be eternal, leading to an infinite regress of composition. Second, if they do not differ in any constitutive part, they are identical in essence, which would collapse the distinction between subject and attribute and imply multiple gods (if we consider multiple eternal entities). Thus, the original claim is false.

Key Terms
قدماء
مفهوم ثبوتي
مشاركة
مخالفة
أجزاء مقومة
ماهية
متماثلة
تعدد الآلهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is a denial of divine attributes.
Correction: The argument is specifically a critique of real, distinct eternal attributes existing as separate entities from God's essence. It does not deny that God has attributes, but rather that those attributes are eternal entities distinct from God.

Checkpoint
What is the second alternative in the second Mu'tazilite argument?
A) The eternal entities differ in constitutive parts.
B) The eternal entities are identical in essence, leading to subject-attribute collapse.
C) The eternal entities are not eternal.
D) The eternal entities are created.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite dilemma shows that multiple eternal entities are rationally impossible, as they lead either to infinite regress or polytheism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you profess the oneness of God (tawheed), does this argument expose any hidden assumptions you might have about the nature of God's attributes? Are you willing to examine whether your own understanding of God's attributes inadvertently implies multiple eternal entities? Take a moment to reflect: In your worship, do you ever feel you are addressing God's attribute (like His mercy) as if it were a separate being? How can you ensure your heart remains focused on God alone?
?
This argument shows that logical absurdity results from positing more than one eternal entity. Have you ever felt a tension between affirming God's perfect attributes (such as His knowledge being eternal) and affirming His absolute oneness? If you find that tension in your heart, do you run from it or confront it? What step can you take today to deepen your conviction in the unity of God?

Third Mu'tazilite argument: attributes entail multiple eternals
The Mu'tazila argue that real attributes would produce multiple eternal beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that real attributes would lead to multiple eternal beings and why they consider this impossible.

Core Idea
Real attributes would multiply eternals; that is rejected by consensus.

This lesson examines the third Mu'tazilite argument against real divine attributes. The argument is brief but powerful: if attributes truly subsist in the essence, they would become additional eternal entities, which is universally rejected. Let's unpack this claim step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If attribute subsists in essence, then multiple eternals arise.'
The argument is presented as a simple conditional with a conclusion.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'صفة' (attribute), 'ذات' (essence), 'قدماء متغايرة' (distinct eternal beings), and 'باطل بالاتفاق' (false by consensus). Understand what each means in this context.
3
Understand the logical flow
The argument follows a logical chain: if attributes are real distinct entities subsisting in the essence, then you have the essence plus each attribute — thus multiple eternal beings. But multiple eternal beings are impossible (by consensus), so attributes cannot be real distinct entities.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the consensus claim
The phrase 'باطل بالاتفاق' indicates that both sides agree that multiple eternal beings are false. Consider why this is a strong rhetorical move: the Mu'tazila are using a premise their opponents accept.
5
Engage with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to test your understanding of the argument's conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنه لو قامت الصفة بالذات حصل في الوجود قدماء متغايرة ، وذلك باطل بالاتفاق
The third argument: If the attribute subsisted in the essence, there would come into existence distinct eternal beings, and that is false by consensus.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This is a short, terse argument. The Mu'tazila claim that if attributes (like knowledge, power) truly subsist in the divine essence as distinct realities, then you would have multiple eternal entities (the essence plus the attributes). This is false by agreement (bil-ittifaq), meaning that even opponents admit that multiple eternal entities are impossible.

Key Terms
صفة
ذات
قدماء متغايرة
باطل بالاتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mu'tazilite argument proves that attributes are impossible in any sense.
Correction: The argument only shows that if attributes were real, distinct entities subsisting in the essence, then multiple eternals would result. It does not rule out attributes being identical with the essence or being mere relations.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the third Mu'tazilite argument?
A) Attributes are real.
B) Attributes would cause multiple eternal beings, which is rejected.
C) Attributes are not eternal.
D) The essence is the only eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that real attributes would multiply eternal beings, an impossibility by consensus, so attributes cannot be real distinct entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining that God's power, mercy, or wrath are separate 'things' added to His essence? How does this lesson's warning against multiple eternal entities reshape your mental picture of God?
?
The Mu'tazila relied on consensus as a decisive proof. When you affirm tawhid, is it because you have personally understood the rational arguments, or are you merely repeating what you've been taught? What would it take for your conviction to be as firm as their consensus?
?
If the slightest notion of multiple eternal beings is rejected by consensus, then how seriously do you take the sins of shirk in your own heart—like showing off in worship or fearing a creation more than the Creator? Are you willing to uproot those subtle associations today?
