Fourth Mu'tazilite Argument: Inherence Implies Spatial Location
Mu'tazilite argument that inherence entails spatial location

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that attribute inherence logically entails spatial location, and why they consider this impossible for God.

Core Idea
Inherence means an attribute shares its subject's location—impossible for the spaceless God.

The Mu'tazila, in their rejection of real divine attributes, advanced several rational arguments. This fourth argument targets the very concept of an attribute inhering (حلول) in a subject. They claim that the only intelligible notion of inherence involves spatial location; since God cannot occupy space, attributes cannot inhere in Him. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice the structure: definition of inherence, conditional reasoning, and conclusion.
The passage is dense; reading it twice may help.
2
Identify the core claim
The Mu'tazila define inherence as the attribute occurring in the same spatial location as its subject. This is the only intelligible meaning for them.
They assume this definition is self-evident.
3
Understand the conditional reasoning
If we remove the spatial meaning, the relationship becomes reversible—why should the attribute subsist in the subject rather than the reverse? This undermines any real distinction.
This step shows the logical necessity of spatial implication.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Since God is beyond space and direction, He cannot be a subject for attributes that inhere spatially. Therefore, attributes cannot inhere in God.
The conclusion is that God has no real attributes; they are either identical to His essence or purely negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن المعقول من حلول الصفة في الذات حصول تلك الصفة في الحيز المعين، تبعاً لحصول ذلك المحل فيه، وإنا لو رفعنا هذا المعنى من العقل، فحينئذ لا يكون قيام أحدهما بالآخر أولى من قيام الآخر بالأول، فيثبت أن قيام الصفة بالموصوف، يقتضي كون الموصوف حاصلاً في الحيز مختصاً بالجهة، وذلك في حق الله تعالى محال
The fourth argument: The intelligible meaning of the inherence of an attribute in a subject is that the attribute occurs in a specific space, following the occurrence of the subject in it. And if we remove this meaning from the intellect, then one's subsisting in the other would not be more appropriate than the other's subsisting in the first; so it is established that the subsistence of the attribute in the subject necessitates that the subject occurs in a space and is particular to a direction, and that is impossible with respect to God most high.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Mu'tazilite argument. They argue that the only coherent concept of an attribute inhering in a subject is that the attribute occupies the same location as the subject. If we discard that notion, there is no reason for one to be the attribute and the other the subject (i.e., you could reverse them). Therefore, inherence implies spatial localization and direction, which is impossible for God. Thus, attributes cannot inhere in God.

Key Terms
حلول
حيز
محل
قيام
موصوف
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila reject attributes because they deny God's perfection.
Correction: They reject attribute inherence specifically because it implies spatial location, which is impossible for God—not because they deny divine perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth Mu'tazilite argument, why is attribute inherence impossible for God?
A) Because attributes are not real.
B) Because inherence implies spatial location and direction.
C) Because God has no attributes.
D) Because attributes are not eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument that inherence entails spatial location, which is impossible for God, is used to deny real divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously imagined God as being 'somewhere'—above the sky, in a specific direction—even though you know He is beyond space? How does this Mu'tazilite argument expose that subtle anthropomorphism in your own thinking, and what will you do to actively purify your conception of God from spatial imagery?
?
The Mu'tazilites rejected real attributes because they assumed inherence must be spatial. Are there any assumptions you hold about God that, if wrong, would lead you to deny something true about Him? What one assumption about God will you examine today in light of revelation, not human reasoning alone?

The Second Argument: Regress from Shared Eternity
Presents the second argument against attributes leading to infinite regress or pantheism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second argument refutes the existence of multiple eternal entities by showing it leads to either infinite regress or pantheism.

Core Idea
Shared eternity forces either infinite composition or pantheism—both absurd.

In this second argument against the distinctness of attributes, the author shows that if multiple eternal beings exist, they must share the concept of eternity. This sharing leads to an inevitable dilemma: either they differ in parts, causing infinite regress, or they are identical, implying multiple gods. Both outcomes are impossible, thus the initial assumption is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its statement
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's claim that the statement of eternal beings leads to absurdity.
The argument uses the concept of eternity as a positive attribute.
2
Examine the first branch (differing parts)
Consider the first horn: if the eternal entities differ in constitutive parts, then each is composed of a shared part (eternity) and a differing part. Both parts are eternal, so each part is further composite, leading to infinite regress.
This is an absurd consequence because infinite composition is impossible.
3
Examine the second branch (identical essences)
Now consider the second horn: if they do not differ in constitutive parts, then they are identical in essence. This implies that essence and attribute become indistinguishable, resulting in polytheism, which is also absurd.
Thus both horns are impossible, so the assumption of multiple eternal entities is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : القول بالقدماء يفضي إلى المحال فيكون محالاً . والدليل عليه : أنا قد دللنا على أن القدم مفهوم ثبوتي ، فالقدماء تكون متشاركة في هذا المفهوم ، ( وإذا كانت متشاركة ) (۱) فإما أن يخالف بعضها بعضاً بشيء من الأجزاء المقومة ، أو لا يكون كذلك. فإن كان الأول فما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة ، لأن كل واحد من أولئك القدماء مركب من هذين القيدين ، وهما قديمان ، ضرورة أن جزء ماهية القديم قديم ، فذانك الجزء ان يتشاركان في القدم، ويتخالفان في موجبه الآخر ، فيكون كل واحد من ذينك الجزئين أيضاً مركباً ، فيلزم كون كل واحد منها مركباً من أجزاء غير متناهية ( وهو محال . وإن كان الثاني وهو أن أولئك القدماء لا يخالف بعضها بعضاً في جزء مقوم للماهية ) (۲) فهي أشياء متماثلة في تمام الماهية ، فيلزم كون الذات صفة ، والصفة ذاتاً ، ويلزم القول بتعدد الآلهة وهو محال )(٣)
The Second Argument: The statement of the eternal entities leads to absurdity, so it is absurd. Its proof: We have shown that eternity is a positive concept, so the eternal entities share in this concept. (If they share) (1), then either they differ from each other by some of the constitutive parts, or they do not. If the first, then that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, because each one of those eternal entities is composed of these two qualifications, and they are both eternal, necessarily because a part of the essence of the eternal is eternal. So those two parts share in eternity and differ in their other determinant, so each one of those two parts is also composite, so it follows that each one of them is composed of infinite parts, which is absurd. And if the second, that those eternal entities do not differ from each other in a part constitutive of the essence, (2) then they are things identical in the entirety of the essence, so it follows that the essence is an attribute and the attribute is an essence, and it follows that one asserts multiple gods, which is absurd. (3)
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the second argument) against the existence of multiple eternal entities, which would be the case if attributes are distinct from the divine essence. The author argues that if multiple eternal entities exist, they share the concept of eternity. They must either differ in some constitutive parts or be identical. If they differ, each eternal is composed of a shared part (eternity) and a differentiating part. Since both parts are eternal, each part is also composite, leading to an infinite regress of parts. If they are identical in all constitutive parts, then they are perfectly alike, which would mean the essence and attribute are indistinguishable, implying multiple gods, which is absurd. Thus, the assumption of distinct attributes is refuted.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
مفهوم ثبوتي
أجزاء مقومة
تسلسل (tasalsul)
تماثل (tamathul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple eternal entities can coexist without logical contradiction as long as they are distinct.
Correction: This argument shows that sharing the attribute of eternity forces either infinite composition or identity, both of which are logically impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what follows if eternal entities differ in constitutive parts?
A) They are identical in essence.
B) Each is composed of infinite parts.
C) They are both temporal.
D) They are unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The second argument demonstrates that assuming multiple eternal entities necessarily leads to absurdity, thereby refuting the claim that attributes are distinct eternal entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If this argument is sound, then any belief in multiple eternal beings (e.g., the Trinity) is logically impossible. Have you ever truly considered the logical implications of your own beliefs about God's eternity? Can you, in your heart, accept that nothing—absolutely nothing—shares in God's eternity? If there is any lingering thought of something else being eternal, what is it and why do you hold onto it?
?
The argument forces you to see that the only way to avoid absurdity is to affirm that God's essence and attributes are not distinct entities. Does your understanding of God's oneness go beyond mere words? Do you actually live as if God alone is worthy of worship because He alone is eternal? Or do you sometimes act as if other things (status, wealth, relationships) are eternal in their importance?
?
If you were to debate a person who claims that God's attributes are separate eternal beings, could you present this argument clearly? More importantly, does your conviction in tawheed rely on blind faith or on logical proofs? This lesson gives you a tool—are you willing to master it so that you can defend the unity of God with certainty?

The Third Argument: Attributes and Multiple Eternals
States the third argument against attribute subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical problem with attributes subsisting in the divine essence and why it leads to multiple eternal entities.

Core Idea
If an attribute is distinct and eternal, then God is not one but many.

Al-Matālib al-ʿĀliyah presents a third argument against the subsistence of attributes in the divine essence. The argument is simple but devastating: if an attribute were to subsist in the essence, both would be eternal and distinct, creating a plurality of eternal beings. This contradicts the consensus of Muslims that there is only one eternal being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the concise structure: a conditional premise followed by a conclusion.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break the argument into three parts: the premise (attribute subsists in essence), the consequence (multiple eternal entities), and the rejection (invalid by consensus).
The consensus (ittiqāq) is a key term in kalām arguments.
3
Reflect on the role of consensus
Consider why the author appeals to consensus. In Islamic theology, consensus of scholars is a source of authority; here it serves to rule out a logical possibility on communal grounds.
This argument does not rely solely on reason but also on accepted doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنه لو قامت الصفة بالذات الحصل في الوجود قدماء متغايرة ، وذلك باطل بالاتفاق
The Third Argument: If the attribute subsists in the essence, then there would come into existence multiple eternal entities that are different, and that is invalid by consensus.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This is a concise third argument. It states that if an attribute (صفة) is said to subsist (تقوم) in the essence (ذات), then the existence of the attribute alongside the essence would entail two distinct eternal entities. Since the consensus (اتفاق) of Muslims rejects multiple eternal entities distinct from God, this possibility is invalid. Thus, attributes cannot be distinct from the essence.

Key Terms

ذات
قدماء متغايرة
اتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that subsistence of an attribute in an essence is a neutral concept with no theological implications.
Correction: This argument shows that if an attribute subsists in the divine essence, it would necessarily be a distinct eternal entity, leading to multiple eternals, which contradicts the consensus that there is only one eternal.

Checkpoint

A) It leads to infinite regress.
B) It implies multiple eternal entities, which is invalid by consensus.
C) It makes God spatial.
D) It contradicts divine simplicity.

Key Takeaway
The third argument shows that subsistence of attributes in the essence would entail multiple eternal beings, which is unanimously rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Fourth Argument: Attribute Inherence Implies Spatiality
Sanusi argues that attribute inherence necessitates spatial location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against the attribution of spatiality to God: that attribute inherence logically requires a spatial locus, which is incompatible with divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Attributing inherence to God would place Him in space.

This lesson presents the fourth argument from the text, which challenges the notion that attributes inhere in God's essence in the same way qualities inhere in physical objects. The argument demonstrates that the concept of inherence (حلول) carries an unavoidable implication of spatial location (حيز) and direction (جهة). Since God is free from spatial limitations, the literal inherence of attributes must be rejected. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: حلول, حيز, جهة, قيام.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow.
2
Understand the central claim
Identify the premise: 'the intelligible meaning of inherence is the occurrence of the attribute in a specific locus.' Ask: why must inherence imply location?
This is the key assumption of the argument.
3
Examine the logical inversion
Consider the conditional: if we remove the spatial meaning, then there is no reason for one thing to subsist in another rather than the reverse. This shows that spatial location is essential to the concept of inherence.
This is a reductio ad absurdum within the argument.
4
Apply to God and conclude
Since inherence implies spatial location and direction, and God cannot be spatial, the literal inherence of attributes in God is impossible. This supports divine transcendence.
The conclusion is that God's attributes do not inhere in His essence like physical qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن المعقول من حلول الصفة في الذات حصول تلك الصفة في الحيز المعين ، تبعاً لحصول ذلك المحل فيه ، وإنا لو رفعنا هذا المعنى من العقل ، فحينئذ لا يكون قيام أحدهما بالآخر أولى من قيام الآخر بالأول ، فيثبت أن قيام الصفة بالموصوف ، يقتضي كون الموصوف حاصلاً في الحيز مختصاً بالجهة ، وذلك في حق الله تعالى محال .
The Fourth Argument: The intelligible meaning of the inherence of an attribute in an essence is the occurrence of that attribute in a specific locus (al-hayyiz al-muʿayyan), following the occurrence of that locus in it. And if we remove this meaning from the intellect, then no longer would the subsistence of one in the other be more appropriate than the subsistence of the other in the first. So it is established that the subsistence of an attribute in the subject necessitates the subject's being present in a locus and specified by direction, and that is impossible with respect to God Most High.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This is a fourth argument against attributes, focusing on the logical implications of inherence (حلول). The author says that the only intelligible (معقول) understanding of an attribute inhering in an essence is that the attribute occurs in a specific location (حيز) corresponding to where the essence itself is located. If we deny that, then there is no reason for one to subsist in the other rather than vice versa. Therefore, attributing inherence to God would imply that God is located in a place and has direction (جهة), which is impossible for God. Thus, the concept of attributes inhering in the divine essence is rejected.

Key Terms
حلول (hulul)
حيز (hayyiz)
جهة (jiha)
قيام (qiyam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'inherence' is a purely abstract relation with no spatial implications.
Correction: This argument shows that the ordinary concept of inherence necessarily implies spatial location, so applying it to God would compromise His transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what does inherence of an attribute essentially imply?
A) The attribute is eternal.
B) The subject is located in a specific place and direction.
C) The attribute is identical to the essence.
D) The subject is immaterial.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument demonstrates that literal inherence implies spatial location, which is impossible for God, so attributes cannot inhere in God in a physical sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you unconsciously assign Him a location—like 'up above' or 'in your heart' in a physical sense? How can you train your mind to think of God without any spatial concepts, as this lesson demands?

?

The Fifth Argument: Essence Self-Sufficiency or Need
Dilemma on necessity of attributes for divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that challenges the necessity of divine attributes: either the essence suffices for divinity, making attributes redundant, or it does not, implying neediness in God.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are either unnecessary or imply dependence—both impossible.

This argument, attributed to scholars who negate the existence of real attributes, presents a powerful logical dilemma. It forces the opponent to choose between two unpalatable options: either God's essence alone is enough for divinity, making attributes superfluous, or His essence is not enough, implying He is needy. We'll unpack this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the dilemma.
Notice the two possibilities presented.
2
Identify the two horns
The passage presents two possibilities: essence completes divinity without attributes, or it does not.
3
Analyze implications
Understand what follows from each horn: neediness from the second, redundancy from the first.
Both lead to negation of attributes.
4
Consider the conclusion
The author concludes that such attributes must be negated by consensus.
This is a key claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن ذات الله تعالى ، إما أن يتم إلهيتها بدون هذه الصفات ، وإما أن لا تتم . والثاني يوجب كونه محتاجاً إلى الغير ، والأول يقتضي أن تكون تلك الذات المخصوصة كافية في الإلهية . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، كان الإله غنياً في الميته عن هذه الصفات . ومثل هذه الصفات يجب نفيها بالاتفاق .
The Fifth Argument: The essence of God Most High, either its divinity is completed without these attributes, or it is not. The second necessitates His being in need of another. The first necessitates that that specific essence is sufficient for divinity. And if that is the case, then God is independent in His essence from these attributes. And such attributes must be negated by consensus.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This fifth argument presents a dilemma regarding the necessity of attributes for God's divinity. The author argues that either God's essence (ذات) fully realizes divinity without attributes, or it does not. If it does not, then God would be dependent (محتاج) on something else, which is impossible for God. If it does, then the essence alone is sufficient for divinity, making attributes unnecessary. Therefore, by consensus, such attributes must be negated. The argument assumes that attributes are either essential or redundant.

Key Terms
إلهية (ilahiyya)
محتاج
كافية
غني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes are necessary for God's perfection.
Correction: This argument shows that if attributes were real distinct entities, they would either be redundant or imply dependency, both incompatible with absolute perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, what is the consequence if God's essence is NOT sufficient for divinity without attributes?
A) Attributes are necessary.
B) God would be in need of another.
C) Attributes are eternal.
D) The essence is sufficient.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument uses a dilemma to challenge the necessity of divine attributes, concluding they must be negated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's essence is utterly self-sufficient and needs nothing at all—not even your worship—then why do you pray? Is it truly for Him, or do you secretly believe that your devotion adds something to His perfection? What does this argument expose about your motives?
?
The dilemma shows that to attribute any form of neediness to God is impossible. But consider: where in your daily life do you act as if God is in need of your righteousness, your rituals, or your defense of His religion? How does this lesson shatter that pride?
?
If God is so independent that even His attributes are not real additions to His essence, then all your mental categories of who God is fall short. Are you comfortable worshipping a God you cannot fully define? Or do you, like the pagans, try to reduce Him to manageable concepts? What will you release today that you thought you knew about Him?

The Sixth Argument: Exhaustive Analysis of Sufficiency
Exhaustive case analysis for the sufficiency of essence and attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a systematic case analysis eliminates the need for attributes as causes of divinity, establishing divinity as a necessary concomitant of the essence.

Core Idea
Divinity is a necessary property of the essence, not a product of parts.

This argument is a rigorous logical exercise that examines every possible way divinity might be attributed to Allah. By systematically testing each scenario—essence alone, attributes alone, and their combination—the argument eliminates all alternatives and establishes that divinity is an intrinsic necessary concomitant of the essence. This refutes any notion that attributes are additional causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for rhythm, then the translation. Notice the logical structure of possibilities.
The argument uses a branching case analysis.
2
Identify the key premises
Identify the starting points: essence alone insufficient, attributes alone insufficient. Understand that if either alone sufficed, the other would be superfluous.
3
Analyze the combination scenario
Examine the dilemma: either the combination produces a new effect or it does not. If it does, trace the cause and see the infinite regress. If it does not, the combination yields the same as isolation.
Focus on the logic of the regress argument.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since no scenario yields divinity as a produced effect, divinity must be a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the essence itself. This means attributes are not needed as causes.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument safeguards tawhid by preventing any association of causal power with attributes independent of essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : الذات وحدها، إما أن تكون كافية في الإلهية أو لا تكون . فإن كانت الذات وحدها كافية في حصول الإلمية ، كانت هذه الصفات لغواً في حصول الإلهية ، وإن لم تكن كافية . فنقول : الصفات إما أن تكون كافية في حصول الإلهية أو لا تكون . فإن كانت كافية في الإلمية كانت ) الذات ( ۱ ) لغواً فيها ، وإن لم تكن كافية فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير الذات من حيث إنها هي غير كافية ، والصفات أيضاً غير كافية ، فعند اجتماعهما إما أن يحصل أمر زائد على ما حصل حال الانفراد ، أو لم يكن كذلك . فإن حصل فالموجب لذلك الزائد ، إما الذات وحدها ، أو الصفة وحدها ، وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول . أو مجموعهما فحينئذ يعود الكلام من أنه إما أن يكون قد حصل حال الاجتماع ( ما لم يحصل ) (۲) حال الانفراد ، أو لم يحصل . فإن كان الأول كان حصول ذلك الزائد لأجل سبب زائد آخر ، ولزم التسلسل ، وهو محال ، وإن لم يحصل زائد أصلا كان الحاصل عند الاجتماع، كما هو عند الانفراد ( ولما كان عند الانفراد ) (۳) ولم تكن الإلهية حاصلة ، وجب القطع بعدم حصولها عند الاجتماع، فيثبت أن الإلهية من لوازم تلك الذات المخصوصة من حيث هي هي ، ولا حاجة معها إلى إثبات صفة أخرى .
The Sixth Argument: The essence alone—either it is sufficient for divinity or it is not. If the essence alone is sufficient for achieving divinity, then these attributes are superfluous in achieving divinity. And if it is not sufficient, we say: the attributes—either they are sufficient for achieving divinity or they are not. If they are sufficient for divinity, then the essence (1) is superfluous in it. And if they are not sufficient, we say: on this assumption, the essence as such is not sufficient, and the attributes are also not sufficient. So when they are combined, either something additional occurs beyond what was the case in isolation, or it does not. If it does occur, then the cause of that additional thing is either the essence alone, or the attribute alone, and then the first division recurs; or both together, and then the discussion returns to whether something occurred in combination that did not occur in isolation, or not. If the first, then that additional thing occurred due to another additional cause, leading to infinite regress, which is absurd. And if no additional thing occurs at all, then what is achieved in combination is the same as what is achieved in isolation. And since in isolation divinity was not achieved, it must be concluded that it is also not achieved in combination. So it is established that divinity is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of that specific essence as such, and there is no need with it to affirm another attribute.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This is a complex sixth argument that systematically examines all possibilities for how essence and attributes might combine to produce divinity. The author starts by considering the essence alone: if sufficient, attributes are unnecessary; if not, then consider attributes alone. If attributes alone are sufficient, then the essence is superfluous. If neither alone is sufficient, then we examine their combination. In combination, either something new results or not. If something new results, its cause must be either essence alone, attribute alone, or both. If essence or attribute alone caused it, then that cause alone should have been sufficient, contradicting the assumption. If both caused it, we need to check again if the combination produces something new. If it does, infinite regress follows; if not, then the combination yields no new property, so divinity is not achieved. Thus, the only consistent conclusion is that divinity is a necessary concomitant (لازم) of the essence alone, making attributes redundant. This detailed argument refutes any possible dependence on attributes.

Key Terms
كافية
لغو
زائد
سبب
تسلسل
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes are necessary causes that combine with essence to produce divinity.
Correction: The argument shows that any such combination leads to contradiction or infinite regress; divinity is a necessary concomitant of the essence alone, not a produced effect.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, what is the final conclusion when neither essence nor attributes alone are sufficient for divinity?
A) Divinity is achieved only when both are combined.
B) Divinity is a necessary concomitant of the essence alone.
C) Divinity is impossible without attributes.
D) Divinity requires an infinite number of causes.

Key Takeaway
Divinity is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the essence itself, not dependent on attributes or their combination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you ever feel that your deeds are what ‘complete’ your relationship with Allah, as if His mercy depends on them? How does the truth that His divinity is a necessary concomitant of His essence—not produced by your actions—free you from that burden and draw you to rely solely on Him?
?
Think of a time you tried to ‘earn’ Allah’s favor by adding more prayers, charity, or good deeds, only to feel anxious about your insufficiency. If divinity wasn’t produced by any combination of essence and attributes, what does that say about your attempts to combine actions to secure His pleasure? Will you now rest in the fact that His mercy flows from His essence, not from your efforts?
?
This argument destroys any notion that Allah needs something outside Himself to be divine. But do you secretly add ‘associates’ in your heart—things you trust alongside Allah, like your own intelligence, wealth, or social status? What would it take to truly strip away those dependencies and accept that Allah’s essence is all-sufficient for you?

The First Argument Against God's Knowledge as an Attribute
First argument against knowledge as a distinct attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument used to deny that God has a distinct attribute of knowledge, based on the principle of similarity and temporal modality.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge were like ours, it would be temporal — impossible.

The author presents three arguments against the view that God knows through a separate attribute of knowledge. The first argument draws on the idea that two knowledges relating to the same object from the same aspect would be similar, and similarity forces identical temporal status. Since human knowledge is created (temporal) and God's knowledge must be eternal, such similarity is impossible. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Let the author's own wording establish the logical structure.
Notice the conditional 'if...then' structure.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break the argument into its premises: (1) If God knows by an attribute, His knowledge relates to the same objects as human knowledge from the same aspect. (2) Two such knowledges are similar. (3) Similarity forces identical temporal status (both eternal or both temporal). (4) This leads to absurdity because human knowledge is temporal and God's knowledge is eternal.
The argument uses the principle of تماثل (similarity).
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself why the argument is considered valid by its proponents. Consider how a defender of the attribute of knowledge might respond.
This argument is one of three; later lessons will examine its refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجوه التي تمسكوا بها في نفي العلم خاصة . فهي ثلاثة : الحجة الأولى : لو كان تعالى عالماً بالعلم ، لتعلق علمه بما تعلق به علم الواحد منا ، من الوجه الواحد . وكل علمين كذلك فهما مثلان ، ويلزم من تماثلهما ، إما قدمهما معاً ، أو حدوثهما معاً ، وذلك محال .
As for the aspects (wujuh) that they held to in negating knowledge specifically, they are three: The First Argument: If He Most High were knowing by knowledge, then His knowledge would attach to what the knowledge of one of us attaches to, from the same aspect. And any two such knowledges are similar (mithlan), and from their similarity follows either both are eternal or both are temporal, and that is absurd.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage introduces a set of three arguments specifically aimed at denying that God has knowledge (علم) as a distinct attribute. The author states that there are three such arguments, and then gives the first. The first argument says: if God were knowing by means of a knowledge (i.e., an attribute of knowledge), then that divine knowledge would be related to its objects in the same way as human knowledge relates to its objects. Whenever two knowledges are related in the same way, they are similar (مثلان). If they are similar, then they must share the same temporal status: both eternal or both temporal. Since God's knowledge must be eternal and human knowledge is temporal, similarity would force either both eternal (impossible for human knowledge) or both temporal (impossible for God). Therefore, God cannot have knowledge as an attribute distinct from His essence.

Key Terms
علم (ʿilm)
عالم (ʿālim)
تماثل (tamāthul)
قدم (qidam)
حدوث (ḥudūth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Similarity between two entities does not necessarily imply they share the same temporal status.
Correction: The argument relies on the principle that if two things are similar in all relevant respects (here, their mode of relation to objects), they must share the same modality of existence (eternal or temporal). This is a standard principle in kalam.

Checkpoint
What does the first argument conclude about God having knowledge as an attribute?
A) God's knowledge is unlike human knowledge.
B) It would require God's knowledge and human knowledge to be either both eternal or both temporal, which is impossible.
C) God's knowledge is identical to His essence.
D) Human knowledge is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against God having a distinct knowledge attribute uses similarity to force impossible temporal implications.

The Sixth Proof: The Essence Suffices for Divinity
The author presents a logical proof that the divine essence alone suffices for divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rational proof demonstrates that divinity is a necessary concomitant of the essence alone, without requiring distinct attributes.

Core Idea
Divinity belongs to the essence itself, not to separate attributes.

This lesson presents a sophisticated logical argument from Islamic theology that the divine essence alone is sufficient for divinity. The proof eliminates the need for distinct attributes as separate entities, showing that attributes would be redundant or lead to an infinite regress. Follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two initial possibilities
The author first considers whether the essence alone suffices for divinity or not. If it does, attributes are redundant. If not, we move to examine attributes.
This sets up the first disjunction.
2
Analyze the case when attributes are considered
Now the same question is applied to attributes: either they suffice for divinity or not. If they suffice, the essence becomes redundant. If not, we proceed to the combined scenario.
3
Examine the combined scenario
When neither essence nor attributes alone suffice, we ask whether their combination produces something new. If yes, what is its cause? If no, then the combination is like the separate state.
This leads to two paths: addition with cause, or no addition.
4
Trace the implications of each branch
If an addition occurs, its cause must be essence, attribute, or both. This leads to a repetition of the same division or an infinite regress. If no addition, then the combined state equals the separate state, and divinity was not achieved.
Both branches lead to a conclusion.
5
Conclude: divinity is a necessary concomitant
The only consistent outcome is that divinity is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the essence itself, requiring no separate attribute. This settles the proof.
The proof eliminates the need for distinct attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : الذات وحدها، إما أن تكون كافية في الإلهية أو لا تكون . فإن كانت الذات وحدها كافية في حصول الإلهية ، كانت هذه الصفات لغواً في حصول الإلهية ، وإن لم تكن كافية . فنقول : الصفات إما أن تكون كافية في حصول الإلهية أو لا تكون . فإن كانت كافية في الإلهية كانت الذات لغواً فيها ، وإن لم تكن كافية فنقول : فعلى هذا التقدير الذات من حيث إنها هي غير كافية ، والصفات أيضاً غير كافية ، فعند اجتماعهما إما أن يحصل أمر زائد على ما حصل حال الانفراد ، أو لم يكن كذلك . فإن حصل فالموجب لذلك الزائد ، إما الذات وحدها ، أو الصفة وحدها ، وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول . أو مجموعهما فحينئذ يعود الكلام من أنه إما أن يكون قد حصل حال الاجتماع ( ما لم يحصل ) حال الانفراد ، أو لم يحصل . فإن كان الأول كان حصول ذلك الزائد لأجل سبب زائد آخر ، ولزم التسلسل ، وهو محال ، وإن لم يحصل زائد أصلا كان الحاصل عند الاجتماع، كما هو عند الانفراد ( ولما كان عند الانفراد ) ولم تكن الإلهية حاصلة ، وجب القطع بعدم حصولها عند الاجتماع، فيثبت أن الإلهية من لوازم تلك الذات المخصوصة من حيث هي هي ، ولا حاجة معها إلى إثبات صفة أخرى .
The sixth proof: The essence alone either is sufficient for divinity or is not. If the essence alone is sufficient for the occurrence of divinity, then these attributes are superfluous for the occurrence of divinity; and if it is not sufficient, we say: The attributes either are sufficient for the occurrence of divinity or are not. If they are sufficient for divinity, then the essence is superfluous in it; and if they are not sufficient, we say: on this assumption the essence in itself is insufficient, and the attributes are also insufficient. So when they are combined, either an additional thing occurs beyond what occurred in the state of separation, or it does not. If it occurs, then the cause of that additional thing is either the essence alone or the attribute alone, and then the first division returns; or both together, then the discussion returns as to whether something occurred in the state of combination that did not occur in the state of separation, or not. If the first, then the occurrence of that additional thing is due to another additional cause, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible. And if no addition occurs at all, then what is obtained in combination is as it is in separation, and since divinity was not obtained in separation, it must be decided that it is not obtained in combination. Thus it is established that divinity is a necessary concomitant (لازم) of that specific essence in itself, and there is no need with it to posit another attribute.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a proof (الحجة السادسة) that the divine attributes are not necessary as distinct entities for divinity. The author uses a disjunctive syllogism: either the essence alone suffices for divinity or not. If it does, attributes are redundant. If not, then the attributes themselves are examined: either they suffice or not. If they suffice, the essence is redundant. If they also do not suffice, then when combined, either something additional occurs or not. If an additional thing occurs, its cause must be either the essence alone, the attribute alone, or both, leading to an infinite regress or repetition of the same division. If no addition occurs, then the combined state is like the separate state, and since divinity was not achieved in separation, it is not achieved in combination. Therefore, divinity is a necessary concomitant (لازم) of the essence itself, without need for separate attributes.

Key Terms
الإلهية
الصفات
الذات
اللزوم
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies all divine attributes entirely, asserting that God has no attributes.
Correction: The proof only denies that attributes are distinct separate entities needed for divinity. Attributes can still be understood as modes or necessary concomitants of the essence, not as independent realities.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, what is established about divinity?
A) Divinity requires both essence and attributes.
B) Divinity is a necessary concomitant of the essence alone.
C) Attributes alone suffice for divinity.
D) Divinity is impossible without attributes.

Key Takeaway
Divinity is a necessary concomitant of the essence, and no separate attribute is needed for divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining that God's attributes add something to His essence, as if He is more divine because of them? This proof calls you to strip away such hidden polytheism from your heart. Will you repent today and affirm that His essence alone is sufficient for His divinity?
?
If divinity is a necessary concomitant of God's essence, then your knowledge of His attributes is secondary to knowing Him as He is in Himself. What concrete step will you take this week to study His names as mere windows to His essence, not as additions to Him?
?
The proof shows that any attempt to make attributes necessary for divinity leads to infinite regress or redundancy. Are there any beliefs in your life where you treat something from creation as necessary for God to be God? Commit to uprooting that misconception now.

Three Arguments Against God Knowing by a Distinct Science
Three mutazilite arguments against God knowing by a distinct science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical arguments the Mu'tazila used to argue that God cannot know by a distinct science, and the structure of each argument.

Core Idea
Three logical dilemmas aim to show that a distinct science in God leads to impossibility.

The Mu'tazila, in their debate with the Ash'aris, argued that attributing a distinct science (علم) to God leads to unacceptable consequences. This lesson presents their three arguments, each targeting a different aspect: the similarity to human science, the number of sciences, and the problem of self-knowledge. These arguments are foundational for understanding the later refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage enumerates three distinct arguments (الحجة الأولى, الثانية, الثالثة).
The arguments are presented in a condensed style typical of kalam texts.
2
Analyze the first argument
The first argument claims that if God's science is similar to human science, both must share the same temporal status—either both eternal or both originated. Since God's attributes are eternal and human sciences are originated, this is impossible.
The key concept is تماثل (similarity) and its implications for temporal origin.
3
Examine the second argument's trilemma
The second argument presents three possibilities: one science, finite sciences, or infinite sciences. Each is shown to be impossible. One science cannot cover multiple distinct objects; finite sciences cannot cover infinite objects; infinite sciences are impossible because every existing number is finite.
This is a classic trilemma argument (تقسيم) that exhausts all options.
4
Understand the third argument
The third argument deals with God knowing His own science. If He knows it by the same science, that is impossible due to the structure of relational knowledge. If by another science, it leads to circularity or infinite regress, both impossible.
This argument uses logical impossibility of self-reference and regress.
5
Reflect on the overall purpose
These arguments are presented as objections to the Ash'ari position. In later lessons, you will see how these objections are answered. For now, focus on the logical structure of each argument.
The passage does not include the rebuttals; it only states the arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجوه التي تمسكوا بها في نفي العلم خاصة. فهي ثلاثة : الحجة الأولى : لو كان تعالى عالماً بالعلم ، لتعلق علمه بما تعلق به علم الواحد منا ، من الوجه الواحد . وكل علمين كذلك فهما مثلان ، ويلزم من تماثلهما ، إما قدمهما معاً ، أو حدوثهما معاً ، وذلك محال . الحجة الثانية : لو كان تعالى عالماً بالعلم ، لكان إما أن يكون عالماً بعلم واحد ، أو بعلوم متناهية ، أو بعلوم غير متناهية ، والثلاثة باطلة ، فالقول بكونه عالماً بالعلم باطل . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون عالماً بعلم واحد ، لأنه لا يصح أن يعلم كونه تعالى عالماً بهذا المعلوم مع الذهول عن كونه بالمعلوم الآخر ، والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم. وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون عالماً بعلوم متناهية ، لأن معلوماته غير متناهية . وإذا توزعت المعلومات المتناهية على المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها ، لزم تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومات كثيرة . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون عالماً بعلوم غير متناهية ، لأن كل عدد موجود ، فإنه يقبل الزيادة والنقصان ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناهي . ينتج : أن كل عدد موجود فهو متناهى . وما لا يكون متناهياً ، يمتنع وجوده . الحجة الثالثة : لو كان عالماً بالعلم، لكان إما أن يعلم ذلك العلم بعين ذلك العلم ، وهو محال . لأن العلم لا بد فيه من نسبة مخصوصة ، وحصول النسبة بين الشيء وبين نفسه محال ، أو بعلم آخر ، والكلام فيه كما في الأول . فيلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل ، وهما محالان .
And as for the arguments they held to in denying [that God knows by] a science in particular, they are three: The first argument: If God were knowing by a science, then His science would be related to what the science of one of us is related to, in one respect. And any two sciences [related] in that way are similar (مثلان), and from their similarity follows either both are eternal or both are originated, and that is impossible. The second argument: If God were knowing by a science, then He would be either knowing by one science, or by finite sciences, or by infinite sciences, and all three are false, so the statement that He is knowing by a science is false. We say: It is not permissible that He be knowing by one science, because it is not valid that He know His being knowing this known while being heedless of His being [knowing] the other known, and the known is other than what is not known. And we say: It is not permissible that He be knowing by finite sciences, because His objects of knowledge are infinite. And if the finite sciences are distributed over the infinite objects, then one science would be related to many objects. And we say: It is not permissible that He be knowing by infinite sciences, because every existing number admits increase and decrease, and everything that is so is finite. The conclusion: every existing number is finite. And what is not finite cannot exist. The third argument: If He were knowing by a science, then He would either know that science by that same science, which is impossible, because science necessarily involves a specific relation, and the occurrence of a relation between a thing and itself is impossible; or by another science, and the discussion about it is as in the first. Thus either circularity or infinite regress follows, and both are impossible.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments attributed to the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari position that God knows by a distinct science (علم). The first argument claims that if God's science is analogous to human sciences, then two such sciences would be similar, implying both are either eternal or originated—both impossible for God's attributes. The second argument presents a dilemma: God's science would be either one, finite, or infinite. One science cannot cover multiple disparate objects. Finite sciences cannot cover infinite objects of knowledge. Infinite sciences are impossible because infinite numbers are non-existent (since every number is finite). Thus the concept is false. The third argument argues that if God knows His own science, He would know it either by itself (impossible) or by another science (leading to circularity or regress). These arguments are meant to refute the idea of a distinct science in God.

Key Terms
العلم
المعلوم
مثلان
دور
تسلسل
متناهي
غير متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both are eternal.
B) Both are originated.
C) Either both eternal or both originated.
D) Both are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila presented three logical arguments to show that the concept of a distinct science in God leads to contradictions, but these arguments are meant to be refuted in subsequent discussion.

?

?
Have you ever assumed that your own human way of knowing is the only way things can be known? How does this passage challenge you to humble your mind before the reality of God? What concrete step will you take this week to submit your intellect to God's revelation?

Refuting Objections to Divine Knowledge
The author refutes three arguments against the necessity of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes three arguments against the necessity of God's knowledge by clarifying the relationship between essence and attributes.

Core Idea
God's essence necessitates His knowledge without circularity.

The Ash'ari position that God's knowledge is necessary for His essence faces several objections. In this passage, the author answers three of them: the first based on the impossibility of one thing being both receptive and effective, the second questioning why the essence cannot necessitate both knowledge and power, and a separate Mu'tazili argument about the nature of necessity. Each response reinforces the idea that divine attributes flow necessarily from the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
Read the passage and note that the author addresses three distinct counter-arguments. The first two are against earlier arguments, and the third is a separate Mu'tazili argument.
The passage begins with 'والجواب عن الأول' and then 'ومن الثاني' then 'وأما الحجة الأولى للمعتزلة'.
2
Analyze the first response
The author rejects the premise that one thing cannot be both receptive (قابل) and effective (فاعل). He claims this premise has already been shown false.
3
Understand the second response
The author asserts that the specific essence, which is necessary in itself, can necessitate both knowledge (عالمية) and power (قادرية) without contradiction.
This defends the idea that attributes are necessitated by the essence.
4
Parse the Mu'tazili argument and its answer
The Mu'tazila argue about the necessity of God's knowledge. The author distinguishes two meanings of 'necessary': if it means from the essence itself, that is the point of dispute; if it means absolutely, then the author's view is unaffected because the essence necessitates a meaning that necessitates the attribute.
This clarifies that the attribute is still necessary in a derived sense.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
These responses show how Ash'ari theologians defend the doctrine that divine attributes are necessary without making them independent of the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول : إنه بناء على أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً . وقد بينا أنه باطل . ومن الثاني : لم لا يجوز أن يقال الذات المخصوصة واجبة الوجود لعينها وهي موجبة لتلك العالمية المخصوصة ولتلك القادرية ؟ . وأما الحجة الأولى للمعتزلة فتقول : إن عنيت بكون عالمية الله واجبة لعين تلك الذات المخصوصة ، فهذا عين محل النزاع. وإن أردت أنها واجبة على الإطلاق فنقول : هذا لا يقدح في غرضنا ، فإن ذات الله إذا كانت موجبة لمعنى ، وكان ذلك المعنى موجباً للعالمية ، فحينئذ تكون ذاته موجبة لما يوجب العالمية وذلك لا يقدح في كون العالمية واجبة .
And the answer to the first [argument] is that it is based on the impossibility of one thing being both receptive and effective, and we have already shown that this is false. And from the second [argument or approach], why is it not permissible to say that the specific essence is necessary of existence in itself and it necessitates that specific attribute of being knowing (worldiyya) and that attribute of being powerful (qadiriyya)? As for the first argument of the Mu'tazila, they say: If you mean by 'the attribute of being knowing of God is necessary for that specific essence', then this is the very point of dispute. And if you mean that it is necessary absolutely, we say: This does not harm our purpose, for if God's essence necessitates a meaning, and that meaning necessitates the attribute of being knowing, then His essence necessitates what necessitates the attribute of being knowing, and that does not harm that the attribute of being knowing is necessary.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage contains three responses. First, the author answers the first argument (likely the first of the three from the previous lesson) by rejecting its premise that one thing cannot be both receptive and active; this premise has already been refuted. Second, he responds to the second argument (perhaps the second of the three) by stating that the specific essence, which is necessary of existence in itself, can necessitate both the attribute of knowledge (عالمية) and power (قادرية) without circularity. Third, he addresses a separate Mu'tazili argument concerning the necessity of God's knowledge: if by 'necessary' one means it is necessitated by the essence, that is exactly the point in dispute; if one means it is necessary absolutely, that does not refute the Ash'ari position, because the essence necessitates a meaning which in turn necessitates the attribute, so the attribute is still necessary in a derived sense.

قابل
واجب الوجود
عالمية

موجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the response concedes that attributes are not necessary.
Correction: The author actually refines the meaning of necessity, showing that attributes are necessary in a derived sense from the essence.

Checkpoint

A) That one thing cannot be both eternal and originated.
B) That one thing cannot be both receptive and effective.
C) That one thing cannot be both necessary and contingent.

Key Takeaway
The author successfully defends the necessity of God's attributes by showing that they are necessitated by His essence without circularity or contradiction.

The Trilemma Argument Against Knowledge Through a Science
Second proof refuting that God knows through a science by enumeration of possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that refutes the notion that God knows through a distinct science by showing that all possible scenarios are impossible.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot be through a science because it leads to an impossible trilemma.

This lesson presents a powerful argument by elimination. The opponent claims God knows through an attribute called knowledge. The argument forces the opponent to choose among three options, then shows each is rationally impossible. By the end, you'll see why the claim collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the trilemma
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the three possibilities: one science, finite sciences, infinite sciences.
The passage begins with 'either...or...or' structure.
2
Examine the refutation of each possibility
The text explains why each of the three possibilities is impossible. Pay attention to the reasoning for each: one science cannot cover distinct objects; finite sciences cannot cover infinite objects; infinite sciences cannot exist because every existing number is finite.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since all three possibilities are false, the premise that God knows through a science is false. Understand that this does not deny God's knowledge, only the means of it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو كان تعالى عالماً بالعلم ، لكان إما أن يكون عالماً بعلم واحد ، أو بعلوم متناهية ، أو بعلوم غير متناهية ، والثلاثة باطلة ، فالقول بكونه عالماً بالعلم باطل . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون عالماً بعلم واحد ، لأنه لا يصح أن يعلم كونه تعالى عالماً بهذا المعلوم مع الذهول عن كونه بالمعلوم الآخر ، والمعلوم مغاير لغير المعلوم. وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون عالماً بعلوم متناهية ، لأن معلوماته غير متناهية . وإذا توزعت المعلومات المتناهية على المعلومات التي لا نهاية لها ، لزم تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومات كثيرة . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون عالماً بعلوم غير متناهية ، لأن كل عدد موجود ، فإنه يقبل الزيادة والنقصان ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناهي . ينتج : أن كل عدد موجود فهو متناهى . وما لا يكون متناهياً ، يمتنع وجوده .
The second proof: If the Exalted were knowing through knowledge (علم), then either He would be knowing through one knowledge, or through finite knowledges, or through infinite knowledges. All three are false, so the statement that He is knowing through knowledge is false. We say: It is not permissible that He be knowing through one knowledge, because it is not valid that He know His being knowing this known object while being heedless of His knowledge of the other known object, and the known is different from what is not known. And we say: It is not permissible that He be knowing through finite knowledges, because His known objects are infinite. And if finite knowledges are distributed over infinite known objects, then one knowledge would be attached to many known objects. And we say: It is not permissible that He be knowing through infinite knowledges, because every existing number is subject to increase and decrease, and everything that is such is finite. It follows that every existing number is finite. And what is not finite, its existence is impossible.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by enumeration of possibilities (taqsīm) against the view that God knows through a distinct science (ʻilm). The author assumes the opponent's position that God's knowledge is through an attribute called knowledge, and then shows that this leads to an impossible triple choice. First, if it were one science, it would have to cover all known objects, but knowledge of one object does not entail knowledge of another, and the objects are distinct. Second, if the sciences are finite in number, they cannot cover the infinite objects of God's knowledge; one science would have to know multiple objects, which is impossible. Third, if the sciences are infinite, then they would form an infinite number, but any existing number is finite because it can be increased or decreased. Therefore, the claim that God knows through a science is false. The conclusion is that the assumption is invalid.

Key Terms
علم
عالمية
معلوم
متناهي
غير متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that God knows anything at all.
Correction: It only denies that His knowledge is through a distinct attribute; it does not deny that He knows.

Checkpoint
What is the third possibility rejected in the second proof?
A) Knowing through one science
B) Knowing through finite sciences
C) Knowing through infinite sciences
D) Knowing without any science

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that God's knowledge cannot be through a distinct science because the only three possibilities (one science, finite sciences, infinite sciences) are all impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

After studying this trilemma, how does it challenge you to trust that God's knowledge is not like human compartmentalization? Will you consciously repeat today: 'God knows all without a science' when doubts arise?
?
When you face a tragedy or injustice, do you sometimes doubt that God knows your exact pain? This argument forces you to admit that if knowledge were through a science, it would be impossible for God to know everything—but since He is the all-knowing, that cannot be. How does that truth demand a change in your heart? This week, will you surrender one specific doubt to God's infinite knowledge?
?
The trilemma shows that God's knowledge cannot be divided or multiplied. Are you willing to purge any anthropomorphic ideas you hold about God? What specific image of God's mind do you need to replace with the reality that His knowledge is beyond all categories? Make a concrete commitment to learn one more attribute of God this week.

Third Proof and Its Answer
Third proof against knowing through a science and the author's two-part reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof against knowing through a science and the author's two-part refutation, including the critique of a false premise and the alternative grounding of attributes in the essence.

Core Idea
The third proof's flaw is its assumption that one thing cannot be both agent and patient.

The third proof attempts to show that if God knows through knowledge, an infinite regress or circularity is unavoidable. However, the author dismantles this objection by exposing a false premise and then offering a positive account of how God's attributes are rooted in His essence. This passage is a masterclass in theological dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Let the argument's flow become familiar before you analyze it.
2
Identify the structure
Notice that the passage has three parts: the proof (the objection), and then two separate answers. Label them in your mind or on paper.
The proof is presented first, then the author responds with 'والجواب'.
3
Understand the first answer
The first answer challenges the proof's premise that one thing cannot be both subject and object (قابلاً وفاعلاً). The author claims this premise is false and that it has been refuted earlier.
This is a common dialectical move: show that the objection relies on an unestablished assumption.
4
Understand the second answer
The second answer offers a positive alternative: the divine essence itself necessitates its attributes (الذات المخصوصة واجبة الوجود لعينها وهي موجبة لتلك العالمية والقادرية). This avoids any regress.
This grounds the attributes directly in the essence without requiring an intermediary science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كان عالماً بالعلم، لكان إما أن يعلم ذلك العلم بعين ذلك العلم ، وهو محال . لأن العلم لا بد فيه من نسبة مخصوصة ، وحصول النسبة بين الشيء وبين نفسه محال ، أو بعلم آخر ، والكلام فيه كما في الأول . فيلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل ، وهما محالان . والجواب عن الأول : إنه بناء على أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً . وقد بينا أنه باطل . ومن الثاني : لم لا يجوز أن يقال الذات المخصوصة واجبة الوجود لعينها وهي موجبة لتلك العالمية المخصوصة ولتلك القادرية ؟
The third proof: If He were knowing through knowledge, then either He would know that knowledge by that same knowledge—and that is impossible, because knowledge necessarily involves a specific relation, and the occurrence of a relation between a thing and itself is impossible—or by another knowledge, and the discussion regarding it is as in the first. Thus either circularity or infinite regress follows, and both are impossible. The answer to the first: It is based on the premise that one thing cannot be both subject and object, and we have shown that this is false. And from the second: Why is it not permissible to say that the specific essence is necessary of existence by itself and it necessitates that specific attribute of being knowing (al-ʻālamiyya) and that specific attribute of being powerful (al-qādariyya)?
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against the view that God knows through a science. It argues that if God knows through a science, then that science itself must be known. Either it is known by itself, which is impossible because knowledge requires a relation between knower and known, and a relation between a thing and itself is impossible. Or it is known by another science, which leads to an infinite regress or circularity. Both are impossible. The author then gives two answers. First, the premise that one thing cannot be both subject and object is false; the author claims to have already refuted it. Second, why not simply say that the specific essence of God necessitates both the attribute of being knowing and the attribute of being powerful? This avoids regress by grounding the attributes directly in the essence.

Key Terms
علم
نسبة
دور
تسلسل
عالمية
قادرية
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second part of the answer to the third proof?
A) It is based on a false premise
B) The essence itself necessitates the attributes
C) Knowledge is not a relation
D) Both circularity and regress are impossible

Key Takeaway
The third proof fails because its key premise is false, and the attributes can be directly necessitated by the essence without regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The author argues that the divine essence necessitates knowing and powerful without regress. In your own life, where do you need to trust that God's attributes are inherent to His essence rather than dependent on external causes? What change in your perspective does this demand?

First Mu'tazilite Proof and Its Refutation
First Mu'tazilite proof and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first Mu'tazilite proof for God's being knowing and how the author refutes it by distinguishing necessity through the essence versus absolute necessity.

Core Idea
Necessity through the essence does not make an attribute independent; it flows from the essence.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if God's attribute of knowledge is necessary, it must be either necessary by the essence or absolutely necessary. The author responds by accepting necessity through the essence but denying absolute independence. This short passage clarifies a key subtlety in the debate on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the two conditional scenarios presented.
The passage uses 'إن عنيت' (if you mean) and 'وإن أردت' (and if you intend).
2
Analyze the first scenario
The first scenario: if the Mu'tazila mean that ʿālamiyya is necessary by the essence itself. The author says this is exactly the point of dispute.
The author does not reject this; he accepts it but notes it is the very issue under debate.
3
Analyze the second scenario
The second scenario: if they mean it is necessary absolutely (independently). The author replies that this does not harm his position.
He explains that the essence necessitates a meaning, and that meaning necessitates the attribute.
4
Understand the logical chain
Trace the chain: Essence → Meaning (maʿnā) → Attribute (ʿālamiyya). This shows how the attribute is necessary yet dependent on the essence.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why this refutation preserves the necessity of the attribute without making it independent from the essence.
The attribute is necessary, but only through the essence's mediation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الأولى للمعتزلة فتقول : إن عنيت بكون عالمية الله واجبة لعين تلك الذات المخصوصة ، فهذا عين محل النزاع. وإن أردت أنها واجبة على الإطلاق فنقول : هذا لا يقدح في غرضنا ، فإن ذات الله إذا كانت موجبة لمعنى ، وكان ذلك المعنى موجباً للعالمية ، فحينئذ تكون ذاته موجبة لما يوجب العالمية وذلك لا يقدح في كون العالمية واجبة .
As for the first proof of the Mu'tazila, it says: If you mean by 'the attribute of God's being knowing (ʻālamiyya) is necessary for that specific essence itself', then this is exactly the point of dispute. And if you mean that it is necessary absolutely, then we say: This does not harm our purpose, for if God's essence necessitates a meaning, and that meaning necessitates the attribute of being knowing, then His essence necessitates what necessitates the attribute of being knowing, and that does not harm the fact that the attribute of being knowing is necessary.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite proof and the author's refutation. The Mu'tazila argued that if God's being knowing (ʻālamiyya) is necessary, it must be either necessary by the essence itself or absolutely necessary. The author points out that if they mean it is necessary by the essence itself, that is exactly the point of dispute (since the author accepts that). If they mean it is necessary absolutely (without dependence on the essence), then the author replies that he does not claim absolute necessity; rather, the essence necessitates a meaning (maʻnā), which in turn necessitates the attribute of being knowing. So the attribute is necessary, but through the essence, which does not harm the author's position.

معنى

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author denies that God's attribute of being knowing is necessary.
Correction: The author does not deny necessity; he denies that the attribute is necessary independently of the essence.

Checkpoint

A) The attribute directly
B) A meaning which then necessitates the attribute
C) Nothing
D) The attribute absolutely

Key Takeaway
God's essence necessitates a meaning, which in turn necessitates the attribute of knowledge, preserving necessity without making the attribute independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you face a trial that makes you question God's knowledge of your situation, do you truly believe that His attribute of knowledge is necessary and perfect? How does this belief affect your patience and trust in His decree? This week, when doubt creeps in, consciously affirm: 'Allah's knowledge is necessary and flows from His essence.'
?
If God's knowledge is not a separate entity but derived from His essence through a meaning, then His knowledge is inseparable from who He is. Does your prayer life reflect that you are speaking to the All-Knowing whose knowledge is inherent? What change will you make today to approach Allah with the confidence that He knows you absolutely?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that necessity implies independence. Are there areas in your life where you have considered God's attributes as distant or disconnected from His essence, making you feel distant from Him? How can you internalize that His knowledge, power, and mercy are not separate but flow directly from His very being?

The Second Mu'tazilite Proof and Its Refutation
Second Mu'tazilite proof using the same argument against the attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the second Mu'tazilite proof by turning their own argument against them.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite objection to 'meaning' applies equally to their own attributes.

The author presents a concise but powerful refutation of the second Mu'tazilite proof. Instead of engaging with their specific argument, he shows that whatever they say against the concept of a mediating meaning can be said against their own position regarding the attributes. This is a classic argumentative strategy known as 'turning the tables'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the core argument.
Notice the use of 'their' (هم) referring to the Mu'tazila.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
The Mu'tazila reject an intervening meaning between essence and attribute. They assert that attributes like knowledge and power are directly from the essence.
They are 'concerned' (محترفون) with how these attributes occur.
3
Identify the refutation
The author says whatever objection they raise against 'meaning' will be applied to their own attributes. This shows inconsistency.
This is a dialectical move.
4
Reflect on the strategy
Consider why this refutation is effective without needing to detail the objection.
It uses the principle of parity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية لهم : فنقول : إنهم محترفون بحصول العالمية والقادرية ، فكل ما تذكرونه في المعنى، فنحن نورده عليكم في العالمية والقادرية .
As for the second proof of theirs, we say: They are concerned with the occurrence of the attribute of being knowing (al-ʻālamiyya) and the attribute of being powerful (al-qādariyya). So whatever you mention regarding the meaning (al-maʻnā), we bring it against you regarding the attribute of being knowing and the attribute of being powerful.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite proof and a succinct refutation. The author says that the Mu'tazila are worried about how the attributes of being knowing and being powerful occur in God. Whatever objections they raise against the concept of a 'meaning' (maʻnā) that mediates between essence and attribute, the same objections can be raised against their own position regarding the attributes themselves. That is, if they claim that the attributes are directly from the essence without any intervening meaning, then the same difficulties apply. This is a method of refutation by turning the argument back on the opponent.

Key Terms
عالمية
قادرية
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author elaborates the Mu'tazilite objection in detail here.
Correction: The author does not elaborate the objection; he uses a general principle of reciprocity.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the second Mu'tazilite proof?
A) He denies the attributes
B) He says the same arguments apply to the Mu'tazila's own view
C) He accepts the proof
D) He ignores the proof

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the second Mu'tazilite proof by demonstrating that their own argument against the 'meaning' applies equally to their attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on Allah's attributes, do you ever find yourself preferring your own logical reasoning over the plain revelation? This lesson shows how the Mu'tazila's intellect turned against them. Will you submit your mind to the Qur'an and Sunnah, even when it challenges your assumptions?
?
The author refuted his opponents by turning their own argument against them. Are there areas in your life where you hold others to a standard you do not apply to yourself? How can you ensure consistency in your own beliefs and actions before Allah?
