Third Mu'tazilite Proof: Two Eternals and the Distinction of Meanings
Third Mu'tazilite proof concerning the implication of two eternal entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the unity of divine attributes by distinguishing between ontological distinctness and spatiotemporal separability.

Core Idea
Conceding a distinction is not conceding separability.

This lesson examines the third Mu'tazilite proof against the Ash'ari position on attributes. The author shows that the argument rests on an equivocation: while the essence and the meaning are indeed distinct realities, that does not mean they are separable in place or time. Understanding this response is key to defending the coherence of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two conditional phrases 'if you mean... and if you mean...'
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage presents two meanings of 'distinct things' (al-ghayrayn). Find each one and note how the author responds to each.
The first interpretation is ontological; the second is spatiotemporal.
3
Understand the concession
The author concedes the first meaning: that the essence and meaning are two different realities. Why is this concession not damaging to his position?
Conceding distinct realities does not imply plurality of eternal entities.
4
Examine the challenge
For the second meaning, the author demands proof that every two existents must be separable. How does this challenge undermine the Mu'tazilite argument?
The burden of proof shifts to the opponent.
5
Reflect on the logical move
Consider why this response is effective. The author does not deny distinctness but denies that distinctness entails separability.
This is a common strategy in kalam: clarifying terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثالثة : وهو التزام قدماء متغايرة ، فإن عنيتم بالغيرين الحقيقتان المختلفتان . فهذا مسلم. وهو أول المسألة ، وإن عنيتم به الشيئين اللذين يجوز أن يفارق أحدهما الآخر، إما بمكان أو بزمان فلم قلتم : إن كل موجودين يجب أن يكونا كذلك ؟
As for the third proof: It is the implication of two ancient (eternal) entities that are distinct. If you mean by 'two distinct things' (al-ghayrayn) two different realities, then this is conceded. That is the beginning of the issue. And if you mean by it two things that may separate from one another, either in place or time, then why do you say that every two existents must be like that?
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the third Mu'tazilite proof, which claims that positing a meaning (maʻnā) distinct from the essence implies two eternal entities, which is problematic. The author responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'distinct things' (ghayrayn). If they mean two different realities (ḥaqīqatān mukhtalifatān), then he concedes that—the essence and the meaning are indeed different realities. But if they mean two things that can separate from each other in place or time, then that is not necessary for all existents. The author challenges the Mu'tazila to prove that every two existents must be capable of separation. Thus, the objection is based on an equivocation.

Key Terms
الغيرين
الحقيقتان
مختلفتان
قدماء
موجودين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceding that the essence and meaning are distinct realities implies that they are separable and thus constitute two eternal entities.
Correction: The author shows that distinctness of reality does not entail spatiotemporal separability; the opponent must prove that all distinct existents are separable, which they cannot.

Checkpoint

A) That two eternals are impossible
B) That the essence and meaning are the same reality
C) That they are two different realities
D) That they can separate

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof fails because it equivocates between two senses of 'distinct things'; the author concedes one sense but challenges the second.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author challenges the Mu'tazilites to justify their assumption about separability. Examine your own beliefs: Is there any aspect of Allah’s nature where you unconsciously assume something that is not proven? Fourth Mu'tazilite Proof on Inherence and Its Refutation
Fourth Mu'tazilite proof on inherence and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth Mu'tazilite proof for inherence and the author's response, including why he dismisses a key objection as a weak premise.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites define inherence as location-based, but the author labels their objection as feeble.

This passage presents the fourth Mu'tazilite argument for inherence, which is based on the idea of attribute occupying the same location as its substrate. The author states that this definition has already been refuted in an earlier discussion about substance and accident. He then addresses and dismisses a potential objection as relying on a weak premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the structure: first the proof, then the author's response to an objection.
The passage is concise; each word matters in kalām dialectics.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite definition
Find the part where the Mu'tazilite definition of inherence is given: it is the occurrence of the attribute in a location following its substrate. Write it in your own words.
The author claims to have already refuted this definition.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection says that under this definition, it is arbitrary to say the attribute inheres in the substrate rather than vice versa. Note why the author calls this premise 'feeble and weak'.
The rejection is based on the weakness of the principle of equality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة : وهي أن المعقول من الحلول حصول الصفة في الحيز ، تبعاً لحصول محلها فيه . فقد أبطلنا هذا التفسير في باب تفسير الجوهر والعرض . وقوله : لو دفعنا بهذا المعنى لم يكن حلول أحدهما في الآخر أولى من العكس : فجوابه : إنا بينا : أن قول القائل ليس هذا أولى من ذلك مقدمة ركيكة ضعيفة .
As for the fourth proof: It is that what is understood from inherence (al-ḥulūl) is the occurrence of the attribute in a location (ḥayyiz), following the occurrence of its substrate therein. But we have already refuted this interpretation in the chapter on the interpretation of substance and accident. And his statement: 'If we accept this meaning, then the inherence of one in the other would not be more appropriate than the reverse.' The answer to it is: We have shown that the statement 'This is not more appropriate than that' is a feeble, weak premise.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Mu'tazilite proof, which argues that inherence (ḥulūl) of an attribute in a substrate means that the attribute occupies the same location as the substrate. The author claims to have already refuted this definition of inherence in an earlier section on substance and accident. Then he addresses a specific argument: if we accept that meaning, then it is arbitrary to say that the essence inheres in the attribute rather than the attribute inheres in the essence. The author dismisses this as a weak argument, because the principle of 'not more appropriate than the reverse' is not a sound premise in this context.

Key Terms
الحلول
الحيز
الجوهر
العرض
مقدمة ضعيفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Strong and valid
B) Feeble and weak
C) The same as his own view
D) Irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the Mu'tazilite fourth proof by dismissing their definition of inherence as already refuted and rejecting a subsequent objection as based on a weak premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this passage, the Mu'tazilites built their argument on a definition of inherence that the author had already refuted. Are there assumptions in your own understanding of Islam that you have never critically examined because you assumed they were settled? What would it take for you to revisit them honestly?
?
The author dismisses a logical objection as based on a 'feeble, weak premise.' Have you ever dismissed someone's concern about your beliefs by labeling it weak without truly examining it? Will you commit to listening carefully to objections before judging their strength?
?
This lesson shows that even a small shift in defining a term can change an entire argument. In your personal convictions about God's attributes, are you relying on definitions that might be incomplete or incorrect? How can you ensure your understanding is built on sound premises?

Fifth Mu'tazilite Proof: Perfect Essence Needs No Attributes?
Fifth Mu'tazilite proof and Ash'ari response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that a perfect God needs no attributes and the Ash'ari response through the concept of 'maʿnā' (meaning).

Core Idea
A perfect essence does not need attributes, but it can cause them.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if God's essence is already perfect and self-sufficient, then attributes would be superfluous. The Ash'ari response, presented here, introduces the notion of 'maʿnā' (meaning) as an intermediate cause. This short passage encapsulates a key debate on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: the Mu'tazilite proof is stated, then the author's response begins with 'We say'.
The Arabic is concise; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite argument
The Mu'tazilites argue: if God's essence is perfect in divinity, then it has no need for attributes. Ask yourself: what does 'perfect' mean here? Why would attributes be seen as redundant?
Contrast this with the Ash'ari view that attributes are distinct from the essence.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author proposes an alternative: the essence necessitates a 'meaning' (maʿnā), and that meaning necessitates the attribute (such as knowing). Consider how this preserves divine perfection while still affirming attributes.
This response also applies to the sixth proof, indicating a pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الخامسة : وهي أن ذاته إن كانت كاملة في الإلهية فلا حاجة إلى الصفة . فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال تلك الذات المخصوصة أوجبت المعنى ؟ وذلك المعنى أوجب العالمية. وهذا هو بعينه الجواب عن الحجة السادسة
As for the fifth proof: It is that if His essence is perfect in divinity, then there is no need for the attribute. We say: Why is it not permissible to say that that specific essence necessitated the meaning, and that meaning necessitated the attribute of being knowing? And this is exactly the answer also to the sixth proof.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth Mu'tazilite proof, which argues that since God's essence is perfect and sufficient in itself, it has no need of attributes. The author responds by asking why it is not permissible to posit that the essence necessitates a meaning (maʻnā), and that meaning then necessitates the attribute of being knowing (al-ʻālamiyya). This preserves the perfection of the essence while still accounting for the attributes through intermediate means. The author notes that this same answer applies to the sixth proof as well.

Misconception: The author denies that the essence is perfect or that attributes are needed.
Correction: The author affirms the essence's perfection, but argues that attributes can arise from it via an intermediate meaning, so they are not superfluous.

Checkpoint
What does the author propose as the cause of the attribute of being knowing?
A) The essence directly
B) A meaning necessitated by the essence
C) A separate eternal entity
D) The attribute causes itself

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim that a perfect essence needs no attributes is answered by positing that the essence necessitates a meaning which then necessitates the attribute, preserving perfection while upholding attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
You claim to believe in God's attributes, but do you live as if His attribute of Justice is real even when life seems unfair? The Ash'ari proof shows attributes are not add-ons but flow from His perfect essence. Are you willing to let that truth transform your trust in Him during your next trial?

First Proof Negating God's Knowledge
First proof against God's knowledge with refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the flawed reasoning behind the first proof used to negate God's knowledge and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between sharing in effects and sharing in essences.

Core Idea
Sharing an effect does not require sharing an essence.

This lesson presents the first of several proofs used by those who deny God's knowledge. The argument attempts to show that if two knowledges relate to the same object, they must share a common essence, which would be problematic. The author responds by clarifying that sharing in the effect (the judgment) does not necessitate sharing in the essence of the effecter. This refutation exposes a logical flaw in the opponent's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that two knowledges attached to the same known object share the same judgment. What conclusion does the opponent draw from this?
The opponent implies that this shared judgment necessitates a shared essence.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author states that sharing in the effect does not necessitate sharing in the essence of the effecter. Why? Because things differing in essence can still share concomitant properties.
4
Reflect on the logical principle
Consider the principle: two things that differ in essence can still have some common effects or properties. How does this undermine the opponent's argument?
This shows that the opponent's inference is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الأولى من الوجوه التي تمسكوا بها في نفي علم الله : فتقول : العلمان إذا تعلقا بذلك المعلوم الواحد ، فقد اشتركا في ذلك الحكم ، والاشتراك في الأثر لا يقتضي الاشتراك في ماهية المؤثر ، لأن المختلفان في الماهية لا يمنع اشتراكهما في بعض اللوازم .
As for the first proof among the aspects they clung to in negating God's knowledge: It says: Two knowledges, when they are attached to that one known object, they have shared in that judgment. But sharing in the effect does not necessitate sharing in the essence of the effecter, because two things that differ in essence are not prevented from sharing in some concomitants.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents one of the proofs used by those who deny God's knowledge. The argument is that if two knowledges both relate to the same known object, they share the same judgment (ruling). This would imply a commonality in the essence of the knowledges. The author refutes this by stating that sharing in the effect (al-athar) does not require sharing in the essence (māhiyya) of the effecter. Two things with different essences can still share some concomitant properties (lawāzim). Therefore, the argument is invalid.

Key Terms
علم
معلوم
حكم
أثر
مؤثر
ماهية
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing an effect necessitates sharing in essence.
Correction: Sharing in the effect does not necessitate sharing in essence; differing essences can share concomitants.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does sharing in the effect not necessitate?
A) Sharing in the known object
B) Sharing in the essence
C) Sharing in the judgment
D) Sharing in the attribute

Key Takeaway
The opponent's argument fails because it incorrectly assumes that sharing an effect (judgment) requires sharing an essence (mahiyya), whereas distinct essences can share concomitant properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because God knows something, He must know it the way you do—through effort, time, or emotion? What does that assumption say about how you truly think of God? Will you today consciously affirm that His knowledge is beyond your comprehension, and repent from measuring Him by your own mind?
?
When you read the opponent's argument, did you find it persuasive even briefly? That shows how easily we can be swayed by logical fallacies when they flatter our own limitations. Ask yourself: In what area of your life are you subtly resisting God's transcendence because it challenges your comfort? What will you do to surrender that area to His absolute uniqueness?
?
The refutation hinges on the principle that different essences can share effects. This means you cannot judge God's nature by the effects you see. Where in your daily life do you judge God's character or intentions based only on what you experience? Write down one concrete situation where you will instead say, 'His essence is unlike anything, so I trust His wisdom even when I don't see the reason.'

Turning the Tables: The Second Proof Rebutted
Second proof rebutted and conclusion stated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author deflects the opponents' proof by showing it applies to their own position, and then states that the attachments (taʻalluqāt) are simply known objects (maʻlūmāt), not caused by attributes.

Core Idea
Your argument against me is actually an argument against you.

In this passage, Sanusi continues his defense against those who deny that God's knowledge is identical with His essence. Having presented his own proofs earlier, he now turns the tables by showing that the opponents' second and third proofs actually damage their own case. He then crisply states his final position: the so-called 'attachments' between God and the known are not caused by attributes but are simply known objects. This lesson walks through that clever dialectical move and the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, dialectical style: the author directly addresses the opponent.
2
Identify the dialectical move
The author says that whatever the opponent argues against him regarding knowledge is equally necessary against the opponent regarding attachments. This is a classic 'tu quoque' or turn-the-tables argument. Ask yourself: why would the opponent's argument apply to their own view?
The opponent likely also has a theory about how God relates to objects of knowledge.
3
Understand the dismissal and final position
The author then dismisses all these proofs as weak, and clarifies that they only apply to someone who posits attributes or meanings causing attachments. Since he does not hold that view, the proofs are irrelevant. His positive claim: attachments are known things (maʻlūmāt).
This is the author's settled position: God's knowledge is direct, not via attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية : فنقول : كل ما أوردتموه علينا في العلم فهو لازم عليكم في التعلقات. وهذا هو الجواب بعينه عن الحجة الثالثة فقد ظهر بهذه التنبيهات أن هذه الدلائل بأسرها ضعيفة . ثم نقول : إنها بأسرها إنما تتوجه على من يقول هذه التعلقات معللة بصفات أو بمعاني قائمة بالذات . ونحن لا نقول بذلك ، بل قد دللنا على أن هذه التعلقات معلومات
As for the second proof, we say: Everything that you have brought against us regarding knowledge is necessary upon you regarding the attachments (al-taʻalluqāt). And this is exactly the same answer to the third proof. So it has become clear from these indications that these proofs are all weak. Then we say: They are all directed at one who says that these attachments are caused by attributes or by meanings subsisting in the essence. But we do not say that; rather, we have demonstrated that these attachments are known things (maʻlūmāt).
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against God's knowledge and concludes the discussion. The author states that whatever arguments the opponents bring against his view regarding knowledge (ʻilm), those same arguments are necessary against their own view regarding 'attachments' (taʻalluqāt) — presumably the relations between God and known objects. He also notes that this answer applies to the third proof. After these points, the author concludes that all these proofs are weak. Furthermore, he clarifies that the arguments of the opponents only target those who claim that these attachments are caused by attributes or meanings that subsist in the essence. But the author disagrees with that; he has already shown that these attachments are known things (maʻlūmāt), i.e., God's knowledge is direct without an attribute. This is the final position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author denies the existence of attachments (taʻalluqāt) altogether.
Correction: The author does not deny attachments; he asserts they are known objects (maʻlūmāt), not caused by attributes, so the opponents' arguments against his view do not apply.

Checkpoint
According to the concluding statement, what has the author demonstrated about the attachments?
A) They are caused by attributes
B) They are caused by meanings
C) They are known things
D) They are impossible

Key Takeaway
The opponents' proofs against God's knowledge are turned back upon themselves, and the author's final position is that the attachments (taʻalluqāt) are known objects (maʻlūmāt), not features caused by separate attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue for a theological point, do you ever use a reasoning that could just as easily be turned back on your own position? Think of a specific instance. Will you examine your argument again or remain content with it?
?
The author says the opponents' proofs only target those who believe attachments are caused by attributes. In your own understanding of God's knowledge, do you inadvertently attribute a cause or intermediary to His knowing? If so, what does that imply about your reliance on causal frameworks rather than pure submission to His transcendence?
?
After reading this passage, you see that the author’s final position is that attachments are simply 'known things'—a simple, uncaused relation. Can you accept a point where theological analysis stops, or do you feel compelled to keep dissecting? What does that impulse reveal about your desire to control rather than submit?

Refuting the Opponents' Second and Third Arguments
Sanusi refutes the second and third arguments against divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author turns the opponents' own arguments against them regarding acquired attributes and the nature of distinct existents.

Core Idea
The opponents' arguments often apply to themselves.

Sanusi continues his refutation of arguments against divine attributes by addressing two more. He shows that the second argument about acquiring attributes can be flipped, and the third argument about two distinct ancient beings is ambiguous and unproven.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second and third arguments
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the author responds to each argument concisely.
The second argument is about the opponents' own acquisition of attributes like 'worldliness' and 'power'.
2
Identify the turning of the second argument
The author says whatever they say about the attribute (al-maʿnā), he can say about 'worldliness' and 'power'. This shows their critique applies to their own view.
3
Analyze the third argument's ambiguity
The third argument claims that if attributes are real, there would be two distinct ancient beings. The author distinguishes between two different realities (admitted) and two separable things (needs proof).
This clarifies what the argument actually proves.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice how the author does not reject the premises outright but shows they are incomplete or misdirected.
This is a common kalam technique: showing an argument works against the opponent as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية لهم : فنقول : إنهم محترفون بحصول العالمية والقادرية ، فكل ما تذكرونه في المعنى، فنحن نورده عليكم في العالمية والقادرية .
وأما الحجة الثالثة : وهو التزام قدماء متغايرة ، فإن عنيتم بالغيرين الحقيقتان المختلفتان . فهذا مسلم. وهو أول المسألة ، وإن عنيتم به الشيئين اللذين يجوز أن يفارق أحدهما الآخر، إما بمكان أو بزمان فلم قلتم : إن كل موجودين يجب أن يكونا كذلك ؟
وأما الحجة الرابعة : وهي أن المعقول من الحلول حصول الصفة في الحيز ، تبعاً لحصول محلها فيه . فقد أبطلنا هذا التفسير في باب تفسير الجوهر والعرض . وقوله : لو دفعنا بهذا المعنى لم يكن حلول أحدهما في الآخر أولى من العكس : فجوابه : إنا بينا : أن قول القائل ليس هذا أولى من ذلك مقدمة ركيكة ضعيفة .
وأما الحجة الخامسة : وهي أن ذاته إن كانت كاملة في الإلهية فلا حاجة إلى الصفة . فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال تلك الذات المخصوصة أوجبت المعنى ؟ وذلك المعنى أوجب العالمية. وهذا هو بعينه الجواب عن الحجة السادسة
وأما الحجة الأولى من الوجوه التي تمسكوا بها في نفي علم الله : فتقول : العلمان إذا تعلقا بذلك المعلوم الواحد ، فقد اشتركا في ذلك الحكم ، والاشتراك في الأثر لا يقتضي الاشتراك في ماهية المؤثر ، لأن المختلفان في الماهية لا يمنع اشتراكهما في بعض اللوازم .
وأما الحجة الثانية : فنقول : كل ما أوردتموه علينا في العلم فهو لازم عليكم في التعلقات. وهذا هو الجواب بعينه عن الحجة الثالثة فقد ظهر بهذه التنبيهات أن هذه الدلائل بأسرها ضعيفة . ثم نقول : إنها بأسرها إنما تتوجه على من يقول هذه التعلقات معللة بصفات أو بمعاني قائمة بالذات . ونحن لا نقول بذلك ، بل قد دللنا على أن هذه التعلقات معلومات زائلة على الذات . وعندنا : إنها معللة بنفس الذات ، وعند هذا يظهر أن شيئاً من هذه الدلائل لا يتوجه علينا البتة . وبالله التوفيق
As for the second argument of them: we say: they are engaged in acquiring 'worldliness' (al-ʿālamiyya) and 'power' (al-qādiriya). So whatever you mention regarding the attribute (al-maʿnā), we will bring it against you regarding worldliness and power. As for the third argument: it is the necessary implication of two distinct ancient beings. If by 'two others' you mean two different realities, this is admitted, and it is the first [point] of the issue. But if you mean two things each of which may separate from the other, either in place or time, then why do you say that every two existents must be such? As for the fourth argument: it is that the understood meaning of 'indwelling' (al-ḥulūl) is the attribute's occurrence in a locus, following the occurrence of its substrate in it. We have refuted this interpretation in the chapter on the interpretation of substance and accident. And his saying: if we were to refute in this sense, then the indwelling of one in the other would not be more appropriate than the reverse, its answer: we have explained that the statement 'this is not more appropriate than that' is a weak and flimsy premise. As for the fifth argument: it is that if His essence is perfect in divinity, then there is no need for the attribute. We say: why may it not be said that that specific essence necessitated the attribute (al-maʿnā), and that attribute necessitated omniscience? That is the very answer to the sixth argument. As for the first argument from the points they clung to in denying God's knowledge: they say: if two knowledges relate to that one known thing, then they share in that judgment, and sharing in the effect does not require sharing in the quiddity of the effecter, because two things different in quiddity are not prevented from sharing in some concomitants. As for the second argument: we say: everything you have brought against us regarding knowledge is incumbent upon you regarding the relations (al-taʿalluqāt). And this is the very answer to the third argument. So it has been made clear by these points that all these proofs are weak. Then we say: all of them only apply to one who says that these relations are caused by attributes or by entities subsisting in the essence. We do not say that; rather we have demonstrated that these relations are known and transient upon the essence. And in our view: they are caused by the essence itself. And with this, it becomes clear that none of these proofs applies to us at all. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of six arguments (second through sixth, then a first from another set) that opponents use to deny divine attributes. The author responds to each briefly and then gives a general conclusion that all these arguments only attack those who believe attributes are caused by separate entities subsisting in the essence, whereas the author's position is that these relations are caused directly by the essence itself. Therefore the arguments do not apply. The author shows that the second argument (about attributes being acquired) can be turned back on the opponents. The third argument about two distinct ancient beings is ambiguous: if it means distinct realities it's admitted, but if it means separable things the opponents must prove that all existents are separable. The fourth argument about indwelling is refuted by an earlier discussion, and the claim that the reverse is equally possible is dismissed as a weak premise. The fifth and sixth arguments about the sufficiency of the essence are answered by saying the essence may necessitate an attribute which then necessitates another attribute. For the second set, the first argument about two knowledges sharing a known object does not require them to have the same quiddity; different things can share effects. The second and third arguments are answered by pointing out that their own framework (relations) is subject to the same objections.

Key Terms
العالمية
القادرية
المعنى
الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the third argument definitively proves multiple eternal beings are impossible.
Correction: The argument only works if 'two distinct beings' means separable things, which is not proven for all pairs.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the second argument?
A) He rejects it outright.
B) He turns it back on the opponents.
C) He modifies it to support his own view.
D) He ignores it.

Key Takeaway
A good argument must not self-destruct, and ambiguous terms (like 'two others') must be clarified to assess validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever use arguments against others that, if honest, would also apply to yourself? Think of a recent disagreement. What would happen if you took your own reasoning and applied it to your own stance? Can you commit to testing your own positions today?
?
When you hear terms like 'two others' or 'multiple entities' in discussions about God, do you assume you know exactly what is meant? Or do you stop to clarify definitions like Sanusi does? Will you resolve from now on to always define key terms before arguing?
?
Sanusi turns the opponent's argument back on them. Is there a belief you hold that, if turned on you, would make you uncomfortable? Are you willing to face that possibility and re-examine your own position with humility?

Refuting Arguments from Indwelling and Essence Sufficiency
Sanusi refutes the fourth, fifth, and sixth arguments against divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's responses to arguments based on indwelling and the idea that a perfect essence needs no attributes.

Core Idea
The essence can necessitate attributes without being imperfect.

The next arguments challenge the very concept of attributes. The fourth claims indwelling is absurd, while the fifth and sixth say a perfect essence needs no attributes. Sanusi refutes these by redefining key terms and offering a causal chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for fourth, fifth, and sixth arguments
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the concise responses.
The fourth argument is about indwelling (al-ḥulūl).
2
Understand the refutation of the fourth argument
The author says they have already refuted the interpretation of indwelling as attribute's occurrence in a locus. He also dismisses the claim that 'this is not more appropriate than that' as a weak premise.
3
Analyze the response to the fifth and sixth arguments
The opponents say if essence is perfect, no need for attributes. The author answers that the specific essence may necessitate the attribute (al-maʿnā), and that attribute necessitates omniscience. This chain shows attributes are not superfluous.
The same answer applies to the sixth argument.
4
Reflect on the underlying model
The author implicitly accepts that attributes are real but caused by the essence, not independent. This distinguishes his view from those who say attributes are separate entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية لهم : فنقول : إنهم محترفون بحصول العالمية والقادرية ، فكل ما تذكرونه في المعنى، فنحن نورده عليكم في العالمية والقادرية .
وأما الحجة الثالثة : وهو التزام قدماء متغايرة ، فإن عنيتم بالغيرين الحقيقتان المختلفتان . فهذا مسلم. وهو أول المسألة ، وإن عنيتم به الشيئين اللذين يجوز أن يفارق أحدهما الآخر، إما بمكان أو بزمان فلم قلتم : إن كل موجودين يجب أن يكونا كذلك ؟
وأما الحجة الرابعة : وهي أن المعقول من الحلول حصول الصفة في الحيز ، تبعاً لحصول محلها فيه . فقد أبطلنا هذا التفسير في باب تفسير الجوهر والعرض . وقوله : لو دفعنا بهذا المعنى لم يكن حلول أحدهما في الآخر أولى من العكس : فجوابه : إنا بينا : أن قول القائل ليس هذا أولى من ذلك مقدمة ركيكة ضعيفة .
وأما الحجة الخامسة : وهي أن ذاته إن كانت كاملة في الإلهية فلا حاجة إلى الصفة . فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال تلك الذات المخصوصة أوجبت المعنى ؟ وذلك المعنى أوجب العالمية. وهذا هو بعينه الجواب عن الحجة السادسة
وأما الحجة الأولى من الوجوه التي تمسكوا بها في نفي علم الله : فتقول : العلمان إذا تعلقا بذلك المعلوم الواحد ، فقد اشتركا في ذلك الحكم ، والاشتراك في الأثر لا يقتضي الاشتراك في ماهية المؤثر ، لأن المختلفان في الماهية لا يمنع اشتراكهما في بعض اللوازم .
وأما الحجة الثانية : فنقول : كل ما أوردتموه علينا في العلم فهو لازم عليكم في التعلقات. وهذا هو الجواب بعينه عن الحجة الثالثة فقد ظهر بهذه التنبيهات أن هذه الدلائل بأسرها ضعيفة . ثم نقول : إنها بأسرها إنما تتوجه على من يقول هذه التعلقات معللة بصفات أو بمعاني قائمة بالذات . ونحن لا نقول بذلك ، بل قد دللنا على أن هذه التعلقات معلومات زائلة على الذات . وعندنا : إنها معللة بنفس الذات ، وعند هذا يظهر أن شيئاً من هذه الدلائل لا يتوجه علينا البتة . وبالله التوفيق
As for the second argument of them: we say: they are engaged in acquiring 'worldliness' (al-ʿālamiyya) and 'power' (al-qādiriya). So whatever you mention regarding the attribute (al-maʿnā), we will bring it against you regarding worldliness and power. As for the third argument: it is the necessary implication of two distinct ancient beings. If by 'two others' you mean two different realities, this is admitted, and it is the first [point] of the issue. But if you mean two things each of which may separate from the other, either in place or time, then why do you say that every two existents must be such? As for the fourth argument: it is that the understood meaning of 'indwelling' (al-ḥulūl) is the attribute's occurrence in a locus, following the occurrence of its substrate in it. We have refuted this interpretation in the chapter on the interpretation of substance and accident. And his saying: if we were to refute in this sense, then the indwelling of one in the other would not be more appropriate than the reverse, its answer: we have explained that the statement 'this is not more appropriate than that' is a weak and flimsy premise. As for the fifth argument: it is that if His essence is perfect in divinity, then there is no need for the attribute. We say: why may it not be said that that specific essence necessitated the attribute (al-maʿnā), and that attribute necessitated omniscience? That is the very answer to the sixth argument. As for the first argument from the points they clung to in denying God's knowledge: they say: if two knowledges relate to that one known thing, then they share in that judgment, and sharing in the effect does not require sharing in the quiddity of the effecter, because two things different in quiddity are not prevented from sharing in some concomitants. As for the second argument: we say: everything you have brought against us regarding knowledge is incumbent upon you regarding the relations (al-taʿalluqāt). And this is the very answer to the third argument. So it has been made clear by these points that all these proofs are weak. Then we say: all of them only apply to one who says that these relations are caused by attributes or by entities subsisting in the essence. We do not say that; rather we have demonstrated that these relations are known and transient upon the essence. And in our view: they are caused by the essence itself. And with this, it becomes clear that none of these proofs applies to us at all. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of six arguments (second through sixth, then a first from another set) that opponents use to deny divine attributes. The author responds to each briefly and then gives a general conclusion that all these arguments only attack those who believe attributes are caused by separate entities subsisting in the essence, whereas the author's position is that these relations are caused directly by the essence itself. Therefore the arguments do not apply. The author shows that the second argument (about attributes being acquired) can be turned back on the opponents. The third argument about two distinct ancient beings is ambiguous: if it means distinct realities it's admitted, but if it means separable things the opponents must prove that all existents are separable. The fourth argument about indwelling is refuted by an earlier discussion, and the claim that the reverse is equally possible is dismissed as a weak premise. The fifth and sixth arguments about the sufficiency of the essence are answered by saying the essence may necessitate an attribute which then necessitates another attribute. For the second set, the first argument about two knowledges sharing a known object does not require them to have the same quiddity; different things can share effects. The second and third arguments are answered by pointing out that their own framework (relations) is subject to the same objections.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a perfect essence cannot have any additional attributes without being imperfect.
Correction: The attributes can be necessary effects of the essence, not additions that imply deficiency.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the fifth argument?
A) He says the essence is not perfect.
B) He says the attribute is the same as the essence.
C) He says the specific essence may necessitate the attribute, which then necessitates omniscience.
D) He says the argument is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Attributes can be understood as necessitated by the essence, preserving divine perfection while affirming real attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God's attributes are just abstract names, distant from your daily life? After this lesson, how might seeing attributes as real, essential features of God change how you speak to Him in your prayers?
?
If God's essence is so perfect that it necessitates attributes like knowledge and power, do you treat your own good qualities (patience, honesty) as mere accidents you can discard, or as necessities of who you are becoming? What would it look like to let your character be necessitated by your deepest beliefs?
?
The opponent argued that a perfect being has no 'need' for attributes, as if need is a sign of lack. But the author shows the essence necessitates attributes not out of need but out of richness. Where in your life have you confused richness of being with deficiency? Will you let this truth reshape how you see your own capacities?

Refuting Arguments Against Divine Knowledge and Final Conclusion
Sanusi refutes arguments against divine knowledge and concludes the entire set of arguments is inapplicable to his position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author counters arguments about multiple knowledges and how the entire set of arguments only targets a position he rejects.

Core Idea
The flaws in these arguments disappear when attributes are seen as relations from the essence.

The final arguments challenge divine knowledge, claiming that multiple knowledges sharing a known object would imply something about their nature. Sanusi shows they don't, and then concludes that all arguments only attack a view he does not hold, exonerating his own position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the knowledge arguments and conclusion
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author responds to the first, second, and third arguments about knowledge.
The first argument is about two knowledges relating to one known object.
2
Understand the response to the first argument
The opponent says if two knowledges share a known object, they share that judgment, implying they are similar. The author says sharing an effect does not require sharing quiddity; different things can share some concomitants.
3
See the responses to second and third arguments
The author says everything the opponent says about knowledge, he can say about the relations (taʿalluqāt). This is the same answer for the third argument.
4
Grasp the general conclusion
The author states that all these arguments only apply to those who believe relations are caused by attributes or entities subsisting in the essence. He does not hold that view; his view is that relations are caused by the essence directly, making the arguments irrelevant.
This is the key to the whole passage.

وأما الحجة الثالثة : وهو التزام قدماء متغايرة ، فإن عنيتم بالغيرين الحقيقتان المختلفتان . فهذا مسلم. وهو أول المسألة ، وإن عنيتم به الشيئين اللذين يجوز أن يفارق أحدهما الآخر، إما بمكان أو بزمان فلم قلتم : إن كل موجودين يجب أن يكونا كذلك ؟

Divine Attributes

The Essence as the Direct Cause of Divine Attributes
Sanusi defines power and knowledge as possible attributes and argues that their cause must be the essence itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine attributes of power and knowledge, being possible, must have their cause in the divine essence itself, and why this cause cannot be something other than the essence.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are possible realities necessitated directly by the essence.

Sanusi begins by clarifying what we mean when we say God is 'powerful' (qādir) and 'knowing' (ʿālim). These are not self-standing entities but specific relations or properties that depend on a cause. The following passage explains why that cause cannot be anything other than the divine essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of power and knowledge
Sanusi defines power as the validity to create or abandon, and knowledge as a specific relation. Note that these are not independent substances.
They are attributed to the essence.
2
Recognize that these attributes are possible (mumkin)
Because they are not self-subsistent, they must be possible in themselves and thus require a cause.
3
Identify the cause as the essence itself
Sanusi shows that if the cause were something else, the essence would be dependent, contradicting its necessity. Therefore, the essence must be the cause.
This is a key argument against other possible causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني : اعلم أنا قد ذكرنا أن المراد من القادرية كونه بحيث يصح منه الإيجاد والترك. والمراد من العالمية هذه النسبة المخصوصة والإضافة المخصوصة فنقول من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن هذه الصحة المخصوصة ، وهذه النسبة المخصوصة لا تكون ذوات قائمة بأنفسها مستقلة بحقائقها ، فهي لا بد وأن تكون ممكنة لذواتها ، وكل ممكن فلا بد له من سبب . وذلك السبب إما تلك الذات أو غيرها . والأول باطل وإلا لكانت تلك الذات مفتقرة في لوازمها إلى غيرها ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، فواجب الوجود لذاته ممكن لذاته فيثبت أن الموجب لهذه الأحكام والنسب هو ذاته المخصوصة ، إلا أنه بقي ههنا بحث آخر ، وهو : أن اللوازم على قسمين لوازم بغير وسط ولوازم بوسط . فهذه العالمية والقادرية لا يبعد أن يكون موجبها هو عين ذات الله ، ولا يبعد أيضاً أن يقال : إن ذات الله تعالى توجب أمراً ، وذلك الأمر يوجب هذه العالمية والقادرية ، سواء قلنا : تلك الواسطة واحدة ، أو وسائط كثيرة ، فكل واحد من الوجهين محتمل. إلا أنا نقول : لما كان لا بد من الاعتراف بكون تلك الذات المخصوصة موجبة لهذه النسب والإضافات . إما بواسطة وإما بغير واسطة ، وكانت الواسطة مجهولة وجب على سبيل الأولى والأخلق حذف هذه الواسطة من البين. والاعتراف بكون الذات المخصوصة موجبة لها . والذي يقرر ذلك : أنا إذا استدللنا بحدوث العالم على الفاعل المختار . فإذا قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : بأن الفاعل المختار معلول علة موجبة بالذات ؟ قلنا : لما : وجب الاعتراف بوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وبوجود الفاعل المختار ، كان الأولى أن نقول : ذلك الفاعل المختار هو ذلك الموجود الواجب لذاته ، وحذفنا الوسائط من البين. فلما قلنا هذا في المعلولات المباينة عن ذات الله تعالى ، وجب أيضاً أن نقوله ونعقله في الصفات القائمة بذات الله .
واحتج القائلون بإثبات هذه المعاني بوجوه :
الأول : وهو أن العلم في الشاهد صفة متعلقة بالمعنى ، فيجب أن يكون
The second station: Know that we have mentioned that the intended meaning of power (al-qādiriya) is that He is such that it is valid for Him to create and abandon. And the intended meaning of omniscience (al-ʿālamiyya) is this specific relation and specific addition. Then we say: it is known by necessity that this specific validity and this specific relation are not self-subsistent essences independent in their realities; they must be possible in themselves, and every possible thing must have a cause. That cause is either that essence or something else. The first is false, for otherwise that essence would be dependent in its concomitants upon something else, and the dependent on another is possible in itself, so the Necessary Existence in itself would be possible in itself. Thus it is established that that which necessitates these judgments and relations is His specific essence. However, there remains another inquiry here, which is: that concomitants are of two kinds: concomitants without an intermediary and concomitants with an intermediary. So these omniscience and power, it is not unlikely that their necessitator is the very essence of God, and it is also not unlikely that it be said that the essence of God, exalted, necessitates a thing, and that thing necessitates this omniscience and power, whether we say that intermediary is one or many intermediaries; each of the two aspects is possible. Except that we say: since it is necessary to acknowledge that that specific essence necessitates these relations and additions, either with an intermediary or without, and the intermediary is unknown, it is obligatory, in the first and most proper manner, to drop this intermediary from between and acknowledge that the specific essence necessitates them. And that which establishes this: when we argue by the temporal origination of the world for the free agent, and it is said: why may it not be said that the free agent is an effect of a necessitating cause essentially? We say: since acknowledgment is necessary of the existence of a being Necessary Existence in itself, and of the existence of the free agent, it is more appropriate that we say: that free agent is that being Necessary in itself, and we drop the intermediaries. So when we said this regarding effects separate from the essence of God, exalted, it is also necessary that we say and conceive it regarding the attributes subsisting in God's essence. And those who affirm these attributes (al-maʿānī) argued by proofs: First: that knowledge in the witnessed world is an attribute related to the attribute (al-maʿnā), so it must be...
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the 'second station' where the author explains the nature of divine attributes (knowledge and power) as specific relations that are not self-subsistent but possible. Since they are possible, they require a cause. The cause cannot be something other than the essence because that would make the essence dependent, leading to a contradiction (the Necessary would be possible). Therefore the essence must be the cause. However, there remains a question whether the causation is direct or through an intermediary. The author argues that since we must acknowledge the essence as the cause and the intermediary is unknown, it is more proper to eliminate the intermediary. He draws an analogy from the proof of the Creator from the world's temporal origination: it is more appropriate to identify the free agent directly with the Necessary Existence rather than postulating intermediate causes. Similarly, for attributes inherent in God, we should say the essence directly necessitates them. The passage then begins to present the arguments of those who affirm real attributes (maʿānī) with the first argument: knowledge in the contingent realm is an attribute related to a meaning, so for God it must also be so (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
القادرية
العالمية
الممكن
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine attributes like knowledge and power are independent realities that do not require a cause.
Correction: Sanusi shows they are not self-subsistent and must be possible, thus requiring a cause, which cannot be other than the essence.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the possibility that the cause of the attributes is something other than the divine essence?
A) Because that other cause would be a created being.
B) Because that would make the essence dependent on another, leading to the Necessary being possible.
C) Because the attributes are self-subsistent and need no cause.
D) Because the essence cannot be a cause at all.

Key Takeaway
The divine essence must be the direct cause of its attributes to avoid contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the divine essence were dependent on something else for its attributes, what would that mean for your trust in God's absolute self-sufficiency? Would your worship be directed to a God who needs others?
?
You claim to believe that God's power and knowledge are inherent. But do you live as though His power is limited by your circumstances or that His knowledge is conditioned by your actions? What would change if you truly internalized that His attributes are necessary and uncaused?
?
The argument shows that God's essence is the sole cause of His attributes. Does your heart acknowledge that He is the source of all perfection, or do you secretly attribute His qualities to other causes? Take a moment to repent of any hidden shirk in your understanding of His attributes.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
7 min
Direct vs. Mediated Causation of Attributes
Sanusi discusses whether divine attributes are necessitated directly by the essence or through an intermediary, and argues for direct causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi argues that divine attributes are directly necessitated by the essence without any intermediary, using the analogy from the proof of God from creation.

Core Idea
When the intermediary is unknown, it is more proper to drop it.

Having established that the essence is the cause of the attributes, Sanusi now addresses whether this causation is direct or through some intermediary. He argues that since any intermediary is unknown, it is preferable to affirm direct causation. He draws a parallel with the argument for God's existence from the world's temporal origination.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two kinds of concomitants
Sanusi divides concomitants into those with and without an intermediary. Both are possible in this context.
2
Consider the possibility of an intermediary
He admits that it is possible that the essence necessitates an intermediary which then necessitates the attributes, but the intermediary is unknown.
3
Apply the rule of dropping the unknown intermediary
Since the intermediary is unknown and we must acknowledge the essence as the cause, it is better to drop the intermediary and affirm direct causation.
This is based on the principle of 'first and more proper'.
4
Learn from the analogy with the proof of God
Just as in proving God's existence, we identify the free agent directly with the Necessary Existence, dropping intermediaries, similarly for attributes.
This establishes consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني : اعلم أنا قد ذكرنا أن المراد من القادرية كونه بحيث يصح منه الإيجاد والترك. والمراد من العالمية هذه النسبة المخصوصة والإضافة المخصوصة فنقول من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن هذه الصحة المخصوصة ، وهذه النسبة المخصوصة لا تكون ذوات قائمة بأنفسها مستقلة بحقائقها ، فهي لا بد وأن تكون ممكنة لذواتها ، وكل ممكن فلا بد له من سبب . وذلك السبب إما تلك الذات أو غيرها . والأول باطل وإلا لكانت تلك الذات مفتقرة في لوازمها إلى غيرها ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، فواجب الوجود لذاته ممكن لذاته فيثبت أن الموجب لهذه الأحكام والنسب هو ذاته المخصوصة ، إلا أنه بقي ههنا بحث آخر ، وهو : أن اللوازم على قسمين لوازم بغير وسط ولوازم بوسط . فهذه العالمية والقادرية لا يبعد أن يكون موجبها هو عين ذات الله ، ولا يبعد أيضاً أن يقال : إن ذات الله تعالى توجب أمراً ، وذلك الأمر يوجب هذه العالمية والقادرية ، سواء قلنا : تلك الواسطة واحدة ، أو وسائط كثيرة ، فكل واحد من الوجهين محتمل. إلا أنا نقول : لما كان لا بد من الاعتراف بكون تلك الذات المخصوصة موجبة لهذه النسب والإضافات . إما بواسطة وإما بغير واسطة ، وكانت الواسطة مجهولة وجب على سبيل الأولى والأخلق حذف هذه الواسطة من البين. والاعتراف بكون الذات المخصوصة موجبة لها . والذي يقرر ذلك : أنا إذا استدللنا بحدوث العالم على الفاعل المختار . فإذا قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : بأن الفاعل المختار معلول علة موجبة بالذات ؟ قلنا : لما : وجب الاعتراف بوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وبوجود الفاعل المختار ، كان الأولى أن نقول : ذلك الفاعل المختار هو ذلك الموجود الواجب لذاته ، وحذفنا الوسائط من البين. فلما قلنا هذا في المعلولات المباينة عن ذات الله تعالى ، وجب أيضاً أن نقوله ونعقله في الصفات القائمة بذات الله .
واحتج القائلون بإثبات هذه المعاني بوجوه :
الأول : وهو أن العلم في الشاهد صفة متعلقة بالمعنى ، فيجب أن يكون
The second station: Know that we have mentioned that the intended meaning of power (al-qādiriya) is that He is such that it is valid for Him to create and abandon. And the intended meaning of omniscience (al-ʿālamiyya) is this specific relation and specific addition. Then we say: it is known by necessity that this specific validity and this specific relation are not self-subsistent essences independent in their realities; they must be possible in themselves, and every possible thing must have a cause. That cause is either that essence or something else. The first is false, for otherwise that essence would be dependent in its concomitants upon something else, and the dependent on another is possible in itself, so the Necessary Existence in itself would be possible in itself. Thus it is established that that which necessitates these judgments and relations is His specific essence. However, there remains another inquiry here, which is: that concomitants are of two kinds: concomitants without an intermediary and concomitants with an intermediary. So these omniscience and power, it is not unlikely that their necessitator is the very essence of God, and it is also not unlikely that it be said that the essence of God, exalted, necessitates a thing, and that thing necessitates this omniscience and power, whether we say that intermediary is one or many intermediaries; each of the two aspects is possible. Except that we say: since it is necessary to acknowledge that that specific essence necessitates these relations and additions, either with an intermediary or without, and the intermediary is unknown, it is obligatory, in the first and most proper manner, to drop this intermediary from between and acknowledge that the specific essence necessitates them. And that which establishes this: when we argue by the temporal origination of the world for the free agent, and it is said: why may it not be said that the free agent is an effect of a necessitating cause essentially? We say: since acknowledgment is necessary of the existence of a being Necessary Existence in itself, and of the existence of the free agent, it is more appropriate that we say: that free agent is that being Necessary in itself, and we drop the intermediaries. So when we said this regarding effects separate from the essence of God, exalted, it is also necessary that we say and conceive it regarding the attributes subsisting in God's essence. And those who affirm these attributes (al-maʿānī) argued by proofs: First: that knowledge in the witnessed world is an attribute related to the attribute (al-maʿnā), so it must be...
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the 'second station' where the author explains the nature of divine attributes (knowledge and power) as specific relations that are not self-subsistent but possible. Since they are possible, they require a cause. The cause cannot be something other than the essence because that would make the essence dependent, leading to a contradiction (the Necessary would be possible). Therefore the essence must be the cause. However, there remains a question whether the causation is direct or through an intermediary. The author argues that since we must acknowledge the essence as the cause and the intermediary is unknown, it is more proper to eliminate the intermediary. He draws an analogy from the proof of the Creator from the world's temporal origination: it is more appropriate to identify the free agent directly with the Necessary Existence rather than postulating intermediate causes. Similarly, for attributes inherent in God, we should say the essence directly necessitates them. The passage then begins to present the arguments of those who affirm real attributes (maʿānī) with the first argument: knowledge in the contingent realm is an attribute related to a meaning, so for God it must also be so (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
الواسطة
اللوازم
بغير وسط
بوسط
الفاعل المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's attributes are caused through some intermediate being.
Correction: Sanusi argues that such an intermediary is unknown and unnecessary, so direct causation is preferred.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Sanusi gives for dropping the intermediary in the causation of attributes?
A) The intermediary is impossible.
B) The intermediary is unknown and it is more proper to drop it.
C) The intermediary would be a created being.
D) The attributes do not need any cause.

Key Takeaway
When the essence must be the cause and any intermediary is unknown, direct causation is the correct position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi argues that when an intermediary is unknown, it is more proper to drop it. In your own life, do you tend to attribute blessings or trials to secondary causes (people, luck, circumstances) rather than directly to Allah? How can you train yourself to see His direct hand more clearly, even when means are visible?
?
The lesson draws an analogy between the proof of God's existence and the attributes: consistency demands dropping unknown intermediaries in both. Is there any area of your faith where you tolerate logical inconsistency because admitting consistency would require you to change a comfortable belief? What would it cost you to resolve it?
?
If you truly internalized that Allah's attributes are necessitated directly by His essence without any intermediary, how would that change the way you think about His knowledge and power being immediate and always present? Does your daily awareness reflect this directness, or do you still feel a distance?

The Contingency of Divine Power and Knowledge
Defines qudra and 'ilm and argues for their direct causation by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that divine power and knowledge are contingent relations necessitated directly by God's essence.

Core Idea
Attributes are contingent relations, not self-subsistent realities.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah lays the groundwork for understanding God's attributes. The author defines 'power' and 'knowledge' as particular possibilities and relations. He then argues that these cannot be independent; they are contingent and require a cause. Through logical deduction, he proves that the cause must be God's essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Define qudra and 'ilm
Read the definitions: qudra is the possibility to create or leave, 'ilm is a particular relation. These are not self-subsistent.
2
Understand the contingency claim
The author states these attributes are possible in themselves, meaning they need a cause.
3
Follow the causal reasoning
The cause is either the essence or something else. If something else, the essence would be dependent, making the Necessary Being contingent — impossible.
4
Conclude direct causation
Therefore, the essence itself necessitates these attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني : اعلم أنا قد ذكرنا أن المراد من القادرية كونه بحيث يصح منه الإيجاد والترك. والمراد من العالمية هذه النسبة المخصوصة والإضافة المخصوصة فنقول من المعلوم بالضرورة : أن هذه الصحة المخصوصة ، وهذه النسبة المخصوصة لا تكون ذوات قائمة بأنفسها مستقلة بحقائقها ، فهي لا بد وأن تكون ممكنة لذواتها ، وكل ممكن فلا بد له من سبب . وذلك السبب إما تلك الذات أو غيرها . والأول باطل وإلا لكانت تلك الذات مفتقرة في لوازمها إلى غيرها ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، فواجب الوجود لذاته ممكن لذاته فيثبت أن الموجب لهذه الأحكام والنسب هو ذاته المخصوصة ، إلا أنه بقي ههنا بحث آخر ، وهو : أن اللوازم على قسمين لوازم بغير وسط ولوازم بوسط . فهذه العالمية والقادرية لا يبعد أن يكون موجبها هو عين ذات الله ، ولا يبعد أيضاً أن يقال : إن ذات الله تعالى توجب أمراً ، وذلك الأمر يوجب هذه العالمية والقادرية ، سواء قلنا : تلك الواسطة واحدة ، أو وسائط كثيرة ، فكل واحد من الوجهين محتمل. إلا أنا نقول : لما كان لا بد من الاعتراف بكون تلك الذات المخصوصة موجبة لهذه النسب والإضافات . إما بواسطة وإما بغير واسطة ، وكانت الواسطة مجهولة وجب على سبيل الأولى والأخلق حذف هذه الواسطة من البين. والاعتراف بكون الذات المخصوصة موجبة لها . والذي يقرر ذلك : أنا إذا استدللنا بحدوث العالم على الفاعل المختار . فإذا قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : بأن الفاعل المختار معلول علة موجبة بالذات ؟ قلنا : لما : وجب الاعتراف بوجود موجود واجب الوجود لذاته ، وبوجود الفاعل المختار ، كان الأولى أن نقول : ذلك الفاعل المختار هو ذلك الموجود الواجب لذاته ، وحذفنا الوسائط من البين. فلما قلنا هذا في المعلولات المباينة عن ذات الله تعالى ، وجب أيضاً أن نقوله ونعقله في الصفات القائمة بذات الله .
The second station: Know that we have mentioned that what is meant by 'being powerful' (al-qadiriyya) is that it be such that bringing into existence and leaving are possible from it. And what is meant by 'being knowing' (al-'alamiyya) is this particular relation and particular addition. So we say: It is known necessarily that this particular possibility and this particular relation are not essences subsisting by themselves, independent in their realities; so they must be possible in themselves, and everything possible must have a cause. That cause is either that essence or something else. The first is false, otherwise that essence would be dependent in its concomitants on something else, and the dependent on another is possible in itself, so the Necessary of Existence in itself would be possible in itself. So it is established that the necessitator of these judgments and relations is His particular essence. However, there remains here another investigation: that concomitants (al-lawazim) are of two types: concomitants without an intermediate and concomitants with an intermediate. So for this 'being knowing' and 'being powerful', it is not unlikely that their necessitator be the very essence of God, and it is also not unlikely that it be said: The essence of God, exalted, necessitates an affair, and that affair necessitates this 'being knowing' and 'being powerful', whether we say that intermediate is one or many intermediates; each of the two possibilities is conceivable. Except that we say: Since it is necessary to acknowledge that that particular essence is the necessitator of these relations and additions, either with an intermediate or without an intermediate, and the intermediate is unknown, it is incumbent by way of priority and appropriateness to drop this intermediate from between, and acknowledge that the particular essence is their necessitator. And what establishes that is: When we prove by the originatedness of the world the free agent, and if it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the free agent is an effect of a cause necessary by essence? We say: Since acknowledgment is necessary of the existence of an existent necessary of existence in itself, and of the existence of the free agent, it is more appropriate to say: that free agent is that existent necessary in itself, and we drop the intermediates from between. So when we have said this concerning effects separate from God's essence, it is also incumbent that we say and reason it concerning the attributes subsisting in God's essence.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument. It begins by defining qudra (power) as the possibility of bringing into existence and leaving, and 'ilm (knowledge) as a particular relation and addition. The author then argues that these (the possibility and the relation) cannot be self-subsistent essences; they are contingent (possible in themselves) and thus need a cause. The cause could be the essence itself or something else. If it were something else, the essence would be dependent in its concomitants, making the Necessary of Existence contingent, which is impossible. Therefore, the cause is the essence itself. Then a further investigation: concomitants can be direct or via an intermediate. Both are possible, but since the intermediate would be unknown, it is better to drop it and attribute the relationship directly to the essence. He supports this with an analogy from proving the free agent through the origination of the world: we prefer to identify the free agent with the Necessary Being directly, without intermediates. Similarly, for attributes subsisting in God's essence, we should attribute them directly to the essence without postulating intermediate causes.

عالمية
نسبة
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine attributes are eternal self-subsisting entities like the essence.
Correction: The passage shows they are contingent relations that depend on the essence for their existence.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why must the attributes (qudra and 'ilm) have a cause?
A) Because they are necessary in themselves.
B) Because they are possible in themselves and every possible needs a cause.
C) Because they are created by God.
D) Because they are accidents in the essence.

Key Takeaway
Divine power and knowledge are contingent relations directly caused by God's essence, not independent entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power and knowledge are contingent relations caused by His essence, what does that say about your own attributes? Are they independent or are they also contingent on something? How does this passage dismantle any delusion of self-sufficiency you might harbor?
?
When you worship God, do you ever treat His attributes as if they are separate entities—like 'Power' and 'Knowledge' are things He 'has' rather than what He 'is'? How does this passage challenge you to unify your understanding of God?
?
The argument shows that if attributes were caused by something other than God's essence, then God would be dependent. How does this realization expose areas in your life where you still depend on creatures as ultimate causes? What concrete step can you take today to redirect that dependence solely to God?

Direct Causation vs. Intermediate Causes for Divine Attributes
Discusses direct vs. mediated causation of attributes and argues for direct attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is preferable to attribute divine attributes directly to God's essence without postulating intermediate causes.

Core Idea
Drop unknown intermediates; attribute directly to essence.

The author has already established that the essence is the cause of the attributes. Now he considers a further question: could there be intermediate causes between the essence and the attributes? He explains that both direct and indirect causation are logically possible, but since any intermediate would be unknown, it is more appropriate to drop it. He supports this with an analogy from the proof of the free agent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of concomitants
Concomitants can be without an intermediate or with an intermediate.
2
Consider both possibilities
The attributes could be caused directly by essence or via an intermediate. Both are logically possible.
3
Apply the principle of dropping unknowns
Since any intermediate would be unknown, it is better to drop it and attribute directly.
4
Analogous argument from the free agent
Just as we identify the free agent with the Necessary Being without intermediates, we should also do so for attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

The author says dropping the unknown is 'more appropriate' (أولى وأخلق). Is there an area of your faith where you are holding onto something you don't understand, afraid to let it go? How can you apply this principle of 'first and more suitable' to your own relationship with God?
?
If you truly believed that God is exactly as He describes Himself without needing extra explanations, how would that change the way you speak about Him, worship Him, or think about His actions in your life? What is one thing you can do today to remove an unnecessary 'intermediate' in your understanding of God?

Three Arguments for Attributes as Real Meanings
Presents three classical kalam proofs for divine attributes as distinct real entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical kalam arguments used to prove that God's attributes are real, distinct entities rather than mere relations or mental concepts.

Core Idea
God's attributes are real, not just mental—proven by analogy, origination, and relation.

Theologians who affirm that divine attributes are real meanings (ma'ani) distinct from God's essence rely on several rational proofs. This lesson presents three key arguments drawn from the witnessed world to show that attributes like knowledge are real existent qualities. Each argument refutes the idea that attributes are mere relations or aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to get an overview of the three arguments. Notice how each one builds on a different starting point.
The arguments are numbered in the text.
2
Understand the first proof: analogy
Focus on the first argument from analogy (shahid-gha'ib). Understand why the similarity of quiddity allows reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen.
3
Analyze the second proof: origination
The second argument focuses on the temporal origination of 'being knowing' in the witnessed. Consider why it implies knowledge is an existent attribute.
This argument uses the impossibility of the body's essence being related to the known.
4
Examine the third proof: relation
The third argument looks at the nature of knowledge as having a relation to the known. Understand why the essence itself cannot be the relatum.
5
Reflect on the common thread
Notice that all three arguments conclude that attributes are real entities separate from the essence, rejecting the notion of mere relational accidents.
This is a key point in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بإثبات هذه المعاني بوجوه : الأول : وهو أن العلم في الشاهد صفة متعلقة بالمعنى ، فيجب أن يكون في الغالب كذلك ، لأن الحقيقة والماهية لا تختلف بسبب اختلاف الشاهد والغالب . والثاني : إن العالمية حصلت بعد أن لم تكن في الشاهد ، فوجب كون العلم صفة موجودة ، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن الذي حدث هو مجرد النسب والإضافات لأن ذات الجسم لا يمكن أن تكون متعلقة بالمعلوم ، فوجب أن يكون الحادث صفة يحصل لها هذا التعلق . والثالث : إن العلم ما له تعلق بالمعلوم ، فلو كانت الذات متعلقة بالمعلوم لزم أن تكون ذات الله علماً ، وذلك باطل
And those who affirm these [real] meanings argued with arguments: The first: that knowledge in the witnessed realm (al-shahid) is an attribute related to a meaning, so it must be likewise in the unseen (al-gha'ib) because the reality and quiddity do not differ due to the difference between the witnessed and the unseen. The second: that 'being knowing' occurs after it was not in the witnessed realm, so it is necessary that knowledge be an existent attribute, and it cannot be said that what occurred is merely relations and additions because the essence of the body cannot be related to the known, so it must be that the occurent is an attribute by which this relation is acquired. The third: that knowledge is that which has a relation to the known; if the essence were related to the known, then it would follow that God's essence is knowledge, and that is false.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs (wujuh) used by those who affirm the attributes as real, distinct meanings (ma'ani) rather than mere relations. The first proof is the analogy between the witnessed (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib): in the witnessed world, knowledge is an attribute related to a meaning, so it must be likewise in the unseen, because realities do not change with context. The second proof argues from origination: in the witnessed realm, knowing occurs after not knowing, which implies that knowledge is an existent attribute that comes to be, not merely a relation, because the essence of a body cannot be related to the known (since relation would require the essence itself to be knowledge, which is false). Therefore, what occurs is an attribute that enables the relation. The third proof: knowledge is that which has a relation to the known. If the essence itself were related, then the essence would be knowledge, which is false; therefore, there must be a distinct attribute that is knowledge. These are classical arguments for the reality of attributes in kalam.

Correction: The passage provides three rational arguments to show that attributes like knowledge must be real, existent qualities distinct from the essence.

Checkpoint

B) Because the occurent cannot be mere relations, as the body's essence cannot relate.
C) Because knowledge is eternal.
D) Because the unseen is different.

Key Takeaway
Through analogy, origination, and relation arguments, theologians establish that divine attributes are real, distinct entities.
