The author denies any name that indicates a part of essence. This means you cannot reduce Allah's attributes to pieces. How often do you find yourself defining Allah by what He does or has rather than by what He is? Today, let this lesson remind you that Allah's attributes are not parts added to His essence; they are His very reality in a way we cannot fully grasp. Will you stop trying to force Allah into your mental categories and instead submit to the mystery of His oneness?

Refutation of the Claim that the Divine Essence Has No Name
Author refutes the philosophers' claim by presenting multiple plausible counter-arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that the divine essence is unknowable and nameless is not rationally conclusive, and how the author defends the possibility of special knowledge and naming.

Core Idea
The divine reality can be known through God's grace, even if human reason alone cannot grasp it.

The philosophers argued that since the divine reality is beyond human reason, it has no name. But is that claim proven? In this passage, the author dismantles their certainty by showing multiple plausible avenues for knowledge and naming. You'll see how the argument rests on lack of proof, not impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' position
Read the opening statement: they claim the divine reality is imperceptible and therefore nameless.
Notice their concession that it is necessary of existence.
2
Follow the first rebuttal
The author states that impossibility has not been proven, and God may grant knowledge to some servants.
This undermines the philosophers' certainty.
3
Examine the subsequent possibilities
Consider the additional arguments: angelic knowledge, a divinely taught name, and spiritual benefits of mentioning it.
Each possibility further weakens the philosophers' claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعبر قدماء الفلاسفة عن هذا المعنى فقالوا : إنها حقيقة لا تدركها عقول الخلق ، فلا إسم لها ، إلا أن نشرح الحقيقة بأنها واجبة الوجود لذاتها . ولمجيب أن يجيب فيقول : الذي ثبت عندنا : أنا لا نعرف تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة . وأما أنه يمتنع عقلا عرفاتها ، فذلك لم يثبت بالدليل البتة . وبتقدير أن يكون ذلك ممتنعاً لم يبعد أن يشرف الحق ـ سبحانه ـ بعض عباده بتلك المعرفة . وحينئذ يكون وضع الاسم منتفعاً به ، وأيضاً : فهب أن العقول البشرية تمتنع عليها ( هذه المعرفة إلا أنه لم يثبت أن الأرواح القدسية الملكية يمتنع عليها ) (١) ذلك . وأيضاً : فهب أن العقول البشرية والأرواح الملكية يمتنع عليها تحصيل هذا العرفان ، إلا أنه لا يبعد أن يكون لتلك الحقيقة المخصوصة إسم معين . ثم إنه تعالى يشرف بعض عبيده بتعريف ذلك الاسم ، ولا يبعد أن يكون ذكر ذلك الاسم ، سبباً ، لأن تنقاد لذكره الأرواح المطهرة المقدسة ، ولا يبعد أيضاً : أن يحصل لذاكره ، قوة في الروح ، لا تحصل إلا بذلك الطريق . فكل هذه الوجوه محتملة .
And the ancient philosophers expressed this meaning and said: It is a reality that the intellects of creation do not perceive, so it has no name, except that we explain the reality as being necessary of existence in itself. And one may answer and say: What is established with us is that we do not know that specific reality. As for it being rationally impossible to know it, that has not been established by any proof at all. And even if that were impossible, it is not far-fetched that the Truth—glorified be He—should honor some of His servants with that knowledge. Then the coining of a name would be beneficial. Also: Suppose that human intellects are prevented from (this knowledge, but it has not been established that the holy angelic spirits are prevented from) (1) that. Also: Suppose that human intellects and angelic spirits are prevented from obtaining this recognition, yet it is not far-fetched that that specific reality has a specific name. Then the Exalted honors some of His servants by teaching that name, and it is not far-fetched that mentioning that name causes the purified holy spirits to submit to its mention, and it is not far-fetched also that the one who mentions it obtains a strength in spirit that is not obtained except by that means. All these possibilities are plausible.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (and then elaboration). The author begins by quoting the ancient philosophers: they claim that the divine reality (al-haqiqa) is not perceived by created intellects, and therefore it has no name. They then explain that the reality is that God is necessary of existence in Himself. The author then provides a response from the perspective of one who answers. He says: what is established for us is that we do not know that specific reality. However, the claim that it is rationally impossible to know it has never been proven by any proof. Even if it were impossible for human intellects, it is not far-fetched that God might honor some of His servants with that knowledge, in which case naming would be beneficial. Furthermore, even if human intellects are prevented, it has not been proven that holy angelic spirits are prevented. Even if both are prevented, it is not far-fetched that there is a specific name for that reality, and God might teach that name to some of His servants. Mentioning that name could cause purified spirits to submit and give the speaker a spiritual strength not attainable otherwise. The author concludes that all these possibilities are plausible. Thus, the philosophers' claim is not decisive.

Key Terms

واجبة الوجود لذاتها

عرفان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage denies that God can be known at all.
Correction: The passage actually affirms the possibility of special divine knowledge through God's grace, angelic cognition, or a divinely taught name.

Checkpoint

The philosophers' claim that the divine essence has no name is not proven; there are plausible ways that God can be known and named.

Impossibility of Part-of-Essence Names for the Necessary Being
Author classifies and rules on names indicating part of essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why names that indicate part of the essence cannot apply to the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being is absolutely simple and free from composition.

In this passage, the author addresses the second type of divine name: those that indicate part of the essence. He declares such names impossible for the Necessary Being because of His absolute simplicity. Let's examine his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

2

3
Understand the reason
The author gives a reason: the Necessary Being is an individual free from multiplicity and composition. Reflect on why this reason forces the impossibility.
This connects to the core idea of divine simplicity.

The reason is clear: the Necessary Being is a single individual (fard) who is transcendentally free (munazzah) from all aspects of multiplicity (kathra) and composition (tarkib). Any name that suggests a part would imply that the essence is composed of parts, which contradicts His simplicity and unity. Hence, such names cannot apply to God.

Seven Types of Names Indicating External Attributes
Author enumerates seven types of names indicating external attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven types of names that indicate attributes external to the essence, as categorized by the author.

Core Idea
Divine names are not all alike—they fall into seven categories based on attribute type.

In this passage, the author continues his discussion of divine names by focusing on names that point to attributes outside the essence. He systematically lists seven possible kinds, showing the logical possibilities of combining real attributes, relations, and negations. This classification is essential for precise theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's enumeration come through.
Notice the structure: 'either ... or ... or ...'
2
Identify the three basic types
Find the three fundamental categories mentioned: real attribute, relation, and negation. These are the building blocks.
Real attribute: sifa haqiqiyya; relation: idafa; negation: salb.
3
List the seven combinations
The author lists seven types: pure real, pure relation, pure negation, real+relation, real+negation, relation+negation, and composite of all three. Write them down.
The seventh type is a composite: 'sum of real, relational, or negative attribute'.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why this classification matters. It shows that names are not all simple real attributes; they include relational and negative aspects.
This guards against oversimplifying divine names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو الاسم الذي يفيد صفة خارجة عن الماهية فنقول : هذا الاسم إما أن يدل على صفة حقيقية عارية عن الإضافة ، أو يدل على إضافة فقط ) أو على سلب فقط ) (٢) أو على صفة حقيقية مع إضافة أو على صفة حقيقية مع سلب . أو على صفة إضافية مع سلب أو على مجموع صفة حقيقية إضافية أو سلبية فهذه أقسام سبعة (٣)
As for the third division: it is the name that indicates an attribute external to the essence. We say: This name either indicates a real attribute devoid of relation, or indicates relation only, or negation only, or a real attribute with relation, or a real attribute with negation, or a relational attribute with negation, or a composite of a real, relational, or negative attribute. These are seven divisions.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a classification or enumeration. The author introduces the third type of divine names: those that indicate an attribute (sifa) external to the essence (kharija 'an al-mahiyya). He then lists seven possible kinds of such names. The seven kinds are: (1) a name indicating a real attribute (sifa haqiqiyya) devoid of any relation (i.e., pure attribute without reference to another); (2) a name indicating relation (idafa) only (e.g., 'Lord' in relation to servants); (3) a name indicating negation (salb) only (e.g., 'Holy' meaning free from defects); (4) a name indicating a real attribute with relation; (5) a real attribute with negation; (6) a relational attribute with negation; (7) a name that combines a real, relational, or negative attribute (i.e., it may include any of these types in combination). The author concludes that these are seven divisions. This classification is foundational for understanding the logic of divine names in kalam.

Key Terms

إضافة
سلب
أقسام سبعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that all divine names indicating external attributes are simply real attributes (like power or knowledge).
Correction: The author shows that names can also be purely relational (e.g., Lord), purely negative (e.g., Holy), or combinations, giving seven distinct types.

Key Takeaway

?

The Real Attribute of Existence and the Ambiguity of 'al-Mawjud'
Author discusses the first sub-type of external attribute: real attribute, specifically existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'al-mawjud' is ambiguous for expressing God's existence and how some scholars used 'al-masqiyah' to be unequivocal.

Core Idea
A single word can change meaning—precision matters in divine attributes.

In this passage, the author explores the first type of real attribute: existence itself. He shows that the common term 'al-mawjud' carries ambiguity, as it can refer to what is perceived or to essential existence. To avoid confusion, some monotheists adopted the Persian word 'al-masqiyah', which unambiguously denotes existence in itself. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully to understand the classification of real attributes and the discussion on the term 'al-mawjud'.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'al-mawjud'
Notice that the author explains two possible meanings: 'what is perceived' (wijdan) and 'being established in itself'. Distinguish between them.
3
Understand why 'al-masqiyah' is preferred
Observe that because of its ambiguity, 'al-mawjud' is not nass (unequivocal). Hence some monotheists used the Persian word 'al-masqiyah' to clearly refer to existence in essence.
4
Reflect on the importance of precise language
Consider why theologians must be careful with terminology when discussing God's attributes. This example shows how a seemingly clear word can cause confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القسم الأول : وهو الذي يدل على صفة حقيقية خارجة عن الماهية ، فنقول : تلك الصفة إما الوجود، وإما شيء من كيفيات الوجود ، وإما قسم ثالث مغاير للقسمين ، أما اللفظ الدال على كونه موجوداً ، فهو لفظ الموجود واعلم أن الموجود قد يراد به ما قبل (١) الوجدان. وعلى هذا التقدير ، فالموجود يجرى مجرى المعلوم. ومنه قوله تعالى : ( لوجدوا الله ثواباً رحيماً) (٣) وقد يراد به كونه ثابتاً في نفسه متحققاً في ذاته . وإذا ثبت هذا الاحتمال فحينئذ لا يكون لفظ الموجود نصاً في هذا المعنى . ولهذا السبب كان بعض الموحدين يعبر عن وجوده تعالى في نفسه بلفظ : المسقية ، لأن هذا اللفظ ، وإن كان فارسياً ، إلا أنه نص في المقصود ، وأما لفظ الموجود فإنه ليس نصاً في هذا المعنى .
As for the first division: it is what indicates a real attribute external to the essence. We say: That attribute is either existence, or something from the qualities of existence, or a third division different from the two. As for the word indicating that He is existent, it is the word 'al-mawjud'. And know that 'al-mawjud' may be intended to mean what is before perception (wijdan). On this assumption, 'al-mawjud' runs in the course of the known. And from this is His saying: 'They would have found Allah a merciful reward' (Quran 4:64). And it may be intended to mean His being established in Himself, realized in His essence. When this probability is established, then the word 'al-mawjud' is not unequivocal (nass) in this meaning. For this reason, some of the monotheists express His existence in Himself by the word 'al-masqiyah', because this word, even if Persian, is unequivocal in the intended meaning, whereas the word 'al-mawjud' is not unequivocal in this meaning.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the first sub-type of external attribute: a real attribute (sifa haqiqiyya). The author states that such an attribute can be either existence (al-wujud), or something from the qualities of existence (kayfiyyat al-wujud), or a third type different from these two. He focuses on the word indicating existence: 'al-mawjud'. He then points out an ambiguity: 'al-mawjud' can mean what is perceived (wijdan), i.e., what is found, in which case it is like 'known'. He cites Quran 4:64 'they would have found Allah' (law wajadu). Alternatively, it can mean being established in itself, realized in its essence. Because of this ambiguity, the word 'al-mawjud' is not unequivocal for the intended meaning of necessary existence. For this reason, some monotheists (muwahhidun) used the Persian word 'al-masqiyah' to express God's existence in Himself, since that word is unambiguous (nass) in that meaning. Thus, the author demonstrates the importance of precise terminology in naming God's attributes.

الوجود
كيفيات الوجود
الموجود
الوجدان
المسقية
نص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'al-mawjud' always and unambiguously means 'existent in essence'.
Correction: The author shows that 'al-mawjud' can also mean 'perceived' (from wijdan), so it is not unequivocal for essential existence. Hence some scholars used 'al-masqiyah'.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Meaning of al-Haqq: Truth as Existence
Author defines 'al-Haqq' as existent and applies logical rule to belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name 'al-Haqq' as the existent, and how truth in belief is defined by the necessary existence of its object.

Core Idea
Truth is existence; belief in the Necessary Existent is truth.

The author begins a discussion of names indicating the divine essence. He starts with 'al-Haqq', defining it not merely as 'truth' but as existence itself. This lesson unpacks that definition and shows how it leads to a precise criterion for true belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's style: he defines, cites Quran, then deduces a logical rule.
2
Identify the key definitions
Find where the author defines 'al-Haqq' and 'al-Batil'. Note that he equates truth with existence and falsehood with non-existence.
This is the foundation of the entire discussion.
3
Understand the Quranic support
The author cites three verses to show Allah is most deserving of the name al-Haqq. Reflect on how each verse reinforces the connection between Allah and truth/existence.
The verses are: 6:62, 10:82, 31:33.
4
Grasp the logical rule
The author states: for something necessary of existence, belief in it is called truth; for something necessary of non-existence, belief in it is called falsehood. This connects the name to modal ontology.
This rule is key to understanding later discussions about the necessary, impossible, and possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الألفاظ الدالة على الذات كثيرة . ونحن نذكر بعضها : فالأول : لفظة الحق. فنقول الحق هو الموجود ، والباطل هو المعدوم . وأحق الأشياء باسم الحق هو الله - سبحانه - قال - سبحانه - : ( ثم ردوا إلى الله مولاهم الحق) (1) وهو أيضاً : يحق الحق . قال تعالى : ( ويحق الله الحق بكلماته ) () وأيضاً : وعده حق . قال تعالى : ( إن وعد الله حق ) (1) واعلم أن الحق هو الموجود ، والباطل هو المعدوم ، فإذا كان الشيء واجب الوجود كان اعتقاد وجوده ، والإقرار بوجوده : مستحق التقرير والإثبات ، فلا جرم يسمى هذا الاعتقاد، وهذا الإقرار حقاً . وأما إذا كان واجب العدم، كان اعتقاد وجوده والإقرار بوجوده مستحق العلم ، فلا جرم يسمى هذا الاعتقاد ، وهذا الإقرار : باطلا .. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الشيء إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته ، أو ممتنعاً
If you have understood this, we say: The words indicating the essence are many. We will mention some of them. The first: the word 'al-Haqq'. We say: al-Haqq is the existent (al-mawjud), and al-Batil (falsehood) is the non-existent (al-ma'dum). The most deserving of things of the name al-Haqq is Allah—glorified be He. He—glorified be He—said: 'Then they are returned to Allah, their true Master' (Quran 6:62). And He also: establishes the truth (yuhiqqu al-haqq). He said: 'And Allah establishes the truth by His words' (Quran 10:82). And also: His promise is true. He said: 'Indeed, the promise of Allah is true' (Quran 31:33). And know that al-Haqq is the existent, and al-Batil is the non-existent. So when a thing is necessary of existence, believing in its existence and affirming its existence deserves confirmation and establishment, so this belief and affirmation are called truth (haqq). And when it is necessary of non-existence, believing in its existence and affirming its existence deserves annulment, so this belief and affirmation are called falsehood (batil). If you have understood this, we say: A thing is either necessary of existence in itself, or impossible...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of a divine name. The author begins by noting that words indicating the essence (al-dhat) are many; he will mention some. The first is 'al-Haqq'. He defines 'al-Haqq' as the existent (al-mawjud), and 'al-Batil' as the non-existent (al-ma'dum). He then states that Allah is the most deserving of the name 'al-Haqq'. He supports this with Quranic verses: 'Then they are returned to Allah, their true Master' (6:62), 'And Allah establishes the truth by His words' (10:82), and 'Indeed, the promise of Allah is true' (31:33). He then reiterates the definitions: truth is existence, falsehood is non-existence. From this, he derives a logical rule: For a thing that is necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud), believing in its existence and affirming it is worthy of confirmation, and thus that belief is called truth (haqq). Conversely, for a thing that is necessary of non-existence (wajib al-'adam), believing in its existence is worthy of annulment, and that belief is called falsehood (batil). He then transitions to a new classification: a thing is either necessary of existence in itself, or impossible (i.e., necessarily non-existent), or possible. This sets up the next discussion. Thus, the author systematically connected the name 'al-Haqq' with the concept of existence and truth in belief.

Key Terms

الباطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'al-Haqq' only means 'truth' in a doctrinal sense, without connection to existence.
Correction: The author explicitly ties 'al-Haqq' to existence: truth is existence itself, and falsehood is non-existence.

Checkpoint

A) When it is based on revelation.
B) When it affirms the existence of something that is necessary of existence.
C) When it is commonly accepted.
D) When it is pleasing to God.

Key Takeaway
Al-Haqq is existence; true belief is that which affirms a necessarily existent object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Haqq is existence, and al-Batil is non-existence. What 'truths' do you claim to believe that are actually empty—beliefs you affirm with your tongue but are not real in your heart? Will you confront one today and either make it real or discard it?
?
Allah is the most deserving of being called al-Haqq. Is He the most real thing in your life, or is something else—money, status, relationships—more present in your thoughts and actions? How can you shift your certainty today?
?
You are a possible being: you did not have to exist. Does this realization humble you? When you feel self-sufficient, remind yourself that you are contingent—dependent on the Necessary Existent. Will you repeat 'al-Haqq' today as a call to drop your ego and rely only on Him?

Is Allah a 'Thing'? The Debate with Jahm ibn Safwan
Debate over naming Allah 'thing'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments for and against calling Allah 'thing' (shay'), and how linguistic and Quranic reasoning is used in kalam.

Core Idea
Naming Allah 'thing' hinges on whether 'thing' is synonymous with 'existent' or more general.

In this lesson, we dive into a classical debate: can Allah be called a 'thing'? The theologian Jahm ibn Safwan said no, while the majority of scholars said yes. The passage presents both sides using Quranic verses and linguistic analysis. By examining these arguments, you'll see how kalam thinkers reasoned about divine names and the limits of human language.

Guided Path
1
Understand Jahm's position
Read the first part where the author states Jahm's objection. Note that Jahm does not deny Allah's existence, only the appropriateness of the name 'thing'.
This is important to avoid a common misunderstanding.
2
Explore the affirmative arguments
The passage presents two Quranic verses and a linguistic argument. See how each supports calling Allah 'thing'.
The linguistic argument contrasts two views on whether 'thing' and 'existent' are synonymous.
3
Examine the negative arguments
Now read the counter-arguments from the Quran: the verses 'Creator of everything' and 'Nothing like unto Him'. How do these seem to forbid calling Allah a 'thing'?
These arguments are more complex and involve logical implications.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاسم الثاني: إنه تعالى (شيء). وقال جهم بن صفوان: لا يجوز تسمية الله بهذا الاسم. واعلم أن جهماً لا ينازع في كونه تعالى موجوداً وإنما ينازع في أنه تعالى هل يسمى بهذا الاسم؟ أما الذين أطلقوا هذا الاسم على الله فقد احتجوا بالقرآن واللغة. أما القرآن فقوله تعالى: {قل: أي شيء أكبر شهادة؟ قل: الله} قالوا: هذه الآية دالة على أنه تعالى مسمى باسم الشيء. وأيضاً: قوله: {كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه} والمراد بالوجه: الذات فههنا: استثنى ذاته عن الشيء والمستثنى داخل تحت المستثنى منه. وأما اللغة: فهو أن من أنكر كون المعدوم شيئاً قال: الشيء والموجود مترادفان. ومن أثبت كون المعدوم شيئاً قال: الشيء ما يصح أن يعلم ويخبر عنه، فكأن لفظ الشيء أهم من لفظ الموجود. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: توافقنا على أنه تعالى موجود، فإن كان الشيء مرادفاً للموجود وجب كونه تعالى مسمى باسم الشيء، وإن كان الموجود أخص من الشيء، وقد صدق عليه الوجود الذي هو أخص، وجب أن يصدق عليه الشيء، الذي هو أعم. ولمن أنكر إطلاق اسم الشيء على الله أن يحتج بالقرآن والمعقول. أما القرآن فآيات: أحدها: قوله تعالى: {خالق كل شيء} فلو كان هو تعالى مسمى بالشيء لزم أن يكون خالقاً لنفسه، وهو محال. ولا يجوز أن يحمل ذلك على كون هذا العام مخصوصاً، لأن الخارج إذا كان هو القسم القليل يجوز حسن إطلاق لفظ الكل عليه، فإنه يجوز على القسم الأعظم الأكبر، إجراء للأعظم مجرى الكل. والحق سبحانه بتقدير أن يقع عليه اسم الشيء هو أعظم الأشياء وأجلها. لا يجوز ذلك، فتخصيص العموم في مثل هذه الصورة قبيح وغير جائز. والآية الثانية: قوله: {ليس كمثله شيء} حكم على مثل مثل نفسه بأنه ليس بشيء، ولا شك أنه مثل لمثل نفسه.
The second name: that He—exalted is He—is a 'thing'. And Jahm ibn Safwan said: it is not permissible to call Allah by this name. And know that Jahm does not dispute that He—exalted is He—exists; rather he disputes whether He—exalted is He—is called by this name. As for those who applied this name to Allah, they argued by the Qur'an and language. As for the Qur'an, His statement—exalted is He—: {Say: 'What thing is greatest in testimony?' Say: 'Allah'} They said: this verse indicates that He—exalted is He—is named by the name 'thing'. Also His statement: {Every thing is perishing except His Face} and what is meant by 'Face' is the Essence; so here He excepted His Essence from 'thing', and the excepted item is included under the exception. As for language, he who denies that the non-existent is a 'thing' says: 'thing' and 'existent' are synonymous. And he who affirms that the non-existent is a 'thing' says: 'thing' is what can be known and reported about; so the term 'thing' is more general than the term 'existent'. If this is established, we say: we agree that He—exalted is He—exists. If 'thing' is synonymous with 'existent', it is necessary that He—exalted is He—be named by the name 'thing'. And if 'existent' is more specific than 'thing', and existence—which is more specific—is true of Him, then it is necessary that 'thing'—which is more general—be true of Him. And for him who denies applying the name 'thing' to Allah, he may argue by the Qur'an and reason. As for the Qur'an, there are verses: one of them is His statement: {Creator of every thing}. If He—exalted is He—were named 'thing', it would imply that He is creator of Himself, which is impossible. And it is not permissible to interpret this as the universal being particularized, because when the excluded part is a small division, it may be good to apply the word 'all' to it—for it is permissible for the greatest and larger division, treating the greatest as the whole. But the Truth—glory be to Him—if it is supposed that the name 'thing' applies to Him, He is the greatest and most majestic of things. That is not permissible; so particularizing the universal in such a form is ugly and impermissible. The second verse: His statement: {There is nothing like unto Him}. He ruled that a likeness of Himself is not a thing, and there is no doubt that it is a likeness of Himself.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer debate concerning whether Allah can be called 'thing' (shay'). The author presents the position of Jahm ibn Safwan, who denies this naming. Then he gives the arguments of those who affirm it: from the Qur'an (Surah al-An'am verse 19 and Surah al-Qasas verse 88) and from language (the synonymy or generality of 'thing' relative to 'existent'). The author concludes that whether 'thing' is synonymous or more general, it applies to Allah because He exists. Then he presents the objection from those who deny, using two Qur'anic verses: (1) {Creator of every thing} would imply Allah creates Himself if He is a thing, and (2) {There is nothing like unto Him} — the verse negates that any likeness is a thing, but the likeness of Allah is a likeness of Himself, so it implies that Allah is not a thing. The argument is incomplete in the OCR but presents both sides.

The Debate on Calling Allah 'Thing' (Shay')
The author presents arguments for and against applying the name 'thing' to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments for and against applying the name 'thing' (shay') to Allah, and why the author concludes the debate is purely verbal.

Core Idea
What we call Allah is ultimately a verbal discussion.

This passage presents a classic kalam debate about whether the term 'thing' (shay') can be applied to Allah. Both sides use Quranic verses and linguistic reasoning. After laying out the arguments, the author reveals that the entire dispute is merely verbal, not a real theological division. Follow along to see how the arguments unfold and why the conclusion is so important for understanding the nature of theological disagreements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read through the Arabic and English carefully. Notice the structure: first the arguments in favor, then the arguments against, then the conclusion.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Quranic proofs
List the verses used by each side. Understand how each side interprets them to support their position.
There are two verses for the proponents and three for the opponents.
3
Analyze the linguistic argument
Focus on the discussion about whether 'thing' and 'existent' are synonyms or one is more general.
This is crucial for understanding the verbal nature of the debate.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusion
The author says it's a pure verbal discussion. What does that mean for how we should view such debates?
Consider how this affects theological methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الذين أطلقوا هذا الاسم على الله . فقد احتجوا بالقرآن واللغة . أما القرآن فقوله تعالى : ( قل : أي شيء أكبر شهادة ؟ قل : الله ) قالوا : هذه الآية دالة على أنه تعالى مسمى باسم الشيء . وأيضاً : قوله : ( كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه ) والمراد بالوجه : الذات فههنا : استثنى ذاته عن الشيء والمستثنى داخل تحت المستثنى منه . وأما اللغة : فهو أن من أنكر كون المعدوم شيئاً . قال : الشيء والموجود مترادفان . ومن أثبت كون المعدوم شيئاً ، قال : الشيء ما يصح أن يعلم ويخبر عنه ، فكأن لفظ الشيء أهم من لفظ الموجود . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : توافقنا على أنه تعالى موجود ، فإن كان الشيء مرادفاً للموجود وجب كونه تعالى مسمى باسم الشيء ، وإن كان الموجود أخص من الشيء ، وقد صدق عليه الوجود الذي هو أخص ، وجب أن يصدق عليه الشيء ، الذي هو أعم .
ولمن أنكر إطلاق اسم الشيء على الله . أن يحتج بالقرآن والمعقول . أما القرآن فآيات: أحدها: قوله تعالى : خالق كل شيء ) فلو كان هو تعالى مسمى بالشيء لزم أن يكون خالقاً لنفسه ، وهو محال . ولا يجوز أن يحمل ذلك على كون هذا العام مخصوصاً ، لأن الخارج إذا كان هو القسم القليل يجوز حسن إطلاق لفظ الكل عليه ، فإنه يجوز على القسم الأعظم الأكبر ، إجراء للأعظم مجرى الكل . والحق - سبحانه - بتقدير أن يقع عليه اسم الشيء هو أعظم الأشياء وأجلها . لا يجوز ذلك فتخصيص العموم في مثل هذه الصورة قبيح وغير جائز . والآية الثانية : قوله ( ليس كمثله شيء ) حكم على مثل مثل نفسه بأنه ليس بشيء ، ولا شك أنه مثل لمثل نفسه فوجب أن لا يسمى بالشيء . وقول من يقول : الكاف زائدة : بعيد . لأن حمل الفاظ هذا الكتاب العالي على الإفادة، وصونها عن اللغو أولى . والآية الثالثة . قوله تعالى : ( والله الأسماء الحسنى فادعوه بها ) أمر بأن يدعو الله بالأسماء الحسنى ، وحسن الاسم عبارة عن الألفاظ الدالة على صفات الجلال والكمال ولفظ الشيء يدل على القدر المشترك بين جميع الموجودات خسيسها ونفيسها. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك، كان هذا اللفظ خالياً عن الدلالة على صفات الجلال والكمال فوجب أن لا يجوز ذكره في حق الله - سبحانه . فهذا تمام البحث . وهو بحث لفظي محض
As for those who applied this name to Allah, they argued by the Qur'an and language. As for the Qur'an, His statement: 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: Allah.' They said: This verse indicates that He, exalted, is named with the name 'thing'. And also His statement: 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' And by 'Face' is meant the Essence; so here He excluded His Essence from 'thing', and the excluded is included under that from which it is excluded. As for language: he who denies that the non-existent is a 'thing' says that 'thing' and 'existent' are synonyms. And he who affirms that the non-existent is a 'thing' says that 'thing' is what can be known and reported about, so the word 'thing' is more general than the word 'existent'. And if this is established, we say: we agree that He, exalted, is existent. If 'thing' is synonymous with 'existent', then He must be named with the name 'thing'. And if 'existent' is more specific than 'thing', and existence which is more specific is true of Him, then 'thing' which is more general must be true of Him.
And for one who denies applying the name 'thing' to Allah, he may argue by the Qur'an and reason. As for the Qur'an, there are verses: One of them is His statement: 'Creator of every thing.' If He were named 'thing', it would follow that He is the creator of Himself, which is impossible. And it is not permissible to interpret this as a general term made specific, because if the exception is a small part, it is permissible to apply the word 'all' to the greatest and largest part, treating the greatest as if it were the whole. But Allah, exalted, if it were supposed that the name 'thing' applies to Him, He is the greatest and most majestic of things. That is not permissible. So specifying a general term in such a case is ugly and impermissible. The second verse: His statement: 'Nothing is like Him' – He ruled that something like His like is not a thing, and there is no doubt that He is like His like, so it follows that He should not be called 'thing'. And the saying of those who say the 'kaf' is redundant is far-fetched, because carrying the words of this exalted Book on beneficence and protecting them from futility is more appropriate. The third verse: His statement: 'And to Allah belong the beautiful names, so call upon Him by them.' He commanded that Allah be called by beautiful names. The beauty of a name consists of words indicating attributes of majesty and perfection, and the word 'thing' indicates the common denominator among all existents, low and noble. And since this is the case, this word is devoid of indicating attributes of majesty and perfection, so it is not permissible to mention it regarding Allah, exalted. This completes the discussion, and it is a purely verbal discussion.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents a complete dialectical debate about whether the name 'thing' (shay') can be applied to Allah. The author first states the position of those who do apply it, giving their proofs from Quran (two verses: 'What thing is greatest in testimony?' and 'Everything is perishing except His Face') and from language (the relationship between 'thing' and 'existent'). Their linguistic argument is that if 'thing' and 'existent' are synonymous, then Allah who is existent must be called 'thing'; and if 'existent' is more specific, then since Allah is existent, He must also be included in the more general term 'thing'.

The author then presents the counterarguments of those who deny the application. They cite three Quranic verses: (1) 'Creator of every thing' – if Allah were a 'thing', He would be creating Himself, which is absurd; the general term cannot be specified here because Allah is the greatest of things and specifying it would be inappropriate. (2) 'Nothing is like Him' – interpreting it as saying that something like His likeness is not a thing, implying He is not a thing; the objection that the 'kaf' (like) is redundant is rejected because the Quran is meaningful. (3) 'To Allah belong the beautiful names' – the word 'thing' indicates a common denominator among all existents, lacking specific praise, so it is not a beautiful name. The author concludes that the entire discussion is purely verbal (lafzī), meaning it concerns word usage rather than a real theological difference.

موجود (mawjūd)
الاسم (al-ism)
بحث لفظي (baḥth lafẓī)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this debate has serious theological consequences.
Correction: The author clarifies it is merely verbal (baḥth lafẓī), meaning it doesn't affect essential theology.

Checkpoint
What is the author's conclusion about the debate on calling Allah 'thing'?
A) It is a real theological disagreement that must be resolved definitively.
B) It is a purely verbal discussion without deep doctrinal import.
C) It is impermissible to call Allah 'thing' under any circumstances.
D) It is obligatory to call Allah 'thing' as per the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The debate over calling Allah 'thing' is a verbal discussion, not a substantial theological issue.

The Name 'Mahiyyah' and Its Quranic Root
Definition of 'mahiyyah' with linguistic and Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'mahiyyah' (quiddity) is a valid theological term with a Quranic basis, derived from the question 'mā hiya', and its usage in the Quran illustrates the permissibility of asking about God's essence.

Core Idea
The name 'mahiyyah' comes from 'what is it?' and is rooted in the Quran.

This lesson introduces the third divine name under discussion: 'al-māhiyyah' (quiddity). The author first explains its linguistic origin, showing it is a compound of the interrogative phrase 'mā hiya' (what is it?). He then demonstrates its Quranic occurrence in the dialogue between Pharaoh and Moses, proving that this term is not a late philosophical invention but has scriptural support. Pay attention to how the author uses the Quranic proof to legitimize the term in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the author's style: he defines, then proves.
The passage is short but dense with meaning.
2
Focus on the derivation of 'mahiyyah'
The author says the word is originally a compound from the question 'mā hiya' (ما هي). Understand that this makes 'mahiyyah' a noun that asks about the quiddity or essence of something.
This is different from 'man' which asks about attributes.
3
Identify the Quranic proof
The author quotes Pharaoh's question to Moses: 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' (Q. 26:23). See how Moses answers without objecting to the question, indicating its permissibility.
4
Notice the distinction between 'man' and 'mā'
The passage begins a discussion about two Quranic debates: one using 'man' (asking about attributes) and one using 'mā' (asking about essence). Here only the 'man' example is given, but the author implies both are legitimate.
This distinction is important for understanding different ways to inquire about God.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this matters: 'mahiyyah' is a key term in kalam and philosophy. Showing its Quranic basis grounds theological discussion in revelation.
Many critics claim such terms are foreign to Islam; the author refutes that.

الاسم الثالث : الماهية. واعلم أن هذا اللفظ لفظ مركب في الأصل ( لأنه في الأصل ) مأخوذ من قولهم عند الإشارة إلى الحقيقة المعينة : ما هي ؟ وهذا اللفظ وارد في القرآن . قال تعالى حكاية عن فرعون أنه قال لموسى : (وما رب العالمين) ؟ ثم إن موسى عليه السلام - ما منعه من هذا السؤال : بل اشتغل بجوابه ، فقال : رب السموات والأرض وما بينهما إن كنتم موقنين) .

The third name: al-māhiyyah (quiddity). Know that this word is originally a compound word, derived from their saying when pointing to a specific reality: 'What is it?' And this word occurs in the Qur'an. He, exalted, said, narrating from Pharaoh that he said to Moses: 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' Then Moses, peace be upon him, did not refrain from this question, but engaged in answering it, saying: 'The Lord of the heavens and the earth and what is between them, if you are certain.'
And know that there is a lofty and noble investigation here, which is that in the Divine Book there came a debate of Pharaoh with Moses, peace be upon him, twice. Once he said: 'So who is the Lord of you two, O Moses?' and he requested from him a definition of the deity by the word 'who', and this word seeks the attribute, not the essence.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage introduces the third name under discussion: 'al-māhiyyah' (quiddity or essence). The author first explains its etymology: it is a compound word originally formed from the question 'mā hiya?' (what is it?) used when pointing to a specific reality. He then notes that this word appears in the Qur'an in Pharaoh's question to Moses: 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' (Q. 26:23). Moses answered directly without objecting to the question, proving its permissibility.

The author then highlights a subtle point: the debate between Pharaoh and Moses occurs twice in the Qur'an. One instance uses the interrogative 'man' (who) which asks about attributes, not the essence. The second instance (not fully quoted here) uses 'mā' (what) which asks about the quiddity. This distinction shows that both questions are legitimate in seeking knowledge of God, though they focus on different aspects—attributes versus essence. The passage is incomplete in this window but sets up a broader discussion on divine names and their implications.

ما هي (mā hiya)

Misconception: One might think 'mahiyyah' is a later philosophical term with no Quranic basis.
Correction: The author shows its root in the Quranic dialogue (Q. 26:23) and its derivation from 'mā hiya', proving its legitimacy in Islamic discourse.

Key Takeaway
The term 'mahiyyah' is linguistically derived from 'mā hiya' and is authentically used in the Quran, validating its place in theological inquiry.

?

The Divine Name al-Haqiqah: The Realizer of Realities
Razi defines the name 'al-Haqiqah' and explains its active and passive meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dual meaning of the divine name al-Haqiqah as active (regarding God) and passive (regarding creation).

Core Idea
Al-Haqiqah means the One who makes realities real, and everything else is made real by Him.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi explains the morphological form of the divine name al-Haqiqah. He shows how the Arabic pattern faʿīl can function both as an active participle and a passive participle, and applies this to God and possible beings. This clarifies why the same word can refer to God as the realizer and to creatures as the realized.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Razi begins by stating the name and then analyzes its form.
2
Understand the morphological point
Understand that the Arabic form faʿīl can be an active participle (like naṣīr = helper) or a passive participle (like qatīl = killed). This is key to interpreting the name.
Razi gives examples: al-naṣīr, al-baṣīr, al-ʿalīm for active; al-qatīl for passive.
3
Apply to God: active meaning
When applied to God, al-Haqiqah means the one who realizes realities (haqa'iq) and brings quiddities (mahiyyat) into existence. God is the active realizer.
4
Apply to creation: passive meaning
When applied to possible beings (mumkinat), al-Haqiqah means they are realized by God's act of realization. They are the passive recipients.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Notice how this name emphasizes God's absolute creativity and the contingency of everything else. Everything depends on God's act of making real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاسم الرابع : الحقيقة . وهي فعيلة ، والفعيل قد يكون بمعنى الفاعل ( كالنصير بمعنى الناصر ) (۲) والبصير بمعنى الباصر ، والعليم بمعنى العالم . وقد يكون بمعنى المفعول كالقتيل بمعنى المقتول . فالحقيقة في حق الله تعالى ، بمعنى الفاعل : هو الذي يحقق الحقائق ، ويكون الماهيات. وفي حق الممكنات بمعنى المفعول ، يعني أنها محققة بتحقيق الحق لها
The fourth name: al-Ḥaqīqah. It is the form faʿīlah, and faʿīl can be in the meaning of the active participle (like al-naṣīr meaning the helper, and al-baṣīr meaning the seer, and al-ʿalīm meaning the knower). And it can be in the meaning of the passive participle, like al-qatīl meaning the killed one. So al-Ḥaqīqah with respect to God the Exalted, in the meaning of the active participle, is He who realizes realities and brings quiddities into existence. And with respect to possible beings, it is in the meaning of the passive participle, meaning they are realized by the realization of the Truth (al-Ḥaqq) for them.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines the name 'al-Haqiqah' from a morphological and theological perspective. It first explains that the Arabic form faʿīl can be used both as an active participle (like naṣīr = helper) and as a passive participle (like qatīl = killed one). Applying this to 'al-Haqiqah', when referring to God, it is active: He is the one who makes realities (haqa'iq) and quiddities (mahiyyat) exist. When referring to possible beings (mumkinat), it is passive: they are the ones that are realized by God's act of realization. This highlights God's absolute creativity and the contingency of everything else.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-Ḥaqīqah)
فعيلة (faʿīlah)
فعيل (faʿīl)
المفعول (al-mafʿūl)
ماهيات (māhiyyāt)
ممكنات (mumkināt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Haqiqah simply means 'truth' and has no deeper theological meaning.
Correction: Al-Haqiqah is a divine name meaning the One who makes realities real, and it has both an active sense (for God) and a passive sense (for creation).

Checkpoint
What does 'al-Haqiqah' mean when applied to God?
A) The truth
B) The one who realizes realities
C) The essence
D) The active participle

Key Takeaway
Al-Haqiqah as a divine name means God is the realizer of all realities, while created things are realized by Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How does that challenge your feelings of security or control? What would it look like to consciously depend on God for that thing today?
?
If God is al-Haqiqah, the Realizer of realities, then every fear you have is only a reality because He makes it real. Which fear are you currently surrendering to as if it were independently powerful? What would it mean to ask God to 'un-real' that fear in your heart and see His reality as the only ultimate reality?

The Name al-Dhat: Essence and Self-Subsistence
Razi explains the name 'al-Dhat' and why God's essence is most worthy of it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic origin of the term 'al-Dhat' and why God's essence is most worthy of this name due to His self-subsistence.

Core Idea
The name 'al-Dhat' points to God as the self-subsistent essence independent of all substrate.

This passage from Razi explores the divine name 'al-Dhat'. It begins with the word's original meaning—indicating a thing's possession of something—and traces its technical development in kalam to refer to the quiddity qualified by attributes. Razi concludes that God's essence is the most perfect in self-subsistence, making Him the most deserving of this name.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the progression from linguistic usage to technical definition.
2
Analyze the linguistic origin
Examine how 'dhat' originally indicated possession (e.g., 'a woman having wealth'). Reflect on how this relates to attributes.
3
Understand the technical meaning
See how the term evolved to signify the quiddity that is qualified by attributes, the thing that has the capacity to be described.
4
Apply to God
Consider why God's essence is the most worthy of this name—because of His complete independence from any substrate (mahall) or receptacle (qabil).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاسم الخامس : الذات. اعلم أن هذه اللفظة وضعت للدلالة على اختصاص شيء بشيء آخر. يقال: امرأة ذات مال ، وذات جمال ، وماهية ذات كذا، وحقيقة ذات كذا. وتمام البيان فيه أن ذوات الأشياء من حيث أنها تلك الذوات والحقائق مجهولة. لا يمكن تعريفها إلا بصفاتها ، فلهذا السبب يقال : حقيقة ذات كذا وكذا ، حتى تصير تلك الصفة معرفة لتلك الحقيقة . ثم جعل لفظ الذات مفيد لتلك الماهية التي هي موصوفة بالصفات ، فلا جرم دل لفظ الذات على الأمر الذي له صلاحية أن تكون هي موصوفة بالصفات . وهذا هو تفسير الذات ، وأحق الذوات بهذا الاسم ذات الله - تعالى - لأنه أكمل الذوات في القيام بالنفس عن المحل ، وفي الاستغناء عن المحل والقابل
The fifth name: al-Dhāt. Know that this word is placed to indicate the specialization of a thing with another. One says: a woman having wealth (dhātu mālin), and having beauty, and the quiddity of such-and-such, and the reality of such-and-such. The complete explanation of it is that the essences (dhawāt) of things, in terms of their being those essences and realities, are unknown. They cannot be defined except by their attributes. For this reason it is said: the reality of such-and-such an essence (ḥaqīqatu dhāti kadhā), so that the attribute becomes a definition of that reality. Then the word dhāt was made to indicate that quiddity which is qualified by attributes. So necessarily the word dhāt indicates the thing that has the capacity to be qualified by attributes. And this is the interpretation of dhāt. And the most worthy of essences of this name is the essence of God the Exalted, because it is the most perfect of essences in self-subsistence (al-qiyām bil-nafs) without being in a substrate, and in independence from substrate and receptacle.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage explains the name 'al-Dhat'. It begins with its linguistic origin: the word 'dhat' originally denotes a thing's possession of something (e.g., a woman having wealth). Then it explains that the essences (dhawat) of things are unknown in themselves; they are known through their attributes. So the phrase 'reality of such-and-such an essence' uses 'dhat' to refer to the quiddity that is described by attributes. Over time, 'dhat' came to indicate that quiddity which is qualified by attributes. The author then concludes that God's essence is most worthy of this name because He is self-subsistent (qiyam bil-nafs) and independent of any substrate (mahall) or receptacle (qabil). This emphasizes God's absolute ontological independence.

Key Terms
الذات (al-Dhāt)
اختصاص (ikhtiṣāṣ)
ماهية (māhiyya)
صفات (ṣifāt)
القيام بالنفس (al-qiyām bil-nafs)
المحل (al-maḥall)
القابل (al-qābil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'dhat' simply means 'essence' without appreciating its original linguistic connection to possession and its technical development in kalam.
Correction: The term 'dhat' originally indicated a thing's possession of something (e.g., a woman having wealth) and evolved through kalam to refer to the quiddity qualified by attributes, which is why God's self-subsistent essence is the most worthy of the name.

Checkpoint
Why is God's essence the most worthy of the name 'al-Dhat'?
A) Because He has many attributes
B) Because He is independent of substrate and receptacle
C) Because He is the only essence that is self-subsistent
D) Because He is known through His attributes

Key Takeaway
God's essence is the most deserving of the name 'al-Dhat' because He is the most perfect in self-subsistence and independence from substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You routinely describe God as 'the Self-Subsistent'. When you say that, do you truly feel His independence from everything in your life, or do you still act as if His help depends on something? How can you practically affirm His self-subsistence in your dua today?
?
The passage says essences are unknown except through attributes. In your knowledge of God, do you seek to know His attributes from revelation, or have you contented yourself with a vague notion of 'essence' that you never let define your worship?
?
Razi argues God is most worthy of the name 'al-Dhat' because He is most independent. What in your life are you still trying to make independent apart from God? Will you let go of that false independence today?

The Divine Name al-Nafs: Essence, Not Body
Razi defines the name 'al-Nafs' and clarifies that it means essence, not body, for God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine name 'al-Nafs' refers to God's essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqah), not to a body or soul.

Core Idea
'Al-Nafs' for God means His essence, not a body.

This lesson explores the sixth divine name, al-Nafs, as defined by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. Razi begins by citing a Quranic verse where the term is used in reference to God. He then clarifies a potential misunderstanding: the word 'nafs' can mean body, but that is impossible for God. Instead, for God, it means essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqah).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the verse citation and the two possible meanings presented.
2
Identify the Quranic proof text
Locate the verse and note how it uses the term 'nafs' for God. This shows the scriptural basis for the name.
3
Note the two possible meanings of 'nafs'
Razi explains that 'nafs' can refer to the body (jasad) or to the essence (dhat). Understand why the corporeal meaning is impossible for God.
The impossibility is due to God's transcendence beyond physicality.
4
Understand which meaning applies to God
Razi affirms that for God, 'nafs' means essence and reality. This guards against anthropomorphism.
This aligns with the broader Islamic rejection of corporealism (tajsim).
5
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Consider why distinguishing these meanings is important for understanding divine attributes and avoiding misinterpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاسم السادس : النفس . قال تعالى : تعلم ما في نفسي . ولا أعلم ما في نفسك (۳) والنفس قد يراد به الجسد ، وذلك في حق الله تعالى محال وقد يراد به الذات والحقيقة. وهو المراد بهذا اللفظ في حق الله تعالى
The sixth name: al-Nafs. He said, Exalted: 'You know what is in my self (nafsī) and I do not know what is in Your Self (nafsik).' And al-nafs may refer to the body, and that is impossible with respect to God the Exalted. And it may refer to the essence (dhāt) and reality (ḥaqīqah), and that is what is intended by this word with respect to God the Exalted.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines the name 'al-Nafs' by first citing a Quranic verse where the term is used for God. It then clarifies that the word 'nafs' can mean the body (jasad), but that is impossible for God. The other meaning is essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqah). For God, 'al-Nafs' is used exclusively in the sense of essence, synonymous with 'al-Dhat'. This guards against any corporeal interpretation when the Quran describes God's 'self'.

Key Terms
النفس (al-Nafs)
الجسد (al-jasad)
الذات (al-dhāt)
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'al-Nafs' in the Quran refers to a body or a soul for God.
Correction: For God, 'al-Nafs' means the essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqah), not a body or soul. The corporeal meaning is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What does the name 'al-Nafs' mean when applied to God according to Razi?
A) The body
B) The essence
C) The soul
D) The self

Key Takeaway
The name 'al-Nafs' for God signifies His essence, not a body or soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does realizing that His essence is simple and indivisible change the way you approach His other names? What will you do when you next read a verse that might seem to imply a body?

Definition of al-Dhat: Essence as the Subject of Attributes
Defines the name al-Dhat linguistically and theologically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and theological meaning of the term 'al-Dhat' and why God's essence is the most deserving of this name.

Core Idea
Al-Dhat refers to that which is qualified by attributes, and God's essence is the most perfect in self-subsistence.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah defines the fifth divine name, al-Dhat. It explains that the word 'dhat' originally indicated possession, then came to signify the subject of attributes. The author argues that essences are known only through their attributes, and God's essence is supreme because it is absolutely independent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the progression from linguistic origin to theological conclusion.
2
Understand the linguistic origin
Identify the original meaning of 'dhat' as indicating possession (e.g., 'woman with property'). See how this leads to the technical meaning.
The example 'امرأة ذات مال' illustrates the root concept.
3
Grasp the theological conclusion
Focus on why God's essence is most worthy of the name: because He is self-subsistent and independent of any substrate.
This connects to the doctrine of God's absolute oneness and transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاسم الخامس : الذات. اعلم أن هذه اللفظة وضعت للدلالة على اختصاص شيء بشيء آخر. يقال: امرأة ذات مال ، وذات جمال ، وماهية ذات كذا، وحقيقة ذات كذا. وتمام البيان فيه أن ذوات الأشياء من حيث أنها تلك الذوات والحقائق مجهولة. لا يمكن تعريفها إلا بصفاتها ، فلهذا السبب يقال : حقيقة ذات كذا وكذا ، حتى تصير تلك الصفة معرفة لتلك الحقيقة . ثم جعل لفظ الذات مفيد لتلك الماهية التي هي موصوفة بالصفات ، فلا جرم دل لفظ الذات على الأمر الذي له صلاحية أن تكون هي موصوفة بالصفات . وهذا هو تفسير الذات ، وأحق الذوات بهذا الاسم ذات الله - تعالى - لأنه أكمل الذوات في القيام بالنفس عن المحل ، وفي الاستغناء عن المحل والقابل
The fifth name: al-Dhat. Know that this word was coined to indicate the special association of a thing with another thing. It is said: a woman with property, and with beauty, and essence with such-and-such, and reality with such-and-such. And the complete explanation of it is that the essences (dhawāt) of things, inasmuch as they are those essences and realities, are unknown. They cannot be defined except by their attributes. For this reason it is said: the reality of such-and-such an essence, so that the attribute becomes a definer of that reality. Then the word dhat came to be used for the quiddity (māhiyyah) that is described by attributes. So necessarily the word dhat indicates the thing that has the capacity to be described by attributes. And this is the interpretation of dhat. And the most worthy of essences for this name is the essence of God - exalted is He - because He is the most perfect of essences in self-subsistence without a substrate, and in independence from substrate and receptacle.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines the name al-Dhat. It first explains the linguistic origin: dhat indicates the possession of something (e.g., 'woman with property'). Then it argues that essences in themselves are unknown; we know them through attributes. The word dhat therefore refers to that which is qualified by attributes. God's essence is the most deserving of this name because He is absolutely self-subsistent and independent of any substrate.

Key Terms
الذات
الماهية
الصفات
القيام بالنفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'al-Dhat' refers to a concrete object like a physical thing.
Correction: It actually denotes the subject of attributes, especially God's transcendent essence, which is not a physical object but the self-subsistent reality.

D) Because He is the first essence

Key Takeaway
The name al-Dhat refers to the subject of attributes, and God's essence is most deserving of this name due to His absolute self-subsistence.

?

Understanding al-Qawiyy: The Strong
Defines al-Qawiyy with two interpretations from Quran 51:58.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of the divine name al-Qawiyy and why the second interpretation is preferred.

Core Idea
Strength in Allah means absolute independence and causal efficacy, not physical power.

When we say Allah is al-Qawiyy (the Strong), what does that mean? This lesson explores two possible meanings derived from the Quranic verse, 'Possessor of strength, the Firm.' The first emphasizes His immunity to non-existence, while the second highlights His active power and impassivity. Let's examine the text to see which interpretation the author favors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author cites a Quranic verse and then explains two interpretations.
The verse is from Surah Adh-Dhariyat (51:58).
2
Identify the two interpretations
Look for where the author presents the first interpretation (strength as not accepting non-existence) and the second (strength as being effective in others, firmness as not being affected).
The second is introduced with 'ويمكن أن يقال' (and it can be said).
3
Understand the preference
The author states 'وهذا أحسن' (and this is better). Why? The second interpretation gives a more complete picture of Allah's attributes—both active power and impassivity.
4
Reflect on the broader meaning
Consider how these interpretations guard against thinking of Allah's strength in physical terms. His strength is metaphysical—He is the source of all effect and immune to any effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاسم الثاني : القوي . قال تعالى : ( إن الله هو الرازق ذو القوة المتين ) (۱) وذلك لأن الشيء الذي لا يقبل التغيير يقال : إنه قوي . فكونه تعالى بحيث لا يقبل العدم في ذاته البنة ، هو المراد من القوة . ويمكن أن يقال : المراد من القوة: هو كونه مؤثراً في غيره ، والمراد بكونه متيناً بحيث لا يقبل الأثر من غيره ، وهذا أحسن
The second name: al-Qawiyy (the Strong). He, the Most High, said: 'Indeed, Allah is the Provider, the Possessor of strength, the Firm' (1). That is because a thing that does not accept change is called strong. So His being, exalted, such that He does not accept non-existence in His essence at all is what is intended by 'strength' (al-quwwah). And it can be said that what is intended by strength is His being effective (mu'aththir) in others, and what is intended by His being firm (matin) is that He does not accept effect from others. And this is better.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines the name al-Qawiyy. It cites the Quranic verse 'possessor of strength, the Firm' and gives two interpretations. The first: strength means not admitting non-existence in one's essence; the second (preferred): strength means being effective in others, while 'the Firm' means not being affected by others. The author favors the second as a better explanation.

Key Terms
القوي
القوة
المتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's strength is similar to physical strength or might like a powerful being.
Correction: The passage clarifies that divine strength is about existential independence and causal efficacy, not physical power.

Checkpoint
Which interpretation of al-Qawiyy does the author consider better?
A) Not accepting non-existence in His essence
B) Being effective in others and not accepting effect from others
C) Having physical strength
D) Being immune to change

Key Takeaway
Allah's name al-Qawiyy means He is the source of all effect and immune to any effect, combining active power with absolute impassivity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author says it is better to understand al-Qawiyy as active power and al-Matin as impassivity. How does this confront your desire to control outcomes in your life? The Meaning of al-Qayyum (The Self-Subsistent and Sustainer)
Defines al-Qayyum as self-subsisting and sustaining all else.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the name al-Qayyum combines two essential meanings: self-subsistence and sustaining all others, and why this makes Him perfect in self-subsistence.

Core Idea
Al-Qayyum means both self-subsisting and sustaining all creation.

The name al-Qayyum appears in the Qur'an (e.g., Ayat al-Kursi) and is often translated as 'the Self-Subsistent'. But the classical scholars explain that it carries a richer meaning: it includes both being independent of everything and being the source of existence for everything else. In this passage, the author unpacks these two aspects to show why Allah alone deserves this name.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that the definition has two clear parts separated by the conjunction 'wa' (and).
The Arabic is concise; each part is explained briefly.
2
Understand 'self-subsisting'
Focus on the first part: 'qā'im bi-dhātih'. This means He exists by Himself, needing no cause or sustainer. The result is complete independence from everything else.
This is the aspect of being self-sufficient.
3
Understand 'sustaining others'
Now look at the second part: 'muqawwim li-ghayrih'. It means He constitutes and brings into existence everything other than Him. He is the Creator and Originator.
This shows His active relationship with creation.
4
Combine the meanings
The passage concludes that the combination of these two attributes makes Him 'the perfect in self-subsistence', which is why He is named Qayyum. Reflect on how both aspects together affirm His uniqueness.
Neither aspect alone would be sufficient to capture the full meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third: al-Qayyum (the Self-Subsistent). And it is a name for that which is self-subsisting (qā'im bi-dhātih) and sustaining (muqawwim) for others. So His being self-subsisting means that He exists for Himself and by Himself, so necessarily He is independent of everything else. And His being sustaining for others means that He is the one who constitutes (mukawwin) everything else. And He is the one who brings into existence everything other than Him. And the essence described as self-subsisting and sustaining others is the perfect in self-subsistence, hence He is called Qayyum.

Commentary
This passage defines the name al-Qayyum. It breaks the meaning into two parts: (1) self-subsisting (qā'im bi-dhātih) meaning existing by and for Himself, independent of all else; (2) sustaining (muqawwim) for others, meaning He is the creator and originator of all other things. Combining these two aspects makes Him the perfect in self-subsistence, hence deserving the name Qayyum.

The Three Names of Necessity: Wajib, Qawi, Qayyum
Author enumerates three names under necessity: Wajib, Qawi, Qayyum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three divine names that indicate the necessity of God's existence—Wajib, Qawi, and Qayyum—and the distinct aspect each emphasizes.

Core Idea
Wajib, Qawi, and Qayyum each reveal a unique dimension of God's necessary existence.

This lesson explores three key names of Allah that describe the modality of His existence: Wajib, Qawi, and Qayyum. Each name highlights a different facet of His necessary nature—from self-necessity to absolute strength and self-subsistence. The passage clarifies their meanings and connects them to the concept of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the three names and their explanations.
Notice how the author first distinguishes two modalities: necessity and permanence, then focuses on necessity.
2
Identify each name and its explanation
Find the section for each name: Wajib al-Wujud, al-Qawi, and al-Qayyum. Note the specific meaning and any Quranic proof.
For al-Qawi there are two interpretations; the author prefers the second.
3
Reflect on the distinct aspects
Ask yourself what unique aspect of God's necessity each name emphasizes. Compare them.
They are not synonyms; each covers a different angle: self-necessity, strength against non-existence, and self-subsistence plus sustaining others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني : الأسماء الدالة على كيفية الوجود : واعلم أن هذه الكيفية نوعان : أحدهما : الوجوب والآخر : الدوام . أما الاعتبار الأول ففيه ثلاثة أسماء : الأول : قولنا واجب الوجود لذاته ، وذلك يفيد أنه يستحق الوجود من ذاته المخصوصة ، ولذاته المخصوصة ، وقريب من هذا اللفظ قولنا بالفارسية : ( خداي ) وأصل هذه اللفظة قولهم : خوداي ، ومعناه : بنفسه جاء ، والمراد من هذا المجيء : الوجود . فصار قولنا : « خداي : أي بنفسه وجد . وذلك هو اللفظ المطابق لقولنا : واجب الوجود لذاته . الاسم الثاني : القوي . قال تعالى : ( إن الله هو الرازق ذو القوة المتين ) (۱) وذلك لأن الشيء الذي لا يقبل التغيير يقال : إنه قوي . فكونه تعالى بحيث لا يقبل العدم في ذاته البنة ، هو المراد من القوة . ويمكن أن يقال : المراد من القوة: هو كونه مؤثراً في غيره ، والمراد بكونه متيناً بحيث لا يقبل الأثر من غيره ، وهذا أحسن . الثالث : القيوم . وهو إسم لما يكون قائماً بذاته مقوماً لغيره ، فكونه قائماً بذاته . معناه : أنه يكون موجوداً لذاته وبذاته ، فلا جرم يستغني عن كل ما سواه ( وكونه مقوماً لغيره : معناه . أنه هو المكون لكل ما سواه ) (٢) وهو الموجد لكل ما عداه ، والذات الموصوفة بكونها قائمة بذاتها ، ومقومة لغيرها هو الكامل في القيام ، فلهذا سمي قيوماً .
The Second Section: Names indicating the modality of existence: Know that this modality is of two types: one is necessity (wujub) and the other is permanence (dawam). As for the first consideration, there are three names in it: The first: our saying 'Wajib al-Wujud li-Dhatihi' (Necessary of Existence in itself), and that indicates that He deserves existence from His specific essence and for His specific essence. Close to this expression is our saying in Persian: 'Khuday'; the origin of this word is their saying 'Khuday', meaning 'by Himself He came', and the intended meaning of this 'coming' is existence. So our saying 'Khuday' means 'by Himself He exists'. And that is the expression corresponding to our saying 'Wajib al-Wujud li-Dhatihi'. The second name: al-Qawi (the Strong). He, exalted, said: 'Indeed, Allah is the Provider, the Possessor of strength, the Firm' (51:58). That is because a thing that does not accept change is called strong. So His being, exalted, such that He absolutely does not accept non-existence in His essence is what is meant by strength. It can also be said: what is meant by strength is His being effective in others, and what is meant by 'Firm' such that He does not accept effect from others, and this is better. The third: al-Qayyum (the Self-Subsisting). It is a name for that which is self-subsisting and sustains others. So His being self-subsisting means that He exists in Himself and by Himself, so necessarily He is independent of everything else. And His being the Sustainer of others means that He is the one who brings everything else into existence. And the essence described as being self-subsisting and sustaining others is perfect in subsistence, therefore He is called Qayyum.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage classifies the names of God that indicate the modality of existence (kayfiyyat al-wujud). The author states that this modality is of two types: necessity (wujub) and permanence (dawam). Under the first type (necessity), he lists three names. The first is 'Wajib al-Wujud li-Dhatihi' (Necessary of Existence in Himself), which means He deserves existence from and for His own unique essence. The author connects this to the Persian word 'Khuday', which etymologically means 'by Himself He came', i.e., existence by Himself. The second name is 'al-Qawi' (the Strong). He cites the Quranic verse 'Inna Allaha huwa al-Raziqu dhu al-Quwwati al-Matin' (51:58). The meaning of strength here is that God does not accept non-existence at all. An alternative interpretation, which the author considers better, is that strength means His power to affect others, while 'al-Matin' (the Firm) means He is not affected by others. The third name is 'al-Qayyum' (the Self-Subsisting). This indicates that God is self-subsisting (qa'im bi-dhatihi) and sustains others (muqawwim li-ghayrihi). Being self-subsisting means He exists by and for Himself, independent of all else. Being the Sustainer means He brings everything else into existence. Such an essence is perfect in subsistence, hence the intensive form 'Qayyum'.

واجب الوجود لذاته
القوي
القيوم
قائم بذاته
مقوم لغيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All three names are synonyms with no difference in meaning.
Correction: The passage shows that each name highlights a distinct aspect: Wajib emphasizes self-necessity, Qawi emphasizes strength against non-existence or effective power, and Qayyum emphasizes self-subsistence and sustaining others.

Key Takeaway
