The Eternal: Al-Qadim and Its Correct Meaning
Author defines al-Qadim and refutes two erroneous interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Qadim is a positive attribute and why two common misconceptions about it are false.

Core Idea
Al-Qadim affirms that God has no beginning; it is not a negative or separate attribute.

The author begins by explaining the second type of existence modality: permanence. The first name under this category is al-Qadim. He defines it clearly and then refutes two incorrect views: that qidam is a negative attribute and that it is a separate attribute. This lesson focuses on the correct understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Qadim
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice the definition and the two refutations.
Focus on the logical arguments.
2
Identify the two wrong views
The author rejects the idea that qidam is negative and the idea that it is a separate attribute. Understand why each is problematic.
3
Grasp the positive nature
The author concludes that qidam is the negation of prior non-existence, and negation of negation is affirmation. This makes it a positive description.
This is crucial for kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوع الثاني من كيفيات الوجود فهو الدوام . وفيه أنواع من الأسماء : الإسم الأول : القديم . واعلم أن القديم هو الذي لا أول لوجوده وقد يراد به : الذي طالت مدة وجوده. قال تعالى : ( إنك لفي ضلالك القديم ) (1) وقال : ( حق عاد كالعرجون القديم ) (2) وقد دللنا على أنه - سبحانه - قديم أزلي . وقال بعضهم : القدم صفة سلبية . وهو خطأ . لأنه عبارة عن نفي العدم السابق ، ونفي النفي ثبوت . ومنهم من قال : إنه قديم بقدم هو صفة قائمة به . وهو أيضاً باطل . وإلا لكان ذلك القدم قديماً بقدم آخر ، ولزم التسلسل ، وهو محال . الثاني : الأزلي . وهو غير ما ذكرناه في تفسير القدم . الإسم الثالث : الأبدي . ومعناه أنه لا آخر له ، ولا يصير معدوماً بعد وجوده البتة . الإسم الرابع : الباقي ) قال تعالى في ( طه ) : ( والله خير وأبقى ) (3) ومنهم من قال : هو الذي وجد أكثر من زمان واحد . ومنهم من قال : الباقي هو الذي يكون مبرأ من العدم من جميع الوجوه . وعلى هذا التقدير ، فالباقي ليس إلا الله . واعلم أنه تعالى واجب الوجود لذاته ، وكل ما كان كذلك كان دائم الوجود ، فإن اعتبرنا دوامه في الماضي (4) ، فهو القدم والأزل ، وإن اعتبرنا دوامه في المستقبل فهو الأبد والسرمد ، ولفظ السرمد اشتقاقه من السرد ، وهو الاستمرار والتعاقب ، وأدخل فيه الميم ليفيد هذا المعنى على سبيل المبالغة . وقال بعضهم : الباقي هو الذي لا ابتداء لوجوده ، ولا انتهاء لوجوده . وقيل : الباقي الذي يكون في أمده على الوصف الذي في أمده . وقيل : هو الأول بلا ابتداء ، والآخر بلا انتهاء
As for the second type of the modalities of existence, it is permanence (dawam). In it are types of names: The first name: al-Qadim (the Eternal). Know that al-Qadim is that which has no beginning to its existence, and sometimes it is used to mean that which has a long duration of existence. He, exalted, said: 'Indeed, you are in your old error' (12:95) and said: 'The remains of 'Ad like an old palm stump' (54:39). And we have demonstrated that He—glorified—is Qadim (eternal) and Azali (pre-eternal). Some said: al-Qidam (eternity) is a negative attribute. That is an error because it is an expression of negating prior non-existence, and the negation of negation is affirmation. And some said: He is Qadim by a qidam that is an attribute subsisting in Him. That is also false, because then that qidam would be qadim by another qidam, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible. The second: al-Azali (the Pre-Eternal). It is different from what we mentioned in the interpretation of al-Qidam. The third: al-Abadi (the Everlasting). Its meaning is that there is no end to Him, and He will never become non-existent after His existence at all. The fourth: al-Baqi (the Enduring). He, exalted, said in Surah Taha: 'And Allah is better and more enduring' (20:73). Among them are those who said: He is that which exists for more than one time. Among them are those who said: al-Baqi is that which is free from non-existence in all respects. According to this estimation, al-Baqi is none but Allah. Know that He, exalted, is Wajib al-Wujud li-Dhatihi (Necessary of Existence in Himself), and everything that is so is ever-existing. If we consider its permanence in the past, it is al-Qidam (eternity) and al-Azal (pre-eternity), and if we consider its permanence in the future, it is al-Abad (everlastingness) and al-Sarmad (perpetuity). The word sarmad is derived from al-sard, which is continuity and succession, and the meem is inserted to convey this meaning in an intensive sense. Some said: al-Baqi is that which has no beginning to its existence and no end to its existence. And it is said: al-Baqi is that which in its duration is upon the description that is in its duration. And it is said: He is the First without beginning, and the Last without end.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of names indicating the modality of existence: permanence (dawam). Under dawam, the author lists four names: al-Qadim, al-Azali, al-Abadi, and al-Baqi, with additional discussion of sarmad. For al-Qadim (the Eternal), he defines it as that which has no beginning. He notes that the term can also be used for something with a long duration, but the intended meaning here is the real pre-eternity. He then refutes two incorrect views: (1) that qidam is a negative attribute (sifah salbiyyah) — he argues that it is actually the negation of prior non-existence, and the negation of negation is an affirmation, so it is positive. (2) that qidam is a separate attribute inherent in God — if so, that attribute would itself be eternal, requiring another attribute ad infinitum, which is impossible (tasalsul). Thus, qidam is not a separate attribute but a description of God's existence having no beginning. Next, al-Azali is mentioned as a distinct name, though not elaborated here except to say it differs from al-Qadim interpretation. Then al-Abadi (Everlasting) means no end and impossibility of non-existence. Then al-Baqi (Enduring) is defined with two opinions: existing for more than one time, or free from non-existence entirely (which applies only to God). The author then connects these to the nature of the Necessary Existent: whatever is Wajib al-Wujud is necessarily ever-existing. If we consider its permanence in the past, it is called qidam and azal; if in the future, it is called abad and sarmad. Sarmad is derived from sard (continuity) with an inserted meem for intensification. The passage concludes with three additional sayings about al-Baqi: that it has no beginning or end; that it is consistent in its duration; and that it is the First without beginning and the Last without end.

Key Terms
القديم
القدم
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Qadim is a negative attribute (sifah salbiyyah) or a separate attribute inherent in God.
Correction: The author argues it is positive because it negates prior non-existence, and negation of negation is affirmation. Also, it cannot be a separate attribute due to infinite regress.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the view that al-Qadim is a negative attribute?
A) Because negative attributes cannot apply to God.
B) Because it is the negation of prior non-existence, and the negation of negation is an affirmation.
C) Because it implies a separate attribute of qidam.
D) Because the Quran uses it positively.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadim is a positive description affirming that God has no beginning, not a negative or separate attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not eternal—if He had a beginning—how would that affect your reliance on His promises and His power to sustain you? Do you truly trust in His eternality when you face uncertainty about tomorrow?
?
The author shows that God's eternity is not a passive lack but a positive reality. When you think of God, do you focus more on what He is not (e.g., not limited, not weak) rather than who He is positively? How can you shift your mindset to see His attributes as active affirmations that shape your worship and hope?
?
Consider your own life: you have a clear beginning, and you are acutely aware of time's passing. How does the reality that God has no beginning challenge your sense of self-sufficiency? Are there areas where you live as if you are the center of your own story, forgetting that you depend on the Eternal One who has no beginning?

Al-Azali, Al-Abadi, and Al-Baqi: Names of Permanence
Author defines three more names of permanence: azali, abadi, baqi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of al-Azali, al-Abadi, and al-Baqi as distinct names of God's permanence.

Core Idea
Al-Abadi means no end, al-Baqi means free from non-existence entirely; al-Azali is distinct from qidam.

After discussing al-Qadim, the author moves to three more names: al-Azali, al-Abadi, and al-Baqi. While al-Azali is mentioned briefly as different from qidam, al-Abadi and al-Baqi are defined with various opinions. This lesson covers these names and clarifies their meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on azali, abadi, and baqi
Read the Arabic text and translation. Note the definitions and the differing opinions.
Pay attention to the distinction between azali and qadim.
2
Understand al-Abadi
Al-Abadi means no end and impossibility of non-existence. It is about the future.
3
Understand al-Baqi
There are two opinions: one says existing for more than one time, another says free from non-existence entirely. The latter applies only to God.
The author seems to favor the second.
4
Note the distinction from azali
Al-Azali is different from qadim, but the author doesn't elaborate. It may refer to pre-eternity without beginning.
This term is often synonymous with qadim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوع الثاني من كيفيات الوجود فهو الدوام . وفيه أنواع من الأسماء : الإسم الأول : القديم . واعلم أن القديم هو الذي لا أول لوجوده وقد يراد به : الذي طالت مدة وجوده. قال تعالى : ( إنك لفي ضلالك القديم ) (1) وقال : ( حق عاد كالعرجون القديم ) (2) وقد دللنا على أنه - سبحانه - قديم أزلي . وقال بعضهم : القدم صفة سلبية . وهو خطأ . لأنه عبارة عن نفي العدم السابق ، ونفي النفي ثبوت . ومنهم من قال : إنه قديم بقدم هو صفة قائمة به . وهو أيضاً باطل . وإلا لكان ذلك القدم قديماً بقدم آخر ، ولزم التسلسل ، وهو محال . الثاني : الأزلي . وهو غير ما ذكرناه في تفسير القدم . الإسم الثالث : الأبدي . ومعناه أنه لا آخر له ، ولا يصير معدوماً بعد وجوده البتة . الإسم الرابع : الباقي ) قال تعالى في ( طه ) : ( والله خير وأبقى ) (3) ومنهم من قال : هو الذي وجد أكثر من زمان واحد . ومنهم من قال : الباقي هو الذي يكون مبرأ من العدم من جميع الوجوه . وعلى هذا التقدير ، فالباقي ليس إلا الله . واعلم أنه تعالى واجب الوجود لذاته ، وكل ما كان كذلك كان دائم الوجود ، فإن اعتبرنا دوامه في الماضي (4) ، فهو القدم والأزل ، وإن اعتبرنا دوامه في المستقبل فهو الأبد والسرمد ، ولفظ السرمد اشتقاقه من السرد ، وهو الاستمرار والتعاقب ، وأدخل فيه الميم ليفيد هذا المعنى على سبيل المبالغة . وقال بعضهم : الباقي هو الذي لا ابتداء لوجوده ، ولا انتهاء لوجوده . وقيل : الباقي الذي يكون في أمده على الوصف الذي في أمده . وقيل : هو الأول بلا ابتداء ، والآخر بلا انتهاء
As for the second type of the modalities of existence, it is permanence (dawam). In it are types of names: The first name: al-Qadim (the Eternal). Know that al-Qadim is that which has no beginning to its existence, and sometimes it is used to mean that which has a long duration of existence. He, exalted, said: 'Indeed, you are in your old error' (12:95) and said: 'The remains of 'Ad like an old palm stump' (54:39). And we have demonstrated that He—glorified—is Qadim (eternal) and Azali (pre-eternal). Some said: al-Qidam (eternity) is a negative attribute. That is an error because it is an expression of negating prior non-existence, and the negation of negation is affirmation. And some said: He is Qadim by a qidam that is an attribute subsisting in Him. That is also false, because then that qidam would be qadim by another qidam, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible. The second: al-Azali (the Pre-Eternal). It is different from what we mentioned in the interpretation of al-Qidam. The third: al-Abadi (the Everlasting). Its meaning is that there is no end to Him, and He will never become non-existent after His existence at all. The fourth: al-Baqi (the Enduring). He, exalted, said in Surah Taha: 'And Allah is better and more enduring' (20:73). Among them are those who said: He is that which exists for more than one time. Among them are those who said: al-Baqi is that which is free from non-existence in all respects. According to this estimation, al-Baqi is none but Allah. Know that He, exalted, is Wajib al-Wujud li-Dhatihi (Necessary of Existence in Himself), and everything that is so is ever-existing. If we consider its permanence in the past, it is al-Qidam (eternity) and al-Azal (pre-eternity), and if we consider its permanence in the future, it is al-Abad (everlastingness) and al-Sarmad (perpetuity). The word sarmad is derived from al-sard, which is continuity and succession, and the meem is inserted to convey this meaning in an intensive sense. Some said: al-Baqi is that which has no beginning to its existence and no end to its existence. And it is said: al-Baqi is that which in its duration is upon the description that is in its duration. And it is said: He is the First without beginning, and the Last without end.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of names indicating the modality of existence: permanence (dawam). Under dawam, the author lists four names: al-Qadim, al-Azali, al-Abadi, and al-Baqi, with additional discussion of sarmad. For al-Qadim (the Eternal), he defines it as that which has no beginning. He notes that the term can also be used for something with a long duration, but the intended meaning here is the real pre-eternity. He then refutes two incorrect views: (1) that qidam is a negative attribute (sifah salbiyyah) — he argues that it is actually the negation of prior non-existence, and the negation of negation is an affirmation, so it is positive. (2) that qidam is a separate attribute inherent in God — if so, that attribute would itself be eternal, requiring another attribute ad infinitum, which is impossible (tasalsul). Thus, qidam is not a separate attribute but a description of God's existence having no beginning. Next, al-Azali is mentioned as a distinct name, though not elaborated here except to say it differs from al-Qadim interpretation. Then al-Abadi (Everlasting) means no end and impossibility of non-existence. Then al-Baqi (Enduring) is defined with two opinions: existing for more than one time, or free from non-existence entirely (which applies only to God). The author then connects these to the nature of the Necessary Existent: whatever is Wajib al-Wujud is necessarily ever-existing. If we consider its permanence in the past, it is called qidam and azal; if in the future, it is called abad and sarmad. Sarmad is derived from sard (continuity) with an inserted meem for intensification. The passage concludes with three additional sayings about al-Baqi: that it has no beginning or end; that it is consistent in its duration; and that it is the First without beginning and the Last without end.

Key Terms
الأزلي
الأبدي
الباقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think al-Baqi applies to anything that lasts, like mountains.
Correction: The author clarifies that in its true sense, al-Baqi means free from non-existence completely, which is only true of God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does al-Baqi mean in its strictest sense?
A) Existing for more than one time.
B) Being free from non-existence in all respects.
C) Having no beginning.
D) Having a long duration.

Key Takeaway
Al-Abadi means no end, al-Baqi (strictly) means free from non-existence entirely—only true of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that only al-Baqi is absolutely free from non-existence. What are you clinging to in your life that will inevitably perish? Can you let go of it today and rely on Him alone?
?
Al-Abadi means no end for Allah. Does your fear of death or loss reflect a hidden assumption that something other than Allah will last? Repent of that misplaced trust.
?
The author distinguishes al-Azali from qidam. Have you confused Allah's attributes with each other, and does your worship truly differentiate His unique perfections? Commit to learning His names with precision.

The Unifying Principle: Wajib al-Wujud and the Names of Permanence
Author unifies the names of permanence through the concept of Wajib al-Wujud and explains sarmad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of Wajib al-Wujud unifies the names of permanence, and how al-Sarmad is derived.

Core Idea
God's necessary existence implies permanence in past (qadim/azal) and future (abad/sarmad).

After listing the individual names, the author now connects them to the core doctrine of Tawhid: God is Wajib al-Wujud (Necessary Existent). Because He exists necessarily, He is ever-existing. Considering His permanence in the past gives us qidam and azal; in the future, abad and sarmad. The term sarmad is derived from 'sard' (continuity). The lesson ends with several sayings about al-Baqi, emphasizing that He is the First without beginning and the Last without end.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage connecting permanence to Wajib al-Wujud
Read how the author brings together the names under the concept of necessary existence. Understand the distinction between past and future permanence.
2
Understand al-Sarmad
Learn the derivation: from sard (continuity) with meem for intensity. It is synonymous with abad in the future sense.
Sarmad intensifies the meaning of continuity.
3
Review the concluding sayings
The passage ends with three sayings about al-Baqi. Consider how they reinforce the idea of God's timelessness.
These emphasize no beginning and no end.
4
Reflect on the unity of these names
All five names (qadim, azali, abadi, baqi, sarmad) ultimately describe the same reality: God's eternal existence.

Defining al-Qadim and Refuting Two Misunderstandings
Defines al-Qadim and refutes two incorrect interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of al-Qadim (the Eternal) and why it cannot be a purely negative attribute nor an additional attribute subsisting in God's essence.

Core Idea
Al-Qadim is not merely negative nor an extra attribute; it is the self-subsistent pre-eternality of God.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah tackles a key divine attribute: al-Qadim (the Eternal). After establishing that God is Qadim, the author refutes two incorrect views. Understanding these refutations clarifies the nature of divine attributes in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the Quranic quote at the beginning.
2
Identify the two incorrect views
Spot the two opinions that the author refutes: one that qidam is a negative attribute, and another that it is an additional attribute.
3
Understand the argument against the first view
The author argues that qidam is the negation of prior non-existence, and negation of negation is affirmation, so it is not purely negative.
4
Understand the argument against the second view
If qidam were an additional attribute, it would itself need to be eternal, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible.
This is a classic argument in kalam.
5
Reflect on the positive meaning of al-Qadim
Conclude that al-Qadim is a positive attribute of God's essence, not a mere negation nor an added quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القديم) (۱) وقال : حق عاد كالعرجون القديم ((٢) وقد دللنا على أنه - سبحانه - قديم أزلي
وقال بعضهم : القدم صفة سلبية . وهو خطأ . لأنه عبارة عن نفي العدم السابق ، ونفي النفي ثبوت .
ومنهم من قال : إنه قديم بقدم هو صفة قائمة به . وهو أيضاً باطل . وإلا لكان ذلك القدم قديماً بقدم آخر ، ولزم التسلسل ، وهو محال .
Al-Qadim (the Eternal). He said: 'It returned like the old palm stalk.' And we have proven that He - glorified is He - is Qadim, Azali. And some said: Qidam is a negative attribute. That is a mistake. Because it is an expression of the negation of prior non-existence, and negation of negation is affirmation. And among them is he who said: He is Qadim by a qidam that is an attribute subsisting in Him. That too is false. Otherwise that qidam would be qadim by another qidam, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Qadim and refutes two incorrect interpretations. The author begins by quoting a Quranic verse and stating that they have already proven God to be Qadim and Azali. Then two wrong views are presented and refuted. The first is that qidam is a negative attribute. The author argues that qidam is the negation of prior non-existence, and since negation of negation is an affirmation, it is not purely negative. The second view is that qidam is an attribute subsisting in God's essence. This leads to infinite regress because if qidam were an attribute, it would itself need another qidam to be eternal, and so on ad infinitum, which is impossible.

Key Terms
قديم
قدم
أزلي
صفة سلبية
نفي العدم
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qidam is merely a negative attribute (negation of non-existence) without positive meaning.
Correction: Qidam negates prior non-existence, but the negation of negation yields positive affirmation, so it is not purely negative.

Checkpoint
Why is the view that qidam is a negative attribute considered wrong?
A) Because it implies God is not eternal.
B) Because negation of negation is affirmation, so it is not purely negative.
C) Because it leads to infinite regress.
D) Because it contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadim is not a negative attribute nor an additional attribute; it is the necessary pre-eternality of God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's pre-eternality is not merely a 'negative' concept but a positive affirmation of His infinite existence, how does this change the way you understand His sovereignty over your life? In what specific moments do you still act as if God's existence is limited by time or circumstances? Commit to one change today that reflects your belief in an eternal, unending God.
?
The refutation of qidam as an added attribute prevents us from imagining God's attributes as separate pieces. Examine your own heart: do you ever treat God's mercy, justice, or power as if they are independent qualities, rather than aspects of His one perfect essence? How might this fragment your worship? Write down one way you will unify your understanding of God's attributes.
?
The argument against infinite regress shows that only God can be eternal without cause. Think of something you rely on—money, relationships, health—that you unconsciously treat as if it will never end. If that thing were taken away tomorrow, would your faith in the Eternal God remain unshaken? What practical step can you take today to transfer your security from the temporal to the Eternal?

Meanings of al-Azali, al-Abadi, and al-Baqi in Relation to Wajib al-Wujud
Defines al-Azali, al-Abadi, al-Baqi, and connects them to necessity of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meanings of the divine names al-Azali, al-Abadi, and al-Baqi, and how they relate to the necessity of Allah's existence (Wajib al-Wujud).

Core Idea
Past eternity and future eternity are two distinct aspects of Allah's necessary existence.

This passage from al-Matālib al-ʿĀliyah clarifies three important divine names: al-Azali (eternal without beginning), al-Abadi (eternal without end), and al-Baqi (the Everlasting). The author also connects these concepts to the foundational notion that Allah is Wajib al-Wujud (Necessary Being) by His essence, which entails perpetual existence in both past and future.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author distinguishes between al-Azali and al-Qadim, defines al-Abadi, and presents varying opinions on al-Baqi.
The passage uses technical terms that may be unfamiliar.
2
Identify the connection to Wajib al-Wujud
Find the sentence that links being Wajib al-Wujud to perpetual existence. Observe that perpetuity in the past is called Qidam/Azal, and in the future is called Abad/Sarmad.
This is the central point of the passage.
3
Reflect on the definitions of al-Baqi
Notice that multiple definitions are given for al-Baqi. Consider which one the author seems to endorse (being free from non-existence in all respects, exclusive to Allah).
Also note the distinction between enduring by His own essence vs. by His sustaining.

الإسم الثاني : الأزلي . وهو غير ما ذكرناه في تفسير القدم الإسم الثالث : الأبدي . ومعناه أنه لا آخر له ، ولا يصير معدوماً بعد وجوده البتة
الإسم الرابع : الباقي ) قال تعالى في ( طه ) : ( والله خير وأبقى ) (۱) ومنهم من قال : هو الذي وجد أكثر من زمان واحد . ومنهم من قال : الباقي هو الذي يكون مبرأ من العدم من جميع الوجوه . وعلى هذا التقدير ، فالباقي ليس إلا الله
واعلم أنه تعالى واجب الوجود لذاته ، وكل ما كان كذلك كان دائم الوجود ، فإن اعتبرنا دوامه ) في الماضي ( () ، فهو القدم والأزل ، وإن اعتبرنا دوامه في المستقبل فهو الأبد والسرمد ، ولفظ السرمد اشتقاقه من السرد ، وهو الاستمرار والتعاقب ، وأدخل فيه الميم ليفيد هذا المعنى على سبيل المبالغة
وقال بعضهم : الباقي هو الذي لا ابتداء لوجوده ، ولا انتهاء لوجوده
وقيل : الباقي الذي يكون في أمده على الوصف الذي في أمده
وقيل : هو الأول بلا ابتداء ، والآخر بلا انتهاء
وقيل : الحق باقي ببقائه ، والخلق باقي بإبقائه .
The second name: al-Azali. And it is different from what we mentioned about the interpretation of al-Qadim. The third name: al-Abadi. Its meaning: there is no end to Him, and He never becomes non-existent after His existence. The fourth name: al-Baqi. He said in (Taha): 'And Allah is better and more enduring.' And some said: He is the one who existed for more than one time. And some said: al-Baqi is the one who is free from non-existence in all respects. On this estimation, al-Baqi is none but Allah. And know that He, exalted, is Wajib al-Wujud by His essence, and everything that is such is perpetual in existence. If we consider its perpetuity in the past, it is al-Qidam and al-Azal; if we consider its perpetuity in the future, it is al-Abad and al-Sarmad. The word al-Sarmad is derived from al-Sard, which is continuity and succession, and the letter mim is inserted to emphasize this meaning. And some said: al-Baqi is the one whose existence has no beginning and no end. And it was said: al-Baqi is the one who in his duration is the same as he was in his duration. And it was said: He is the First without beginning and the Last without end. And it was said: The Truth is enduring by His enduringness, while creation is enduring by His sustaining.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines three divine names: al-Azali, al-Abadi, and al-Baqi, and relates them to the concept of Wajib al-Wujud. The author first distinguishes al-Azali from al-Qadim, then defines al-Abadi as having no end. For al-Baqi, he mentions two opinions: one that it means existing for more than one time, and another that it means completely free from non-existence, making it exclusive to Allah. Then the author states that because Allah is Wajib al-Wujud by His essence, He is perpetual. Perpetuity in the past is called Qidam or Azal; perpetuity in the future is called Abad or Sarmad, with Sarmad derived from 'Sard' meaning continuity. Additional definitions of al-Baqi are given: having no beginning or end, being consistent in duration, being First and Last, and the distinction that the Truth is enduring by His own endurance whereas creatures endure by His sustaining.

باقي (Baqi)

سرمد (Sarmad)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'eternal' is a single simple attribute without distinction between past and future.
Correction: The passage shows that Islamic theology distinguishes two types of eternality: azal (no beginning) and abad (no end), both necessitated by being Wajib al-Wujud.

Key Takeaway
The eternal attributes of Allah are rationally tied to His being Wajib al-Wujud, with past and future eternality distinguished as azal and abad.

The Divine Name al-Warith: God as the Inheritor
Defines al-Warith as God remaining after the annihilation of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of the divine name al-Warith as referring to God's eternal endurance after the annihilation of creation.

Core Idea
Allah is the Inheritor who remains when all else perishes.

In this lesson, we explore the fifth divine name mentioned in the text: al-Warith. The name might initially seem to imply that Allah inherits from someone, but the author clarifies its true meaning. Through Quranic verses and rational explanation, we will see that al-Warith signifies that Allah alone endures after every creature has passed away.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Note the Quranic verses cited and the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the Quranic verses
The author cites three verses. Notice how they support the concept of Allah as the ultimate inheritor.
3
Understand the explanation
The text explains that although Allah owns all things, He grants temporary ownership to servants. When they die, possessions return to the true Owner. This clarifies why Allah is called al-Warith.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that al-Warith means Allah remains after the annihilation of creation. Reflect on the verse about the Kingdom belonging to Allah alone.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Some may think 'inheritor' implies receiving property from the dead. The text corrects this: it means eternal survival.
Misconception is addressed in the common_misunderstanding field.

Thus, the name al-Warith signifies that God endures after the annihilation of all creatures. The author concludes with a Quranic verse emphasizing that ultimate ownership belongs to God alone.

Al-Awwal, al-Akhir, al-Zahir, al-Batin: Cause-Effect Names
Explains four divine names via cause-effect and begins proof for al-Awwal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names al-Awwal, al-Akhir, al-Zahir, and al-Batin as derived from the cause-effect relationship, and you will see the beginning of a rational proof for why Allah is al-Awwal.

Core Idea
Allah is First, Last, Manifest, and Hidden through the cause-effect relationship.

In this passage, the author explains four of Allah's beautiful names using a simple but profound analogy: the relationship between a cause and its effect. By understanding how a cause is prior and manifest through its effect, we can grasp why Allah is called the First, the Last, the Manifest, and the Hidden. The passage then begins a logical argument to prove that Allah is indeed the First, based on the existence of multiple beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its English translation. Notice that the proof for al-Awwal is cut off mid-sentence.
The translation uses 'effective cause' for mu'aththir and 'effect' for athar.
2
Understand the cause-effect analogy for First and Last
Focus on how 'descending' from cause to effect makes the cause 'First', and 'ascending' from effect to cause makes it 'Last'. This is not temporal but logical priority.
Allah is First when we look from Him to creation, and Last when we look from creation back to Him.
3
Understand the cause-effect analogy for Manifest and Hidden
The effect indicates the existence of the cause (so cause is manifest) but does not reveal the cause's essence (so cause is hidden). Apply this to Allah: His creatures show He exists but not His true reality.
Al-Zahir means manifest through evidence, al-Batin means hidden in essence.
4
Note the beginning of the proof for al-Awwal
The author announces an investigation into the secrets of the four terms and starts a proof: given many existents, they are either all possible, all necessary, or a mix. The argument is incomplete here.
This proof continues on the next page.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم السادس : الأول والآخر والظاهر والباطن . واعلم أن المؤثر متقدم بالذات ، وبالعلية على الأثر ، فإذا ابتدأت بالمؤثر ونزلت منه إلى الأثر ، كان المؤثر هو الأول ، وإذا صعدت من الأثر إلى المؤثر كان المؤثر هو الآخر وبهذا الطريق يظهر أنه لما كان مبدأ أولاً لجميع الممكنات ، لزم كونه أولاً عند النزول (1) إلى الممكنات ، وكونه آخراً عند الصعود من الممكنات ، ويكون ظاهراً لأن الأثر يدل على وجود المؤثر ، ويكون باطناً لأن العلم بالمعلول لا يفيد العلم بماهية العلة البتة ، وإنما الغاية القصوى فيه : أن يدل على وجود العلة ) فهو ظاهر لدلالة مخلوقاته على وجوده ) (*) وباطناً لقصور مخلوقاته عن الدلالة على ماهيته المعينة ، وحقيقته المخصوصة. ويجب البحث ههنا عن أسرار هذه الألفاظ الأربعة (٦) . أما الأول : فهو الفرد السابق ، والذي يدل على أنه كذلك : أنه لا شك في وجود الموجودات الكثيرة . فنقول : إنها إما أن تكون بأسرها ممكنة الوجود، أو واجبة الوجود، أو يكون بعضها ممكناً وبعضها
The sixth name: al-Awwal, al-Akhir, al-Zahir, al-Batin. And know that the effective cause is prior in essence and in causality to the effect. So if you begin with the cause and descend from it to the effect, the cause is al-Awwal (the First); and if you ascend from the effect to the cause, the cause is al-Akhir (the Last). By this method it becomes clear that since He was a first origin for all possible things, it necessarily follows that He is al-Awwal when descending to the possible things, and He is al-Akhir when ascending from the possible things. And He is al-Zahir (the Manifest) because the effect indicates the existence of the cause, and He is al-Batin (the Hidden) because knowledge of the effect does not yield knowledge of the essence of the cause at all; the utmost limit in it is that it indicates the existence of the cause. So He is al-Zahir due to the indication of His creatures of His existence, and al-Batin due to the inadequacy of His creatures to indicate His specific essence and particular reality. And here it is necessary to investigate the secrets of these four terms. As for al-Awwal: He is the unique prior. And what indicates that He is such is that there is no doubt about the existence of many existents. We say: They are either all of them possible of existence, or necessary of existence, or some of them possible and some...
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage explains the four divine names al-Awwal, al-Akhir, al-Zahir, al-Batin using the relationship between cause (mu'aththir) and effect (athar). The cause is prior; when we descend from cause to effect, the cause is 'First'; when we ascend from effect to cause, the cause is 'Last'. Since God is the origin of all possible things, He is First in descent and Last in ascent. He is Manifest (al-Zahir) because effects indicate the existence of the cause, and Hidden (al-Batin) because effects do not reveal the essence of the cause. The author then announces an investigation into the secrets of these terms and begins a proof for God being al-Awwal: given the existence of many existents, they must be either all possible, all necessary, or a mixture. The argument is cut off at the end of the page.

آخر (Akhir)
ظاهر (Zahir)
باطن (Batin)
مؤثر (cause)
علية (causality)
ماهية (essence)
معلول (effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking 'First' and 'Last' imply temporal priority and posteriority in time.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'First' and 'Last' are based on the cause-effect relationship, not time. Allah is prior logically and causally, not temporally.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Through the lens of cause and effect, we understand that Allah is the First (origin), the Last (ultimate), the Manifest (evidenced by creation), and the Hidden (beyond full comprehension by creation).

?

Explanation of the Divine Name al-Wārith
The author defines and explains the divine name al-Wārith (the Inheritor).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine name al-Wārith signifies Allah's eternal ownership after all creation perishes.

Core Idea
Allah is the Inheritor: He remains after all else fades.

This passage explains why Allah is called al-Wārith. It clarifies that Allah’s inheritance is not like human inheritance; rather, He is the everlasting owner to whom all things return when their temporary owners pass away. The passage opens with a general principle about Allah’s eternal subsistence versus creation’s contingent persistence, then cites Quranic verses and offers an interpretation grounded in the return of ownership.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author’s own rhythm and argument come through.
2
Note the opening principle
Observe the distinction made at the beginning: Allah remains by His own self‑subsistence, while creation remains only by His preservation. This sets the stage for the name al‑Wārith.
The term 'ibqā' (preservation) is key to understanding contingent existence.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
Two verses are cited: Q19:40 and Q57:10. Reflect on how these verses use the concept of inheritance in relation to Allah. Notice that the first speaks of Allah inheriting the earth and its inhabitants, and the second states that to Allah belongs the inheritance of the heavens and the earth.
4
Understand the explanation
The author clarifies that although Allah is the true owner of all contingent things, He grants temporary ownership to some servants. When the servant dies and Allah alone remains, ownership returns to its source. This return is what is meant by 'inheritance' in reference to Allah.
The final verse (Q40:16) emphasizes that all dominion returns to Allah on that Day.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : الحق باقي ببقائه ، والخلق باقي بإبقائه . الإسم الخامس : الوارث . قال تعالى : ( إنا نحن نرث الأرض ومن عليها ) (۱) وقال : والله ميراث السموات والأرض ) (۲) واعلم أن مالك جميع الممكنات هو الله - سبحانه - ولكنه يفضله جعل بعض الأشياء ملكاً لبعض عباده ، فالعبد إذا مات ، ويقي الحق - سبحانه - عادت تلك الأملاك إلى المالك ، فكان هذا هو المراد من كونه وارثاً . فالحاصل : أن المراد من كونه وارثاً : كونه باقياً بعد فناء الخلق ، كما قال : ( لمن الملك اليوم ؟ الله التواحد الحقيقي القهار ) (۳)
And it is said: The Truth (Allah) remains by His self-subsistence, and the creation remains by His preservation. The fifth name: al-Wārith (the Inheritor). He said, Most High: (Indeed, We will inherit the earth and whoever is on it) (1) and He said: (And to Allah belongs the inheritance of the heavens and the earth) (2). Know that the owner of all contingent things is Allah—glorified be He—but by His grace He made some things property for some of His servants. So when the servant dies, and the Truth—glorified be He—remains, those properties return to the [true] owner. This is what is meant by His being Inheritor. The upshot: that what is meant by His being Inheritor is His being everlasting after the annihilation of creation, as He said: (Whose is the dominion today? It is Allah, the One, the Subduer) (3).
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the divine name al-Wārith (the Inheritor). It begins by noting a general principle: Allah (al-Ḥaqq) is eternal by His own essence (baqā' bi-baqā'ihi), while creatures persist only through His preservation (ibqā'). Then it identifies al-Wārith as the fifth name among the names being discussed. Two Quranic verses are cited as proof: 'Indeed, We will inherit the earth and whoever is on it' (19:40) and 'To Allah belongs the inheritance of the heavens and the earth' (57:10). The author explains that although Allah is the true owner of all contingent things (al-mumkināt), He graciously grants temporary ownership to some servants. When the servant dies and Allah alone remains, those possessions revert to the original owner. Therefore, the meaning of al-Wārith is that Allah is the ever-lasting One after the annihilation of all creatures, as confirmed by the verse: 'Whose is the dominion today? It is Allah, the One, the Subduer' (40:16). This clarifies that there is no actual inheritance from someone deceased; rather, ownership returns to its eternal source.

البقاء
الفناء
الإبقاء
الممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Allah inherits from a deceased person.
Correction: The passage clarifies it means He is the eternal owner after all temporal owners perish.

Checkpoint

A) He inherits property from His deceased servants.

C) He gives inheritance to His chosen servants.
D) He is the sole inheritor of material wealth.

Key Takeaway
The name al-Wārith teaches that Allah alone is eternal and all ownership ultimately returns to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson says everything you own is only temporarily yours by Allah's grace. When you hold onto your wealth, status, or health, do you truly see yourself as a trustee or as the owner? What would change in how you live today if you fully accepted that it all must return to its true Owner?

Allah remains after all else fades. Your body, your loved ones, your achievements—all will perish. Does that reality unsettle you, or does it give you peace? If it unsettles you, what does that reveal about where you are placing your security?
?
Creation exists only by Allah's preservation (ibqā). Right now, in this moment, your breath and heartbeat are sustained directly by Him. Does this truth make you more grateful and humble, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? Commit to one concrete act of turning to Him today because of this.

Four Divine Names: Awwal, Akhir, Zahir, Batin
The author defines and explains the divine names al-Awwal, al-Ākhir, aẓ-Ẓāhir, al-Bāṭin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine names al-Awwal, al-Ākhir, aẓ-Ẓāhir, and al-Bāṭin are derived from the cause-effect relationship between Allah and creation.

Core Idea
Allah is First as origin, Last as goal, Manifest through effects, Hidden in essence.

This passage explores four profound divine names that describe Allah's relationship to creation through the lens of causality. By understanding these names, you will see how Allah is both intimately known through His creations and utterly transcendent beyond their grasp.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic and English to get the overall argument. Notice the key terms like المؤثر (agent) and الأثر (effect).
The passage is compact; read it twice.
2
Understand the cause-effect framework
Grasp that the agent (cause) is prior in essence and causality to the effect. This priority is not temporal but logical.
The terms مبدأ (principle) and علة (cause) are used synonymously.
3
Derive Awwal and Akhir from perspective
When descending from cause to effect, the cause is 'First' (الأول). When ascending from effect to cause, the cause is 'Last' (الآخر).
This shows Allah is both origin and ultimate goal.
4
Understand Zahir and Batin
He is Manifest (الظاهر) because effects indicate the existence of the cause. He is Hidden (الباطن) because effects do not reveal the cause's essence.
Creatures prove His existence but not His specific nature.
5
Reflect on the implications
These names balance Allah's immanence (through creation) and transcendence (beyond creation's grasp).
This guards against anthropomorphism and agnosticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم السادس : الأول والآخر والظاهر والباطن . واعلم أن المؤثر متقدم بالذات ، وبالعلية على الأثر ، فإذا ابتدأت بالمؤثر ونزلت منه إلى الأثر ، كان المؤثر هو الأول ، وإذا صعدت من الأثر إلى المؤثر كان المؤثر هو الآخر وبهذا الطريق يظهر أنه لما كان مبدأ أولاً لجميع الممكنات ، لزم كونه أولاً عند النزول إلى الممكنات ، وكونه آخراً عند الصعود من الممكنات ، ويكون ظاهراً لأن الأثر يدل على وجود المؤثر ، ويكون باطناً لأن العلم بالمعلول لا يفيد العلم بماهية العلة البتة ، وإنما الغاية القصوى فيه : أن يدل على وجود العلة ) فهو ظاهر لدلالة مخلوقاته على وجوده ) وباطناً لقصور مخلوقاته عن الدلالة على ماهيته المعينة ، وحقيقته المخصوصة.
The sixth name: al-Awwal (the First), al-Ākhir (the Last), aẓ-Ẓāhir (the Manifest), al-Bāṭin (the Hidden). Know that the agent (al-mu'aththir) is prior in essence and in causality to the effect. So if you start with the agent and descend from it to the effect, the agent is the First; and if you ascend from the effect to the agent, the agent is the Last. In this way it appears that since He is a principle (mabda') and first for all contingent things, it necessarily follows that He is First in descending to contingent things, and Last in ascending from contingent things. And He is Manifest because the effect indicates the existence of the agent, and He is Hidden because knowledge of the effect does not yield knowledge of the essence of the cause at all; rather, the utmost end in it is that it indicates the existence of the cause. So He is Manifest through the indication of His creatures on His existence, and Hidden due to the inability of His creatures to indicate His specific essence and particular reality.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage defines and explains four divine names: al-Awwal (the First), al-Ākhir (the Last), aẓ-Ẓāhir (the Manifest), and al-Bāṭin (the Hidden). The author uses the relationship between cause (mu'aththir/agent) and effect (athar). The cause is prior in essence and causality to the effect. When one considers the descent from the cause to the effect, the cause is called 'First' (al-Awwal). When one ascends from the effect back to the cause, the cause is called 'Last' (al-Ākhir). Since Allah is the ultimate principle (mabda') and first cause of all contingent things, He is necessarily First in relation to them (as their origin) and Last (as the goal of return). Next, He is Manifest (Ẓāhir) because the effect (creation) proves the existence of the cause (the Creator). However, He is Hidden (Bāṭin) because knowledge of the effect does not provide knowledge of the essence (māhiyya) of the cause; the effect only indicates the existence of the cause, not what it is in itself. Thus, Allah is Manifest through the evidence of His creatures indicating His existence, and Hidden because creatures cannot convey His specific essence and particular reality.

Key Terms
الأول
الآخر
الظاهر
الباطن
المؤثر
الأثر
العلة
المعلول
المبدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these names refer to temporal priority (first in time) or physical hiddenness (like something physically concealed).
Correction: The passage clarifies they are relational: causal priority (logical priority) and transcendence of essence. Awwal means prior in causality, not time. Batin means inaccessible in essence, not physically hidden.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Allah called aẓ-Ẓāhir (the Manifest)?
A) Because His essence is directly visible to everyone.
B) Because His effects (creatures) indicate His existence.
C) Because He appears in the forms of His creation.
D) Because He is the most apparent being in the universe.

Key Takeaway
The passage teaches that Allah is known through His creation only as far as existence, not essence, balancing manifestation and hiddenness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the world's order, do you stop at affirming Allah's existence, or do you find yourself trying to grasp His essence? How does today's passage expose the limits of your intellectual journey, and what will you do to humbly accept those limits?
?
If Allah is the Last as the goal of return, what direction is your life currently heading? Are you actively ascending from effects to the Cause, or are you distracted by the effects themselves? What one change can you make today to realign your focus?
?
The passage says that creatures cannot indicate Allah's specific essence. How does this truth affect the way you approach worship and dua? Does it make you feel distant or draw you to seek Him in a different way? What will you do this week to seek nearness to a God whose essence remains hidden?

Al-Quddus: The Holy
Explanation of al-Quddus with derivation and scriptural examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Quddus, its derivation from purity, and how it signifies God's transcendence over deficiencies in essence and attributes.

Core Idea
Al-Quddus means the One absolutely pure from all imperfections.

This lesson introduces the first name in the third category: al-Quddus. The author derives it from al-quds (purity) and illustrates its usage in the Quran and tradition, providing a clear foundation for understanding divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and derivation
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting how al-Quddus is derived from quds (purity) and the examples given (Bayt al-Maqdis, Paradise, Jibril).
The derivation establishes the core meaning of purity.
2
Identify the examples
List the three examples used to illustrate purity: Bayt al-Maqdis, Hazirat al-Quds, and Ruh al-Quds. Notice how each relates to freedom from sin or defilement.
These examples reinforce the concept of sanctification (taqdis).
3
Reflect on transcendence in essence and attributes
Understand that al-Quddus negates any deficiency in God's essence and attributes, as the author will later contrast it with al-Salam.
This name is about God's intrinsic perfection.

القسم الثالث من الأسماء : الألفاظ الدالة على التنزيه والتقديس : وهي كثيرة : الإسم الأول : القدوس . قال تعالى : ( هو الله لا إله إلا هو الملك القدوس ) وقال : ( يسبح الله ما في السموات وما في الأرض الملك القدوس ) واعلم . أن القدوس مشتق من القدس وهو الطهارة ، ولهذا يقال : بيت المقدس، أي المكان الذي يتطهر فيه من الذنوب . وقيل للجنة : حظيرة القدس . لطهارتها من آفات الدنيا . ويقال جبريل - عليه السلام - روح القدس . لأنه طاهر عن الخيانة في التبليغ والوحي . قال تعالى حكاية عن الملائكة : ( ونحن نسبح بحمدك ونقدس لك ) الإسم الثاني : السلام. والمراد منه : ذو السلام . والفرق بين القدوس والسلام : أن القدوس هو المنزه عن النقائص في ذاته وفي صفاته . والسلام هو المنزه عن النقائص في أفعاله . الإسم الثالث : العزيز . وهو الذي يقل وجوده ، وتشتد الحاجة إليه ويصعب الوصول إليه ، فما لم تجتمع هذه المعاني الثلاثة في شيء لا يطلق اسم العزيز عليه . فكم من شيء يقل وجوده ، ولكن لا يحتاج إليه ، فلا يسمى عزيزاً . وقد يكون بحيث لا مثل له ، ويحتاج إليه جداً ، ولكن يسهل الوصول إليه فلا يسمى عزيزاً كالشمس فإنه لا مثل لها ويعظم الانتفاع بها ولكنها لا توصف بالعزة ، بسبب أنه لا يصعب الوصول إليها ، أما إذا اجتمعت المعاني الثلاثة فهناك يوصف بكونه عزيزاً ، ثم في كل واحد من هذه المعاني الثلاثة كمال ونقصان . فالكمال في قلة الوجود أن يرجع إلى واحد ، إذ لا أقل من الواحد ويكون بحيث يستحيل وجود مثله ، وليس هذا إلا الله سبحانه وتعالى . فإن الشمس، وإن كانت واحدة في الوجود،
The third category of names: the terms indicating transcendence and sanctification, and they are many: The first name: al-Quddus. He said, exalted: 'He is Allah, besides whom there is no god, the King, the Holy' (59:23) and said: 'Whatever is in the heavens and whatever is on the earth glorifies Allah, the King, the Holy' (62:1). And know that al-Quddus is derived from al-quds which is purity, and for this reason it is said: Bayt al-Maqdis, i.e. the place in which one is purified from sins. And Paradise is called: Hazirat al-Quds, because of its purity from the afflictions of this world. And it is said: Jibril – peace be upon him – is Ruh al-Quds, because he is pure from betrayal in conveying and revelation. He said, exalted, quoting the angels: 'And we glorify You with praise and sanctify You' (2:30). The second name: al-Salam. What is meant by it is: Possessor of peace. And the difference between al-Quddus and al-Salam is that al-Quddus is the one who is transcendent above deficiencies in His essence and His attributes, while al-Salam is the one who is transcendent above deficiencies in His actions. The third name: al-Aziz. It is that whose existence is rare, and the need for it is intense, and access to it is difficult. So unless these three meanings are combined in a thing, the name Aziz is not applied to it. For how many a thing is rare in existence but not needed, so it is not called Aziz. And it may be such that there is no like of it, and it is greatly needed, but access to it is easy, so it is not called Aziz, like the sun, for there is no like of it and great benefit is derived from it, but it is not described as Aziz, because it is not difficult to access. But when the three meanings are combined, then it is described as being Aziz. Then in each of these three meanings there is perfection and deficiency. Perfection in the rarity of existence is that it reduces to one, since there is nothing less than one, and that it be such that the existence of its like is impossible. And this is not except Allah, glorified and exalted. For the sun, even though it is one in existence,
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the third category of divine names: those denoting transcendence (tanzih) and sanctification (taqdis). The author lists three names: al-Quddus, al-Salam, and al-Aziz. For al-Quddus, he derives it from al-quds (purity) and gives examples: Bayt al-Maqdis (Jerusalem), Hazirat al-Quds (Paradise), and Jibril as Ruh al-Quds, all indicating purity. The name signifies God's purity from deficiencies in essence and attributes. For al-Salam, meaning the Possessor of peace, he distinguishes it from al-Quddus: al-Quddus negates deficiencies in essence and attributes, while al-Salam negates deficiencies in actions. For al-Aziz, he defines it by three conditions: rarity of existence, intense need for it, and difficulty of access. He illustrates that if any condition is missing, the term is not applied (e.g., the sun is unique and beneficial but easily accessible, so not aziz). The perfection of these meanings culminates in God alone, who is one and has no like. The text ends mid-explanation of the sun, indicating incompleteness.

Key Terms
قدوس (Holy)
قدس (purity)
تنزيه (transcendence)
تقديس (sanctification)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Quddus refers only to God's actions or to external purity, but it actually pertains to God's essence and attributes.
Correction: The text explains al-Quddus as transcendent over deficiencies in essence and attributes, as highlighted later when contrasted with al-Salam.

Key Takeaway
Al-Quddus is derived from purity and signifies God's absolute freedom from any imperfection in His essence and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that al-Quddus means absolute purity from all imperfections, what hidden impurities in your character—such as pride, envy, or hypocrisy—do you need to repent from and seek His sanctification to cleanse? Make a concrete intention today to work on one specific impurity, and write down a practical step you will take within the next 24 hours.
?
The angels constantly sanctify Allah, saying 'we glorify You with praise and sanctify You.' How often do you consciously sanctify Allah in your daily life? Commit to saying 'SubhanAllah' at least ten times today with full awareness of His transcendence, and reflect on how this changes your attitude toward your own flaws.
?
Al-Quddus is derived from quds, meaning purity, and is applied to places like Jerusalem and to Gabriel. What does it mean for your own heart to be a place of purity (like Bayt al-Maqdis) for the remembrance of Allah? Identify one attachment to worldly distractions that you can begin to purify yourself from starting today.

Al-Salam: The Peace
Definition of al-Salam and its distinction from al-Quddus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of al-Salam as the Possessor of peace and how it differs from al-Quddus by focusing on God's actions.

Core Idea
Al-Salam means the One free from deficiency in His actions.

The second name, al-Salam, is closely related to al-Quddus but has a distinct scope. While al-Quddus negates defects in God's essence and attributes, al-Salam does the same for His actions. This lesson clarifies that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and distinction
Read the passage for al-Salam, noting that it means 'Possessor of peace' and the explicit contrast with al-Quddus.
The contrast is key to understanding the complementary nature of these names.
2
Understand the difference in scope
Notice that al-Quddus pertains to essence and attributes, while al-Salam pertains to actions. This is a precise theological distinction.
Both names indicate transcendence but in different domains.
3
Reflect on God's actions being perfect
Consider how this name assures that all of God's actions are free from any flaw, injustice, or futility.
This connects to the broader theme of divine perfection.

Al-Aziz: The Mighty
Definition of al-Aziz with three conditions and partial discussion of perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold criterion for the name al-Aziz—rarity, need, and inaccessibility—and how only Allah perfectly embodies it.

Core Idea
Al-Aziz is the One whose existence is unique, desperately needed, and beyond reach.

The third name, al-Aziz, is explained through a set of three conditions that must converge for something to be called 'aziz'. The author provides examples to illustrate and begins discussing how these conditions reach perfection in Allah alone. Note that the passage is cut off, but the core definition and analysis are present.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and three conditions
Carefully read the passage for al-Aziz, identifying the three conditions: rarity of existence, intense need, and difficulty of access.
These conditions are explicitly listed: 'يقل وجوده، وتشتد الحاجة إليه ويصعب الوصول إليه'.
2
Examine the examples
The author gives two counterexamples: things rare but not needed, and the sun (unique and needed but easily accessible). Understand why each fails to be called aziz.
The sun example shows that ease of access negates the name, even if other conditions are met.
3
Understand the perfection in Allah
The passage then discusses how perfection in rarity means being one and having no like. Allah alone has this perfection.
The text ends mid-sentence about the sun, but the point is clear: only Allah perfectly combines all three conditions.

القسم الثالث من الأسماء : الألفاظ الدالة على التنزيه والتقديس : وهي كثيرة : الإسم الأول : القدوس . قال تعالى : ( هو الله لا إله إلا هو الملك القدوس ) وقال : ( يسبح الله ما في السموات وما في الأرض الملك القدوس ) واعلم . أن القدوس مشتق من القدس وهو الطهارة ، ولهذا يقال : بيت المقدس، أي المكان الذي يتطهر فيه من الذنوب . وقيل للجنة : حظيرة القدس . لطهارتها من آفات الدنيا . ويقال جبريل - عليه السلام - روح القدس . لأنه طاهر عن الخيانة في التبليغ والوحي . قال تعالى حكاية عن الملائكة : ( ونحن نسبح بحمدك ونقدس لك ) الإسم الثاني : السلام. والمراد منه : ذو السلام . والفرق بين القدوس والسلام : أن القدوس هو المنزه عن النقائص في ذاته وفي صفاته . والسلام هو المنزه عن النقائص في أفعاله . الإسم الثالث : العزيز . وهو الذي يقل وجوده ، وتشتد الحاجة إليه ويصعب الوصول إليه ، فما لم تجتمع هذه المعاني الثلاثة في شيء لا يطلق اسم العزيز عليه . فكم من شيء يقل وجوده ، ولكن لا يحتاج إليه ، فلا يسمى عزيزاً . وقد يكون بحيث لا مثل له ، ويحتاج إليه جداً ، ولكن يسهل الوصول إليه فلا يسمى عزيزاً كالشمس فإنه لا مثل لها ويعظم الانتفاع بها ولكنها لا توصف بالعزة ، بسبب أنه لا يصعب الوصول إليها ، أما إذا اجتمعت المعاني الثلاثة فهناك يوصف بكونه عزيزاً ، ثم في كل واحد من هذه المعاني الثلاثة كمال ونقصان . فالكمال في قلة الوجود أن يرجع إلى واحد ، إذ لا أقل من الواحد ويكون بحيث يستحيل وجود مثله ، وليس هذا إلا الله سبحانه وتعالى . فإن الشمس، وإن كانت واحدة في الوجود،
The third category of names: the terms indicating transcendence and sanctification, and they are many: The first name: al-Quddus. He said, exalted: 'He is Allah, besides whom there is no god, the King, the Holy' (59:23) and said: 'Whatever is in the heavens and whatever is on the earth glorifies Allah, the King, the Holy' (62:1). And know that al-Quddus is derived from al-quds which is purity, and for this reason it is said: Bayt al-Maqdis, i.e. the place in which one is purified from sins. And Paradise is called: Hazirat al-Quds, because of its purity from the afflictions of this world. And it is said: Jibril – peace be upon him – is Ruh al-Quds, because he is pure from betrayal in conveying and revelation. He said, exalted, quoting the angels: 'And we glorify You with praise and sanctify You' (2:30). The second name: al-Salam. What is meant by it is: Possessor of peace. And the difference between al-Quddus and al-Salam is that al-Quddus is the one who is transcendent above deficiencies in His essence and His attributes, while al-Salam is the one who is transcendent above deficiencies in His actions. The third name: al-Aziz. It is that whose existence is rare, and the need for it is intense, and access to it is difficult. So unless these three meanings are combined in a thing, the name Aziz is not applied to it. For how many a thing is rare in existence but not needed, so it is not called Aziz. And it may be such that there is no like of it, and it is greatly needed, but access to it is easy, so it is not called Aziz, like the sun, for there is no like of it and great benefit is derived from it, but it is not described as Aziz, because it is not difficult to access. But when the three meanings are combined, then it is described as being Aziz. Then in each of these three meanings there is perfection and deficiency. Perfection in the rarity of existence is that it reduces to one, since there is nothing less than one, and that it be such that the existence of its like is impossible. And this is not except Allah, glorified and exalted. For the sun, even though it is one in existence,
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the third category of divine names: those denoting transcendence (tanzih) and sanctification (taqdis). The author lists three names: al-Quddus, al-Salam, and al-Aziz. For al-Quddus, he derives it from al-quds (purity) and gives examples: Bayt al-Maqdis (Jerusalem), Hazirat al-Quds (Paradise), and Jibril as Ruh al-Quds, all indicating purity. The name signifies God's purity from deficiencies in essence and attributes. For al-Salam, meaning the Possessor of peace, he distinguishes it from al-Quddus: al-Quddus negates deficiencies in essence and attributes, while al-Salam negates deficiencies in actions. For al-Aziz, he defines it by three conditions: rarity of existence, intense need for it, and difficulty of access. He illustrates that if any condition is missing, the term is not applied (e.g., the sun is unique and beneficial but easily accessible, so not aziz). The perfection of these meanings culminates in God alone, who is one and has no like. The text ends mid-explanation of the sun, indicating incompleteness.

Key Terms
عزيز (Mighty)
تنزيه (transcendence)
تقديس (sanctification)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'aziz' simply means 'mighty' or 'powerful' without considering the conditions.
Correction: The text shows that the term has a precise definition involving rarity, need, and inaccessibility.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the sun not called al-Aziz?
A) Because it is not rare
B) Because it is not needed
C) Because it is easily accessible
D) Because it has a like

Key Takeaway
Al-Aziz is defined by three conditions—rarity, need, and inaccessibility—and only Allah perfectly fulfills them all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that the name al-Aziz combines rarity, intense need, and inaccessibility—and that only Allah perfectly embodies this. Think of one person, position, or possession in your life that you consider 'mighty' or 'indispensable.' Does it fulfill all three conditions? If not, why do you give it that status in your heart? What would it mean for you to re-channel that sense of reliance solely to Allah?
?
The author says perfection in rarity is that it reduces to one, with no like. You claim 'La ilaha illa Allah' with your tongue, but where in your daily routine do you act as if something else is one-of-a-kind and irreplaceable for your happiness? Write down one specific thing you would struggle to lose—and then ask yourself: does this compete with Allah's al-Aziz?
?
The sun is given as an example of something unique and beneficial but accessible—so it's not called Aziz. Many blessings from Allah are like the sun: freely given. Do you take them for granted? How can you practice being in awe of the Giver rather than just receiving the gift? Set a concrete reminder for this week to pause and reflect on the Giver's inaccessibility and your need.

The First Name: Al-Quddus (The Holy)
Defines Al-Quddus through linguistic derivation and scriptural examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name Al-Quddus as derived from purity and its scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
Al-Quddus means absolute purity – Allah is free from all defects.

The first divine name discussed in this section is Al-Quddus, meaning 'The Holy.' The author grounds its meaning in the root word 'quds' (purity) and provides examples from scripture and common religious terms to illustrate that holiness is about being pure and free from imperfections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the linguistic root
Notice that Al-Quddus is derived from al-quds, meaning purity. The author emphasizes this connection immediately after citing the verses.
3
Examine the three examples
The author gives three examples: Bayt al-Maqdis, Paradise, and the angel Jibril. Each is called 'holy' because of purity. Reflect on how this reinforces the meaning of Al-Quddus.
4
Connect to the angelic testimony
The passage ends with the angels saying 'We sanctify You.' This shows that sanctification is declaring purity, completing the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الأول : القدوس . قال تعالى : ( هو الله لا إله إلا هو الملك القدوس ) (١) وقال : ( يسبح الله ما في السموات وما في الأرض الملك القدوس ) (٢) واعلم . أن القدوس مشتق من القدس وهو الطهارة ، ولهذا يقال : بيت المقدس، أي المكان الذي يتطهر فيه من الذنوب . وقيل للجنة : حظيرة القدس . لطهارتها من آفات الدنيا . ويقال جبريل - عليه السلام - روح القدس . لأنه طاهر عن الخيانة في التبليغ والوحي . قال تعالى حكاية عن الملائكة : ( ونحن نسبح بحمدك ونقدس لك ))
The first name: Al-Quddus. Allah Most High said: 'He is Allah, there is no god but Him, the King, the Holy' (59:23) and said: 'Whatever is in the heavens and whatever is on the earth glorifies Allah, the King, the Holy' (62:1). And know that Al-Quddus is derived from al-quds, which is purity. For this reason, Bayt al-Maqdis is called so, meaning the place where one is purified from sins. Paradise is called the enclosure of holiness (hadirat al-quds) because of its purity from the afflictions of this world. And Jibril (peace be upon him) is called the Spirit of Holiness (Ruh al-Quds) because he is pure from betrayal in delivering revelation. Allah Most High said, quoting the angels: 'And we glorify You with praise and sanctify You' (2:30).
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines the first divine name discussed in this section: Al-Quddus. The author begins by citing two Qur'anic verses that contain this name to establish its scriptural basis. Then he explains the linguistic derivation: Al-Quddus comes from al-quds, meaning purity or holiness. He provides examples to illustrate this meaning: Bayt al-Maqdis (Jerusalem) is called so because it is a place of purification from sins; Paradise is called the enclosure of holiness (hadirat al-quds) because it is free from worldly defects; and the angel Jibril is called Ruh al-Quds (Spirit of Holiness) because he is free from betrayal in delivering revelation. Finally, he quotes the angels' statement in the Qur'an, 'We glorify You with praise and sanctify You,' to show that sanctification is an act of declaring purity.

Correction: Al-Quddus specifically means absolute purity and transcendence from all imperfections, as the author demonstrates through its linguistic root and examples.

?
Jibril is called 'Ruh al-Quds' because he is pure from betrayal in his task. In what areas of your life might you be betraying a trust (to Allah, to others, or to yourself)? Will you repent and commit to purity in that area this week?

The Name Al-Salam: Peace and Perfection in Action
Defines Al-Salam and contrasts it with Al-Quddus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name Al-Salam and how it differs from Al-Quddus.

Core Idea
Al-Salam means God is flawless in all His actions.

In this short passage, the author clarifies another beautiful name of Allah: Al-Salam. While Al-Quddus highlights God's transcendence from defects in His essence and attributes, Al-Salam focuses specifically on His actions. This distinction deepens our understanding of divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic and the literal translation together. Notice the structure: first the name, then its meaning, then the contrast.
The text is very concise; every word carries theological weight.
2
Identify the contrast
The passage clearly states the difference between Al-Quddus and Al-Salam. Pinpoint what each name excludes from God.
Al-Quddus: essence & attributes. Al-Salam: actions.
3
Reflect on the domains
Think why the author distinguishes these two domains. How does this help us appreciate God's perfection more fully?
It shows that God's perfection covers every aspect of His being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثاني : السلام. والمراد منه : ذو السلام . والفرق بين القدوس والسلام : أن القدوس هو المنزه عن النقائص في ذاته وفي صفاته . والسلام هو المنزه عن النقائص في أفعاله .
The second name: Al-Salam. What is meant by it is: the Possessor of peace/safety. And the difference between Al-Quddus and Al-Salam is that Al-Quddus is the One transcendent from deficiencies in His essence and His attributes, while Al-Salam is the One transcendent from deficiencies in His actions.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines the name Al-Salam, explaining that it means 'the Possessor of peace/safety' (dhū al-salām). The author then distinguishes it from Al-Quddus: Al-Quddus signifies transcendence from defects in the divine essence and attributes, whereas Al-Salam signifies transcendence from defects in the divine actions. Thus both names affirm God's perfection, but each addresses a different domain: essence/attributes versus actions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Al-Salam merely means peaceful or peace-loving, like a human attribute.
Correction: Al-Salam is a divine name indicating that God is free from any deficiency in His actions, not that He simply promotes peace among creation.

The Three Criteria for Al-Aziz
Sanusi defines the three criteria for Al-Aziz and illustrates with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the name Al-Aziz requires the combination of three meanings: rarity of existence, intense need, and difficulty of access, and why each criterion alone is insufficient.

Core Idea
The name Al-Aziz only applies when all three criteria are met.

Before we can understand why Allah alone is called Al-Aziz, we must first grasp the precise meaning of this name. Sanusi begins by laying out three essential criteria that must all be present for something to truly be described as 'Aziz. Through clear examples, he shows why each criterion on its own falls short.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of Al-Aziz
Read the opening sentence where Sanusi defines Al-Aziz as that which is rare, intensely needed, and difficult to access. Notice the three criteria are given together.
2
Understand why the three criteria must be combined
Sanusi explains that if any criterion is missing, the name is not applied. Consider the examples: something rare but not needed, or something unique and needed but easy to access (the sun).
The sun example is key: it is unique and beneficial but accessible, so not 'Aziz.
3
Identify the conclusion
Notice the concluding phrase: only when all three are combined is something described as 'Aziz. This sets the stage for the next lesson on perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإسم الثالث : العزيز . وهو الذي يقل وجوده ، وتشتد الحاجة إليه ويصعب الوصول إليه ، فما لم تجتمع هذه المعاني الثلاثة في شيء لا يطلق اسم العزيز عليه . فكم من شيء يقل وجوده ، ولكن لا يحتاج إليه ، فلا يسمى عزيزاً . وقد يكون بحيث لا مثل له ، ويحتاج إليه جداً ، ولكن يسهل الوصول إليه فلا يسمى عزيزاً كالشمس فإنه لا مثل لها ( ويعظم الانتفاع بها (٩) ، ولكنها لا توصف بالعزة ، بسبب أنه لا يصعب الوصول إليها ، أما إذا اجتمعت المعاني الثلاثة فهناك يوصف بكونه عزيزاً ، ثم في كل واحد من هذه ) المعاني ) (*) الثلاثة كمال ونقصان . فالكمال في قلة الوجود أن يرجع إلى واحد ، إذ لا أقل من الواحد ويكون بحيث يستحيل وجود مثله ، وليس هذا إلا الله ( سبحانه وتعالى ((٦) . فإن الشمس ، وإن كانت واحدة في الوجود، فليست واحدة في الإمكان ، لأنه يمكن وجود مثلها . والكمال في كسونه منتفعاً به، أن تكون جميع المنافع حاصلة منه وما ذاك إلا الله - سبحانه ـ فإنه هو المبدأ الجميع الممكنات ، والكمال في صعوبة الوصول إليه هو أنه يمتنع الإحاطة بكنهه ، ولا سبيل إلى معرفته إلا بهدايته ، ولا سبيل لأحد من الخلق إلى القيام بشكر نعمه ، وكمال كل هذه المعاني الله - سبحانه - فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه هو العزيز المطلق.
The third name: Al-Aziz. It is that which is rare in existence, intensely needed, and difficult to access. So unless these three meanings are combined in something, the name Aziz is not applied to it. For how many things are rare in existence but are not needed, so they are not called Aziz. And sometimes something is such that it has no like, and is greatly needed, but is easy to access, so it is not called Aziz, such as the sun, for it has no like and the benefit from it is great, but it is not described with 'izzah because it is not difficult to access. However, when the three meanings are combined, then it is described as being Aziz. Then in each of these three meanings there is perfection and deficiency. So the perfection in rarity of existence is that it goes back to one, for there is nothing less than one, and it be such that its like is impossible, and this is only Allah (glorified and exalted). For the sun, even though it is one in existence, it is not one in possibility, because a like to it is possible. And the perfection in being intensely needed is that all benefits come from it, and this is only Allah, for He is the origin of all possible things. And the perfection in difficulty of access is that it is impossible to encompass His true nature, and there is no way to know Him except through His guidance, and no one among creation can fully thank Him for His blessings. And the perfection of all these meanings belongs to Allah alone. So it is established by what we have mentioned that He is the absolute Al-Aziz.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines the name Al-Aziz by identifying three necessary criteria: rarity of existence, intense need, and difficulty of access. The author explains that unless all three are present, the name is not properly used. He gives examples: something rare but not needed is not called Aziz; something unique and greatly needed but easily accessible (like the sun) is not called Aziz because it is not difficult to access. Only when all three are combined is something truly Aziz. Then he discusses the perfection of each criterion: (1) rarity of existence is perfect only when the being is absolutely one with no possibility of a like—this is only Allah; the sun is one in actual existence but not impossible in possibility. (2) Intense need is perfect when all benefits come from that being—again only Allah, who is the origin of all possible things. (3) Difficulty of access is perfect when it is impossible to fully comprehend the being's true nature and when knowledge of Him is only through His guidance, and no one can fully thank Him. The conclusion is that only Allah is the absolute Al-Aziz.

Key Terms
العزيز
العزة
قلة الوجود
شدة الحاجة
صعوبة الوصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Al-Aziz simply means 'powerful' or 'mighty' in a general sense.
Correction: The passage shows that 'izzah has a composite meaning requiring rarity, intense need, and inaccessibility. Without all three, the name is not properly applied.

Checkpoint
Why is the sun not called Aziz according to the passage?
A) Because it is not rare
B) Because it is not needed
C) Because it is easy to access
D) Because it has a like

Key Takeaway
The name Al-Aziz requires the combination of rarity, intense need, and difficulty of access; missing any one makes it inapplicable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of something in your life you consider 'aziz' (precious). Does it truly meet all three criteria? If not, how does this lesson challenge your attachments? Are you placing ultimate value on something that is not truly ʿAzīz?
?
The sun is an example of something unique and needed but easy to access, so it is not called ʿAzīz. In your daily life, do you treat things that are easily available as less valuable? How does this definition reshape your understanding of what is truly worthy of your deepest need and reverence?
?
Only Allah perfectly possesses all three criteria. Do you feel intense need for Him? Or do you rely on created things that are easy to access? What would change in your worship if you truly internalized that Allah alone is Al-ʿAzīz?
