The Perfection of 'Izzah: Why Only Allah is Absolute Al-Aziz
Sanusi explains the perfection of each criterion and demonstrates that only Allah is the absolute Al-Aziz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how each of the three criteria for Al-Aziz reaches its perfection only in Allah, proving that He alone deserves the name absolutely.

Core Idea
Only Allah perfectly fulfills all three criteria of 'Izzah.

Now that we know the three criteria for Al-Aziz, Sanusi takes us deeper. Each criterion has degrees of perfection. The sun, for example, is one in existence but its like is possible, so its rarity is imperfect. In this lesson, we see how each criterion reaches its peak only in Allah, establishing Him as the absolute Al-Aziz.

Guided Path
1
Read about the perfection of rarity
Sanusi explains that the perfection of rarity is when something is absolutely one with no possible like. Only Allah meets this; the sun is one but not impossible to duplicate.
2
Read about the perfection of intense need
Perfection in need means all benefits come from that being. Only Allah is the origin of all possible things.
3
Read about the perfection of difficulty of access
Perfection in difficulty means it is impossible to fully comprehend the being, knowledge only through His guidance, and no one can fully thank Him. This is only true of Allah.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that since all three perfections belong to Allah alone, He is the absolute Al-Aziz.

والإسم الثالث : العزيز . وهو الذي يقل وجوده ، وتشتد الحاجة إليه ويصعب الوصول إليه ، فما لم تجتمع هذه المعاني الثلاثة في شيء لا يطلق اسم العزيز عليه . فكم من شيء يقل وجوده ، ولكن لا يحتاج إليه ، فلا يسمى عزيزاً . وقد يكون بحيث لا مثل له ، ويحتاج إليه جداً ، ولكن يسهل الوصول إليه فلا يسمى عزيزاً كالشمس فإنه لا مثل لها ( ويعظم الانتفاع بها (٩) ، ولكنها لا توصف بالعزة ، بسبب أنه لا يصعب الوصول إليها ، أما إذا اجتمعت المعاني الثلاثة فهناك يوصف بكونه عزيزاً ، ثم في كل واحد من هذه ) المعاني ) (*) الثلاثة كمال ونقصان . فالكمال في قلة الوجود أن يرجع إلى واحد ، إذ لا أقل من الواحد ويكون بحيث يستحيل وجود مثله ، وليس هذا إلا الله ( سبحانه وتعالى ((٦) . فإن الشمس ، وإن كانت واحدة في الوجود، فليست واحدة في الإمكان ، لأنه يمكن وجود مثلها . والكمال في كسونه منتفعاً به، أن تكون جميع المنافع حاصلة منه وما ذاك إلا الله - سبحانه ـ فإنه هو المبدأ الجميع الممكنات ، والكمال في صعوبة الوصول إليه هو أنه يمتنع الإحاطة بكنهه ، ولا سبيل إلى معرفته إلا بهدايته ، ولا سبيل لأحد من الخلق إلى القيام بشكر نعمه ، وكمال كل هذه المعاني الله - سبحانه - فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه هو العزيز المطلق.
The third name: Al-Aziz. It is that which is rare in existence, intensely needed, and difficult to access. So unless these three meanings are combined in something, the name Aziz is not applied to it. For how many things are rare in existence but are not needed, so they are not called Aziz. And sometimes something is such that it has no like, and is greatly needed, but is easy to access, so it is not called Aziz, such as the sun, for it has no like and the benefit from it is great, but it is not described with 'izzah because it is not difficult to access. However, when the three meanings are combined, then it is described as being Aziz. Then in each of these three meanings there is perfection and deficiency. So the perfection in rarity of existence is that it goes back to one, for there is nothing less than one, and it be such that its like is impossible, and this is only Allah (glorified and exalted). For the sun, even though it is one in existence, it is not one in possibility, because a like to it is possible. And the perfection in being intensely needed is that all benefits come from it, and this is only Allah, for He is the origin of all possible things. And the perfection in difficulty of access is that it is impossible to encompass His true nature, and there is no way to know Him except through His guidance, and no one among creation can fully thank Him for His blessings. And the perfection of all these meanings belongs to Allah alone. So it is established by what we have mentioned that He is the absolute Al-Aziz.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines the name Al-Aziz by identifying three necessary criteria: rarity of existence, intense need, and difficulty of access. The author explains that unless all three are present, the name is not properly used. He gives examples: something rare but not needed is not called Aziz; something unique and greatly needed but easily accessible (like the sun) is not called Aziz because it is not difficult to access. Only when all three are combined is something truly Aziz. Then he discusses the perfection of each criterion: (1) rarity of existence is perfect only when the being is absolutely one with no possibility of a like—this is only Allah; the sun is one in actual existence but not impossible in possibility. (2) Intense need is perfect when all benefits come from that being—again only Allah, who is the origin of all possible things. (3) Difficulty of access is perfect when it is impossible to fully comprehend the being's true nature and when knowledge of Him is only through His guidance, and no one can fully thank Him. The conclusion is that only Allah is the absolute Al-Aziz.

قلة الوجود
شدة الحاجة
صعوبة الوصول

C) Because it is not needed
D) Because it is difficult to access

Key Takeaway
Each criterion for Al-Aziz reaches its peak only in Allah, establishing Him as the absolute Al-Aziz.

In what specific situation today will you consciously rely on Allah's absolute 'izzah rather than on your own or others' power?
?
Consider the fact that no one can fully thank Him. Does this realization humble you or does it make you want to try harder? What is one blessing you have taken for granted, and how will you change that starting now?
?
The passage says that knowledge of Allah is only through His guidance. Have you ever assumed you could reach Allah through your own intellect alone? In what ways have you relied on your own reasoning rather than seeking His guidance? What step will you take today to seek His guidance more earnestly?

Three Meanings of Al-Jabbar (The Compeller)
Explains the three distinct meanings of the name Al-Jabbar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of the divine name Al-Jabbar and how each relates to Allah's attributes.

Core Idea
One name, three majestic meanings: the unreachable High, the Repairer, the Compeller.

The name Al-Jabbar appears in the Qur'an and carries rich theological depth. In this passage, the author unpacks three distinct meanings derived from Arabic usage, showing how each applies to Allah. By examining these aspects, we gain a fuller appreciation of Allah's transcendence, His care for creation, and His absolute will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three meanings
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how each meaning is introduced with 'the first', 'the second', 'the third'.
The passage is structured as an enumeration.
2
Explore the first meaning: the High One
Understand the linguistic root from tall palm tree. This meaning emphasizes Allah's transcendence: beyond thought, sight, and intellect.
3
Examine the second meaning: the Repairer
The verb jabara means to repair. Allah is the ultimate Fixer of all affairs, both bodily and spiritual.
4
Understand the third meaning: the Compeller
This relates to Allah's absolute will: He compels creation to His decree, and nothing occurs except by His will.
This has implications for qadar (divine decree).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الرابع : الجبار . وفيه وجوه : الأول : الجبار : العالي الذي لا ينال . ومنه يقال : نخلة جبارة إذا طالت ، وقصرت الأيدي من أن تنال أعلاها . ورجل جبار إذا كان متكبراً لا يتواضع ولا ينقاد لأحد . وهذا الاسم في حق الله يفيد أنه ـ سبحانه ـ بحيث لا تناله الأفكار ، ولا تحيط به الأبصار ، ولا تصل إلى كنه عزته عقول العقلاء ، ولا يرتقي إلى ( مبادىء إشراق جلالـه علوم الحكماء )(١) وهو بهذا المعنى من صفات التنزيه والثاني : الجبار بمعنى المصلح للأمور : يقال : جبرت الكسر ، إذ أصلحته . فالجبار يفيد الكثرة والمبالغة . فعلى هذا : الجبار في الحقيقة : هو الله - سبحانه - لأنه هو المصلح المهمات الخلق بحسب أجسادهم وأرواحهم والثالث : أن يكون الجبار من أجبره على كذا إذا أكرهه على ما أراد . يقال : جبر السلطان فلاناً على كذا ، وأجبره - بالألف ـ إذا أكرهه على ما أراد ، فعلى هذا الجبار في وصف الله هو الذي جبر الخلق على ما أراد منهم ، وحملهم عليه ، أرادوا أم كرهوا ، فلا يجري في سلطانه إلا ما يريد ، ولا يحصل في ملكه إلا ما حكم به .
The fourth name: Al-Jabbar. It has aspects: The first: Al-Jabbar is the High One who cannot be reached. Hence it is said: a tall palm tree (nakhla jabbarah) when it is tall and hands fall short of reaching its top. And a man is called jabbar if he is arrogant, not humble, and subject to no one. This name with respect to Allah indicates that He is such that thoughts cannot reach Him, sights cannot encompass Him, the intellects of the intelligent cannot attain the essence of His might, and the sciences of the wise cannot ascend to the sources of the radiance of His majesty. And in this sense it is among the attributes of transcendence. The second: Al-Jabbar in the sense of the One who sets things right (al-muslih li al-umur). It is said: jabartu al-kasr, meaning I repaired it. So Al-Jabbar indicates abundance and intensification. Thus, Al-Jabbar in reality is Allah, because He is the One who sets right the important affairs of creation in terms of their bodies and souls. The third: that Al-Jabbar is from ajbarahu 'ala kadha, meaning he compelled him to what he wanted. It is said: jabara al-sultan fulanan 'ala kadha, and ajbarahu (with alif) meaning he forced him to what he wanted. Thus, Al-Jabbar as a description of Allah is He who compelled creation to what He wanted from them, and carried them towards it, whether they wished or not. So nothing runs in His dominion except what He wills, and nothing occurs in His kingdom except what He decrees.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage discusses the three meanings of the name Al-Jabbar. The first meaning is 'the High One who cannot be reached,' derived from Arabic usage for a tall palm tree or an arrogant person. For Allah, it signifies that He is beyond the reach of thoughts, sights, and intellects; this is an attribute of transcendence. The second meaning is 'the One who sets things right,' from the verb jabara (to repair). In this sense, Allah is the ultimate Fixer of all affairs of creation. The third meaning is 'the Compeller,' from the verb ajbara (to compel). As such, Allah is the One who compels His will upon creation, and nothing happens except by His decree.

Correction: The name has multiple dimensions: the unreachable High, the Repairer, and the Compeller, each rooted in Arabic usage and revealing different facets of Allah's perfection.

How can you practice more humility in your knowledge of Allah?

The second meaning of Al-Jabbar is that Allah repairs what is broken. What brokenness in your own soul—such as pride, a broken relationship, or a sin you keep repeating—are you relying on Allah to fix today? Will you sincerely turn to Him for that healing?

Al-Mutakabbir: The Supreme
Defines Al-Mutakabbir with exclusive greatness and king-slave analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of Al-Mutakabbir as Allah's exclusive greatness and how it contrasts with human arrogance.

Core Idea
Al-Mutakabbir means Allah sees all as insignificant and attributes greatness only to Himself.

This lesson explores the fifth divine name Al-Mutakabbir. While in human language this word often carries negative connotations of arrogance, for Allah it signifies His absolute supremacy. The passage defines it with a vivid analogy that helps us grasp this unique attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the foundation.
Notice the three-part structure: definition, exclusive attribution, analogy.
2
Understand the core meaning
The phrase 'sees all as insignificant in addition to His essence' means everything else is minuscule compared to Allah.
This is the essence of Allah's supremacy.
3
Note the exclusive attribution
Allah sees no greatness and majesty except for Himself. This underscores His uniqueness.
4
Reflect on the king-slave analogy
The analogy of a king looking at his slaves illustrates the vast difference in status and power.
This helps human minds grasp the concept.
5
Distinguish from human arrogance
In humans, 'mutakabbir' is blameworthy arrogance; for Allah, it is a praiseworthy attribute of exclusive greatness.
This prevents misunderstanding.

The fifth name: Al-Mutakabbir. He is the One who sees all as insignificant in addition to His essence, and sees no greatness and majesty except for Himself, and looks to others as kings look to their slaves.

It explains that Al-Mutakabbir is the One who regards everything else as insignificant compared to His own essence, and attributes greatness and majesty exclusively to Himself. The Divine Name al-Jabbār: Three Meanings
Author explains three linguistic and theological meanings of the divine name al-Jabbār.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three linguistic and theological meanings of the divine name al-Jabbār and how they enrich our understanding of God's attributes.

Core Idea
Al-Jabbār means the High, the Reformer, and the Compeller—each revealing a different facet of Allah's majesty.

This lesson explores the divine name al-Jabbār. The author presents three distinct meanings derived from Arabic linguistics. Each meaning highlights a different aspect of Allah: His transcendence, His care for creation, and His absolute will. By studying these, we gain a deeper appreciation of this beautiful name.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Notice how the author structures the three meanings with linguistic examples.
2
Identify each meaning and its linguistic root
For each of the three meanings, note the Arabic root (j-b-r) and how it applies to God. The first meaning relates to height, the second to mending, the third to compelling.
The first meaning is linked to the name al-ʿĀlī.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how each meaning complements the others. The first emphasizes God's transcendence, the second His care, the third His sovereignty. Together they give a fuller picture of al-Jabbār.
This name is among the attributes of tanzīh and thubūt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الرابع : الجبار . وفيه وجوه :
الأول : الجبار : العالي الذي لا ينال . ومنه يقال : نخلة جبارة إذا طالت ، وقصرت الأيدي من أن تنال أعلاها . ورجل جبار إذا كان متكبراً لا يتواضع ولا ينقاد لأحد . وهذا الاسم في حق الله يفيد أنه ـ سبحانه ـ بحيث لا تناله الأفكار ، ولا تحيط به الأبصار ، ولا تصل إلى كنه عزته عقول العقلاء ، ولا يرتقي إلى ( مبادىء إشراق جلالـه علوم الحكماء )(۱) وهو بهذا المعنى من صفات التنزيه
والثاني : الجبار بمعنى المصلح للأمور : يقال : جبرت الكسر ، إذ أصلحته . فالجبار يفيد الكثرة والمبالغة . فعلى هذا : الجبار في الحقيقة : هو الله - سبحانه - لأنه هو المصلح المهمات الخلق بحسب أجسادهم وأرواحهم
والثالث : أن يكون الجبار من أجبره على كذا إذا أكرهه على ما أراد . يقال : جبر السلطان فلاناً على كذا ، وأجبره - بالألف ـ إذا أكرهه على ما أراد ، فعلى هذا الجبار في وصف الله هو الذي جبر الخلق على ما أراد منهم ، وحملهم عليه ، أرادوا أم كرهوا ، فلا يجري في سلطانه إلا ما يريد ، ولا يحصل في ملكه إلا ما حكم به .
The fourth name: al-Jabbār (the Compeller). And it has aspects:
First: al-Jabbār: the High (al-ʿĀlī) who cannot be reached. And from this it is said: a tall palm tree (nakhla jabbāra) when it is long and hands are too short to reach its top. And a man (is called) jabbār when he is arrogant, not humble, and not submissive to anyone. And this name, with respect to God, indicates that He — glorified is He — is such that thoughts cannot reach Him, sights cannot encompass Him, the intellects of the intelligent cannot attain the reality of His might, and the knowledges of the wise do not ascend to the sources of the radiance of His majesty. And it is, in this meaning, among the attributes of transcendence (ṣifāt al-tanzīh).
Second: al-Jabbār in the sense of the Reformer of affairs: it is said: jabartu al-kasr (I mended the break) when I repaired it. So al-Jabbār indicates abundance and exaggeration. Thus, according to this, al-Jabbār in reality is God — glorified is He — because He is the Reformer of the important matters of creation with respect to their bodies and souls.
Third: that al-Jabbār be from ajbarahu ʿalā kadha (he compelled him to do such) when he forced him to do what he wanted. It is said: jabara al-sulṭān fulānan ʿalā kadha, and ajbarahu (with alif) if he forced him to do what he wanted. Thus, according to this, al-Jabbār in describing God is He who compelled creation to what He wanted from them, and carried them upon it, whether they wanted or disliked. Nothing runs in His dominion except what He wills, and nothing occurs in His kingdom except what He decrees.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Jabbār and explains its three possible meanings. The author begins by stating the name and then enumerates three aspects. For each aspect, he gives a linguistic explanation and then applies it to God. The first meaning is al-ʿĀlī (the High) who cannot be attained, illustrating with language examples such as a tall palm tree and an arrogant man. This emphasizes God's transcendence (tanzīh) in that thoughts, sights, and intellects cannot reach Him. The second meaning is the Reformer (al-muṣliḥ), derived from the root j-b-r meaning to mend or repair, indicating God's active role in fixing the affairs of creation, both physical and spiritual. The third meaning is the Compeller, from the sense of forcing (ajbarahu), indicating God's absolute will over creation such that nothing occurs except what He wills. Each meaning is complementary and contributes to the richness of the name.

العالي
صفات التنزيه

Correction: The passage shows it also means 'the High' and 'the Reformer', revealing positive aspects of God's nature.

?

Defining al-Mutakabbir and the Criterion of True Pride
Defines al-Mutakabbir and establishes the condition for true pride.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Mutakabbir and the criterion that distinguishes true pride from false pride.

Core Idea
Pride is true only when the perception of greatness matches reality.

The author introduces the fifth divine name al-Mutakabbir, explaining that it refers to one who sees all else as insignificant relative to Himself. He then lays out a crucial criterion: if this perception is true, the pride is genuine; if false, it is blameworthy. This lesson unpacks that criterion.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully, focusing on the description of al-Mutakabbir.
Notice the phrase 'يرى الكل حقيراً بالإضافة إلى ذاته'.
2
Understand the criterion
Identify the conditional sentence: if the perception is true, then pride is true. This is the key distinction.
The word 'صادقة' indicates truthfulness of the perception.
3
Apply the criterion
Reflect on why only God can have true pride, since His perception of His own greatness is always accurate.
Consider what it means for a human to claim such pride.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الخامس : المتكبر . وهو الذي يرى الكل حقيراً بالإضافة إلى ذاته ، ولا يرى العظمة والكبرياء إلا لنفسه ، وينظر إلى غيره نظر الملوك إلى العبيد ، فإن كانت هذه الرؤية صادقة كان التكبر حقاً ، وكان صاحبها متكبراً حقاً . ولا يتصور ذلك على سبيل الكمال إلا الله - سبحانه ـ وإن كانت تلك الرؤية باطلة ، ولم يكن ما تراه من التفرد بالعظمة كما يراه ، كان التكبر باطلاً مذموماً . ولهذا قال - عليه السلام - حاكياً عن الله - سبحانه - : ( الكبرياء ردائي ، والعظمة إزاري. من نازعني واحداً منهما قذفته في النار ) (۱) ولما كان الأمر كذلك ظهر أن التكبر في حق الله - سبحانه - صفة مدح وكمال ، وفي حق الخلق صفة نقصان واختلال
The fifth name: al-Mutakabbir (the Proud). And He is the one who sees everything as insignificant in addition to His essence, and does not see greatness and pride except for Himself, and looks at others as kings look at slaves. If this perception is true, then the pride (al-takabbur) is true, and its possessor is truly proud. And this cannot be conceived to perfection except for God — glorified is He. And if that perception is false, and what he sees of uniqueness in greatness is not as he sees it, then the pride is false and blameworthy. For this reason, he (peace be upon him) said, reporting from God — glorified is He: 'Pride (al-kibriyā') is My cloak, and Greatness (al-ʿaẓamah) is My garment. Whoever contends with Me for either of them, I will cast him into the Fire.' Since the matter is thus, it becomes clear that pride (al-takabbur) with respect to God — glorified is He — is an attribute of praise and perfection, and with respect to creation is an attribute of deficiency and corruption.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Mutakabbir. It begins by describing the attribute of seeing all else as insignificant relative to God, and not recognizing greatness or pride except for Himself. The author then makes a crucial distinction: if this perception matches reality, the pride is true and praiseworthy; if it is false, it is blameworthy. Since only God truly possesses unique greatness, true pride in perfection belongs only to Him. This is supported by a prophetic hadith qudsī in which Allah says pride and greatness are His exclusive attributes. The conclusion is that pride (takabbur) is an attribute of perfection for God but a deficiency for created beings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: Pride is praiseworthy when it is based on genuine greatness, which only applies to God.

Key Takeaway

?

Cites hadith qudsi and draws conclusion about pride in God vs. creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pride is praiseworthy for God but blameworthy for humans, as supported by the hadith qudsi.

Core Idea

The author now cites a prophetic hadith to reinforce the point: Allah declares pride and greatness as His exclusive garments. This leads to the conclusion that pride is a perfection in God but a deficiency in creatures. This lesson unpacks that hadith and its implications.

Al-ʿAlī and al-Kabīr: Sensory vs. Intellectual Highness
Author explains the names al-ʿAlī and al-Kabīr and distinguishes sensory highness from intellectual highness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between sensory and intellectual highness and why God's attribute al-ʿAlī must be understood intellectually.

Core Idea
God's highness is not physical but intellectual, befitting His transcendence.

This lesson introduces two of God's beautiful names: al-ʿAlī (the High) and al-Kabīr (the Great). The author, after citing Qur'anic verses, focuses on the meaning of highness (ʿuluww) and makes a crucial distinction: highness can be sensory (physical elevation) or intellectual (spiritual elevation). Since God is above corporeality, His highness must be understood in the intellectual sense—as the perfection of His attributes and His distance from all deficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two names
Read the Arabic passage and note the two names discussed. Pay attention to the Qur'anic verses cited.
2
Understand the distinction between two kinds of highness
Focus on where the author defines sensory highness (e.g., Throne above Chair) and intellectual highness (e.g., raising believers). See how he rejects the sensory for God.
3
Apply the distinction to God's attribute
Connect the author's argument: because God is not a body, His highness can only be intellectual, i.e., His majestic attributes and transcendence.

And He said: 'And to Him belongs pride in the heavens and the earth' (45:37). So we say: As for al-ʿAlī, it is derived from al-ʿuluww (height, highness), and it is the opposite of al-sufl (lowness). Then indeed highness and lowness sometimes occur in sensory matters and sometimes in intellectual matters. As for sensory matters, it is said: the Throne is above the Chair, and the heaven is above the earth. And this highness does not occur except in bodies. And since the Holy Truth (al-Qudus al-Ḥaqq) is exalted above corporeality, His highness is exalted above being in this sense. As for intellectual matters, it is like His statement: 'Allah will raise those who have believed among you and those who have been given knowledge by degrees' (58:11). Commentary
This passage discusses the two divine names al-ʿAlī (the High) and al-Kabīr (the Great). The author begins by quoting several Qur'anic verses that employ these names, noting that sometimes 'al-ʿAlī' precedes 'al-Kabīr' and sometimes the order is reversed. Since God is transcendent above corporeality (al-jismiyya), His highness cannot be physical. Therefore, the highness attributed to God must be of the intellectual kind, which consists of His majestic attributes and His distance from imperfections. This passage reinforces God's transcendence and the need to understand His attributes in a manner befitting His majesty.

The Name al-‘Alī: Conceptual Height and Proof
Author defines al-‘Alī, distinguishes physical/conceptual height, provides proofs, and gives two interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attribute al-‘Alī denotes conceptual height—absolute perfection and primacy over all existents—not spatial elevation, and you will grasp the rational proofs for this and its two possible interpretations.

Core Idea
God's height is not spatial but conceptual—He is above all in perfection and rank.

In this passage, the author explains the divine name al-‘Alī (the Most High). He begins by distinguishing between two kinds of height: physical (only for bodies) and intellectual (conceptual). Since God is beyond corporeality, His height must be understood intellectually. The author then provides three rational arguments to prove that God is above all existents in every perfection. Finally, he offers two ways to interpret this loftiness: as a negation (nothing equals Him) or as a relational attribute (His absolute power and disposal over all).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-‘Alī
Read the Arabic and English translation of the section that defines al-‘Alī and distinguishes physical from conceptual height.
Pay attention to the Quranic verses cited.
2
Understand the two types of height
Note that sensible height applies to bodies only, while intellectual height applies to ranks of perfection. God's height is the latter.
The author gives examples: the Throne above the Chair (sensible), and raising believers in degrees (intellectual).
3
Follow the three proofs of God's absolute height
The author provides three arguments based on categories: effecter vs. effect, necessary vs. possible, absolutely perfect vs. imperfect. See how each concludes that God is higher than all.
These are rational demonstrations, not scriptural.
4
Learn the two interpretations of al-‘uluww
The author reduces the meaning of 'height' to either a negative/transcendental sense (nothing equals Him) or a relational sense (He is powerful over all).
Both are valid; one is a name of tanzīh, the other a ṣifa idāfiyya.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Ask: Why is it important to clarify that God's height is not spatial? How does this protect tawḥīd?
This prevents anthropomorphism and upholds God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاسم السادس والسابع : العلي . الكبير . قال ـ سبحانه - : ( فالحكم العلي الكبير ) وقال : ( عالم الغيب والشهادة الكبير المتعال ) فقدم في الآية الأولى لفظ العلي على لفظ الكبير ، وفي الثانية عكس الترتيب وقال : ( ولا يؤوده حفظهما ، وهو العلي العظيم ) وقال : ( وكبره تكبيرا ) وقال : ( وله الكبرياء في السموات والأرض ) فنقول : أما العلي فهو مشتق من العلو ، وهو يقابل السفل . ثم إن العلو والسفل قد يحصلان في الأمور المحسوسة تارة ، وفي الأمور المعقولة أخرى . أما في المحسوسة فكما يقال : العرش فوق الكرسي ، والسماء فوق الأرض . وهذا العلو لا يحصل إلا في الأجسام . ولما تقدس القدس الحق عن الجسمية ، تقدس علوه عن أن يكون بهذا المعنى . وأما في الأمور المعقولة فكقوله تعالى : ( يرفع الله الذين آمنوا منكم ، والذين أوتوا العلم درجات ) ومعلوم أن هذه الرفعة ليست إلا في صفات الجلال ، وفي البعد عن صفات النقصان . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لا تفرض مرتبة من مراتب الكمال في الموجودات إلا والله - سبحانه - في أعلى الدرجات . فيقال : الموجود إما مؤثر ، وإما أثر ، والمؤثر أعلى درجة من الأثر . والله - سبحانه - هو المؤثر في الكل ، فكان أعلى من الكل . وأيضاً : الموجود إما واجب وإما ممكن ، والواجب أعلى درجة من الممكن ، والله - سبحانه - هو الواجب فكان أعلى من الكل . وأيضاً : الموجود إما كامل مطلقاً ؛ وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، والكامل المطلق أعلى حالا ممن لا يكون كذلك . والله - سبحانه - هو الكامل المطلق . فكان أعلى من كل الموجودات ، وكذا القول في كمال العلم وكمال القدرة وكمال الحياة وكمال الدوام وكمال الوجود وكمال الرحمة . وقس عليها نظائرها . فيثبت أنه - سبحانه - أعلى من جميع الموجودات في المراتب العقلية ، وجل وتعالى عن أن يكون علوه بحسب المكان والجهة . وإذا عرفت العلو بهذا المعنى فقد عرفت الفوقية في قوله : ( وهو القاهر فوق عباده ) وفي قوله : ( يخافون ربهم من فوقهم ) ثم نقول : يرجع حاصل هذا العلو إلى أحد وجهين ، إما أن يكون معناه : هو أنه لا يساويه شيء في الشرف والمجد والعزة ، وحينئذ يكون هذا الاسم من أسماء التنزيه ، ومن أسماء السلوب ، وإما أن يكون معنى ذلك هو أنه قادر على الكل ، ومتصرف في الكل ، فيكون ذلك من باب الصفات الإضافية . وأما الكبير . فقد ورد في صفات الله ألفاظ من هذا الجنس : هذا اللفظ وهو الكبير وثانيها : المتكبر - كما تقدم وثالثها : الأكبر وهذا اللفظ وارد في القرآن في صفات الله . قال : ( ورضوان من الله أكبر ) وقال : ( ولذكر الله أكبر ) فأما في ذات الله - تعالى - فهو قولنا : الله أكبر .
The sixth and seventh names: al-‘Alī (the Most High), al-Kabīr (the Great). He—glorified—said: (So judgment belongs to Allah, the Most High, the Great) and said: (Knower of the unseen and the witnessed, the Great, the Most High) so He presented in the first verse the word ‘al-‘Alī before the word al-Kabīr, and in the second He reversed the order. And He said: (And their preservation tires Him not; and He is the Most High, the Magnificent) and said: (And magnify Him with exaltation) and said: (And His is the pride in the heavens and the earth). So we say: As for al-‘Alī, it is derived from al-‘uluww (height/loftiness), and it is the opposite of al-sufl (lowness). Then indeed al-‘uluww and al-sufl sometimes occur in sensible matters and sometimes in intellectual matters. As for sensible matters, it is said: the Throne is above the Chair, and the sky is above the earth. And this height does not occur except in bodies. And since the Holy Truth is transcendent over corporeality, His height is transcendent over being in this sense. As for intellectual matters, it is like His statement—exalted: (Allah will raise those who have believed among you and those who were given knowledge, by degrees). And it is known that this elevation is only in the attributes of majesty and in distance from attributes of deficiency. When you have understood this, we say: Do not suppose any rank of perfection among existing things except that Allah—glorified—is in the highest degrees. So it is said: The existent is either an effecter or an effect, and the effecter is higher in degree than the effect. And Allah—glorified—is the effecter of everything, so He is higher than everything. Also: The existent is either necessary or possible, and the necessary is higher in degree than the possible, and Allah—glorified—is the Necessary, so He is higher than everything. Also: The existent is either absolutely perfect or not, and the absolutely perfect is higher in condition than that which is not such. And Allah—glorified—is the absolutely perfect, so He is higher than all existents. And likewise the statement regarding perfection of knowledge, perfection of power, perfection of life, perfection of eternity, perfection of existence, and perfection of mercy. And analogize to their likes. So it is established that He—glorified—is higher than all existents in intellectual ranks, and He is exalted and high above being His height according to place and direction. And when you know al-‘uluww in this sense, you have known al-fawqiyyah (above-ness) in His statement: (And He is the Subjugator over His servants) and in His statement: (They fear their Lord above them). Then we say: The outcome of this height reduces to one of two aspects: either its meaning is that nothing equals Him in honor, glory, and might, and then this name is among the names of transcendence and among the names of negation; or its meaning is that He is capable of everything and disposes of everything, and then that falls under the category of relational attributes. As for al-Kabīr (the Great), there have come in the attributes of Allah words of this kind: this word which is al-Kabīr, and the second: al-Mutakabbir (the Proud) as previously mentioned, and the third: al-Akbar (the Greater). And this word occurs in the Qur’an in the attributes of Allah. He said: (And the pleasure of Allah is greater) and said: (And the remembrance of Allah is greater). As for the essence of Allah—exalted—then it is our saying: Allah is Akbar (Allah is the Greatest).
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the two divine names al-‘Alī (the Most High) and al-Kabīr (the Great). The author begins by citing several Quranic verses in which these names appear, sometimes in different orders. He then treats each name separately.

First, al-‘Alī is derived from al-‘uluww (height, loftiness), which is the opposite of al-sufl (lowness). The author distinguishes two types of height: sensible (physical) and intellectual (conceptual). Sensible height applies only to bodies, such as the throne being above the chair. Since God is transcendent over corporeality, His height cannot be physical. Intellectual height is exemplified by God raising believers and those given knowledge in degrees—an elevation in virtue, not location. 

To prove God's absolute height in intellectual terms, the author provides three arguments based on categories of existence: (1) The effecter is higher than the effect; God is the effecter of everything, so He is higher than all. (2) The necessary existent is higher than the possible; God is necessary, so He is higher. (3) The absolutely perfect is higher than the imperfect; God is absolutely perfect, so He is higher. This applies to all perfections: knowledge, power, life, eternity, existence, and mercy. Thus, God is higher than all existents in intellectual ranks, not spatially.

Having established this, the author clarifies that God's attribute of being 'above' (al-fawqiyyah) in verses like "He is the Subjugator over His servants" is understood conceptually, not physically. He then reduces the meaning of al-‘uluww to two possible interpretations: (a) a negative/transcendental sense—nothing equals Him in honor, so it is a name of transcendence (tanzīh) and negation (salb); (b) a relational sense—He is powerful over all and disposes of all, so it is a relational attribute (sifa idāfiyya).

Finally, the author briefly discusses al-Kabīr. He lists related terms: al-Kabīr itself, al-Mutakabbir (the Proud, already covered), and al-Akbar (the Greater). He cites verses where these appear, concluding that when we say 'Allahu Akbar' (Allah is Greatest), this refers to His essence—He is greater than everything.

Key Terms
العلي
العلو
السفل
المحسوس
المعقول
التنزيه
السلوب
الصفات الإضافية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's 'height' means He is located in a physical direction, like above the heavens.
Correction: The author explicitly argues that God's height is not spatial or physical, but conceptual—signifying His absolute perfection and primacy over all existents. Physical height applies only to bodies, and God is beyond corporeality.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is NOT a proof that God is higher than all existents?
A) God is the effecter of everything, while everything else is an effect.
B) God is the Necessary Existent, while all else is possible.
C) God is absolutely perfect, while all else is imperfect.
D) God is physically above the Throne.

Key Takeaway
God's attribute al-‘Alī signifies His absolute conceptual height above all existents in every perfection, not spatial elevation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If Allah’s height is conceptual—meaning He is supremely above in honor and authority—then how does this affect the way you approach Him in prayer? Do you truly submit to His authority in your heart, or are you still clinging to your own sense of control? What will you surrender right now?

The Name al-Kabīr: God's Greatness and Related Terms
Author defines al-Kabīr and lists related terms, citing Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Kabīr (the Great) and its related terms al-Mutakabbir (the Proud) and al-Akbar (the Greatest), and why 'Allahu Akbar' is a statement of God's absolute greatness beyond comparison.

Core Idea
God is the Greatest; His greatness is incomparable and transcends all creation.

After discussing al-‘Alī, the author briefly introduces al-Kabīr. He notes that three related terms appear in the Qur'an: al-Kabīr, al-Mutakabbir, and al-Akbar. The author explains that when we say 'Allahu Akbar', we affirm that God is greater than everything—a declaration of His absolute supremacy. This lesson connects these terms to the broader theme of God's transcendence and majesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Kabīr
Read the Arabic and English translation of the final part of the source passage that discusses al-Kabīr, al-Mutakabbir, and al-Akbar.
This is a short section, but it completes the treatment of the two names.
2
Identify the three related terms
Note the three words: al-Kabīr, al-Mutakabbir, and al-Akbar. Understand that they are lexically connected but have distinct nuances.
The author mentions that al-Mutakabbir was already discussed previously.
3
Understand the Quranic context
Look at the verses cited: 'And the pleasure of Allah is greater' and 'And the remembrance of Allah is greater'—these show comparative greatness.
These verses indicate that certain things (e.g., Allah's pleasure, His remembrance) are greater than others, but ultimately all greatness belongs to Him.
4
Grasp the meaning of 'Allahu Akbar'
The author states that saying 'Allahu Akbar' refers to God's essence—it is a declaration that He is greater than everything.
This phrase is a central Islamic declaration used in prayer and daily life.
5
Reflect on the relationship between al-‘Alī and al-Kabīr
Consider how these two names complement each other: one emphasizes loftiness, the other greatness. Together they affirm God's transcendence in both rank and magnitude.
The author presented them together and sometimes reversed their order in Quranic verses.

سبحانه - في أعلى الدرجات . فيقال : الموجود إما مؤثر ، وإما أثر ، والمؤثر أعلى درجة من الأثر . والله - سبحانه - هو المؤثر في الكل ، فكان أعلى من الكل . وأيضاً : الموجود إما واجب وإما ممكن ، والواجب أعلى درجة من الممكن ، والله - سبحانه - هو الواجب فكان أعلى من الكل . وأيضاً : الموجود إما كامل مطلقاً ؛ وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، والكامل المطلق أعلى حالا ممن لا يكون كذلك . والله - سبحانه - هو الكامل المطلق . فكان أعلى من كل الموجودات ، وكذا القول في كمال العلم وكمال القدرة وكمال الحياة وكمال الدوام وكمال الوجود وكمال الرحمة . وقس عليها نظائرها . فيثبت أنه - سبحانه - أعلى من جميع الموجودات في المراتب العقلية ، وجل وتعالى عن أن يكون علوه بحسب المكان والجهة . وإذا عرفت العلو بهذا المعنى فقد عرفت الفوقية في قوله : ( وهو القاهر فوق عباده ) وفي قوله : ( يخافون ربهم من فوقهم ) ثم نقول : يرجع حاصل هذا العلو إلى أحد وجهين ، إما أن يكون معناه : هو أنه لا يساويه شيء في الشرف والمجد والعزة ، وحينئذ يكون هذا الاسم من أسماء التنزيه ، ومن أسماء السلوب ، وإما أن يكون معنى ذلك هو أنه قادر على الكل ، ومتصرف في الكل ، فيكون ذلك من باب الصفات الإضافية . وأما الكبير . فقد ورد في صفات الله ألفاظ من هذا الجنس : هذا اللفظ وهو الكبير وثانيها : المتكبر - كما تقدم وثالثها : الأكبر وهذا اللفظ وارد في القرآن في صفات الله . قال : ( ورضوان من الله أكبر ) وقال : ( ولذكر الله أكبر ) فأما في ذات الله - تعالى - فهو قولنا : الله أكبر .
The sixth and seventh names: al-‘Alī (the Most High), al-Kabīr (the Great). He—glorified—said: (So judgment belongs to Allah, the Most High, the Great) and said: (Knower of the unseen and the witnessed, the Great, the Most High) so He presented in the first verse the word ‘al-‘Alī before the word al-Kabīr, and in the second He reversed the order. And He said: (And their preservation tires Him not; and He is the Most High, the Magnificent) and said: (And magnify Him with exaltation) and said: (And His is the pride in the heavens and the earth). So we say: As for al-‘Alī, it is derived from al-‘uluww (height/loftiness), and it is the opposite of al-sufl (lowness). Then indeed al-‘uluww and al-sufl sometimes occur in sensible matters and sometimes in intellectual matters. As for sensible matters, it is said: the Throne is above the Chair, and the sky is above the earth. And this height does not occur except in bodies. And since the Holy Truth is transcendent over corporeality, His height is transcendent over being in this sense. As for intellectual matters, it is like His statement—exalted: (Allah will raise those who have believed among you and those who were given knowledge, by degrees). And it is known that this elevation is only in the attributes of majesty and in distance from attributes of deficiency. When you have understood this, we say: Do not suppose any rank of perfection among existing things except that Allah—glorified—is in the highest degrees. So it is said: The existent is either an effecter or an effect, and the effecter is higher in degree than the effect. And Allah—glorified—is the effecter of everything, so He is higher than everything. Also: The existent is either necessary or possible, and the necessary is higher in degree than the possible, and Allah—glorified—is the Necessary, so He is higher than everything. Also: The existent is either absolutely perfect or not, and the absolutely perfect is higher in condition than that which is not such. And Allah—glorified—is the absolutely perfect, so He is higher than all existents. And likewise the statement regarding perfection of knowledge, perfection of power, perfection of life, perfection of eternity, perfection of existence, and perfection of mercy. And analogize to their likes. So it is established that He—glorified—is higher than all existents in intellectual ranks, and He is exalted and high above being His height according to place and direction. And when you know al-‘uluww in this sense, you have known al-fawqiyyah (above-ness) in His statement: (And He is the Subjugator over His servants) and in His statement: (They fear their Lord above them). Then we say: The outcome of this height reduces to one of two aspects: either its meaning is that nothing equals Him in honor, glory, and might, and then this name is among the names of transcendence and among the names of negation; or its meaning is that He is capable of everything and disposes of everything, and then that falls under the category of relational attributes. As for al-Kabīr (the Great), there have come in the attributes of Allah words of this kind: this word which is al-Kabīr, and the second: al-Mutakabbir (the Proud) as previously mentioned, and the third: al-Akbar (the Greater). And this word occurs in the Qur’an in the attributes of Allah. He said: (And the pleasure of Allah is greater) and said: (And the remembrance of Allah is greater). As for the essence of Allah—exalted—then it is our saying: Allah is Akbar (Allah is the Greatest).
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the two divine names al-‘Alī (the Most High) and al-Kabīr (the Great). The author begins by citing several Quranic verses in which these names appear, sometimes in different orders. He then treats each name separately.

First, al-‘Alī is derived from al-‘uluww (height, loftiness), which is the opposite of al-sufl (lowness). The author distinguishes two types of height: sensible (physical) and intellectual (conceptual). Sensible height applies only to bodies, such as the throne being above the chair. Since God is transcendent over corporeality, His height cannot be physical. Intellectual height is exemplified by God raising believers and those given knowledge in degrees—an elevation in virtue, not location. 

To prove God's absolute height in intellectual terms, the author provides three arguments based on categories of existence: (1) The effecter is higher than the effect; God is the effecter of everything, so He is higher than all. (2) The necessary existent is higher than the possible; God is necessary, so He is higher. (3) The absolutely perfect is higher than the imperfect; God is absolutely perfect, so He is higher. This applies to all perfections: knowledge, power, life, eternity, existence, and mercy. Thus, God is higher than all existents in intellectual ranks, not spatially.

Having established this, the author clarifies that God's attribute of being 'above' (al-fawqiyyah) in verses like "He is the Subjugator over His servants" is understood conceptually, not physically. He then reduces the meaning of al-‘uluww to two possible interpretations: (a) a negative/transcendental sense—nothing equals Him in honor, so it is a name of transcendence (tanzīh) and negation (salb); (b) a relational sense—He is powerful over all and disposes of all, so it is a relational attribute (sifa idāfiyya).

Finally, the author briefly discusses al-Kabīr. He lists related terms: al-Kabīr itself, al-Mutakabbir (the Proud, already covered), and al-Akbar (the Greater). He cites verses where these appear, concluding that when we say 'Allahu Akbar' (Allah is Greatest), this refers to His essence—He is greater than everything.

Key Terms
الكبير
التكبر
الأكبر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'Allahu Akbar' means 'Allah is big' in a physical sense.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'Akbar' is a term of greatness, not physical size. It is a declaration that God is greater than everything, transcending all attributes of creation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
God's name al-Kabīr and the declaration 'Allahu Akbar' affirm that He is greater than all things, incomparable in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allahu Akbar' in your daily prayers, do you truly feel the weight that God is greater than every worry, every ambition, every person in your life? Or has it become a mere phrase? What would change if you meant it with your whole heart?

The Divine Name al-Kabir: Linguistic and Theological Dimensions
The author lists the names related to greatness and analyzes al-Kabir's two linguistic and two theological aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the name al-Kabir indicates God's transcendence through two linguistic aspects and two theological interpretations.

Core Idea
Al-Kabir means God is great above all comparison, not physically large.

This passage explores the divine name al-Kabir (the Great). The author first places it among related names from the Qur'an, then clarifies its meaning by distinguishing between physical and intellectual greatness. Finally, he presents two theological interpretations that make al-Kabir a name of tanzih (transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Recognize the family of greatness names
Identify the four related names: al-Kabir, al-Mutakabbir, al-Akbar, and al-Kibriya. Notice the Quranic verses cited for al-Akbar and al-Kibriya.
2
Understand the two linguistic aspects of 'kabir'
Distinguish between physical greatness (which does not apply to God) and intellectual greatness (which does apply). Reflect on how the same word can have different meanings.
This step is crucial for avoiding anthropomorphism.
3
Grasp the two theological interpretations
Learn the first interpretation: God is the most perfect existent, so He is great relative to all others. Then the second: He is great in being above resembling creatures.
4
Realize that al-Kabir is a name of tanzih
Understand that both interpretations lead to affirming God's transcendence (tanzih). The name does not imply physical size but absolute superiority and incomparability.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكبير . فقد ورد في صفات الله ألفاظ من هذا الجنس : هذا اللفظ ) وهو الكبير ) وثانيها : المتكبر - كما تقدم وثالثها : الأكبر ( وهذا اللفظ وارد في القرآن في صفات الله . قال : ورضوان من الله أكبر ) ( وقال : ( ولذكر الله أكبر ) فأما في ذات الله - تعالى - فهو قولنا : الله أكبر . وأما الكبرياء فهو قوله - سبحانه - : ( وله الكبرياء في السموات والأرض) ونقول : أما الكبير ففيه وجهان: أحدهما : أنه الذي يقع في مقابلة الصغير، وقد يعتبر الكبير والصغير في المقادير . والحق مقدس عن المقدار والحجمية فلا يكون كبره بحسب الجثة والحجمية. وقد يعتبر الكبر والصغر في درجات الكمالات العقلية فيقال : فلان كبير القوم ، وإن كان أصغرهم في الجثة ، ويقال فلان كبير في الدين . أي أن درجته عالية إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : في تفسير الكبير بهذا المعنى في حق الله وجهان : الأول : أنه ثبت أن الحق - سبحانه - أكمل الموجودات وأشرفها وأجلها وأعلاها ، فيكون ـ سبحانه - كبيراً بالقياس إلى كل ما سواه ، وكل ما يغايره فهو صغير بالقياس إليه . والثاني : أنه كبير بمعنى أنه كبر عن مشابهة المخلوقات. وعلى هذين الوجهين فيكون الكبير من أسماء التنزيه .
As for al-Kabir: there have come in the attributes of God expressions of this kind: this expression (i.e., al-Kabir), and the second is al-Mutakabbir – as has preceded – and the third is al-Akbar (and this expression occurs in the Qur'an concerning God's attributes. He said: 'And the pleasure of Allah is greater' and said: 'And the remembrance of Allah is greater.' As for the essence of God – exalted is He – it is our saying: 'Allahu Akbar.' And as for al-Kibriya, it is His saying – glorified be He: 'And to Him belongs the Pride in the heavens and the earth.' And we say: As for al-Kabir, it has two aspects: one of them is that it is placed in contrast to al-saghir (the small), and greatness and smallness may be considered in quantities. But the Truth is sanctified above quantity and corporeality, so His greatness is not by body and corporeality. And greatness and smallness may be considered in the degrees of intellectual perfections, so it is said: 'So-and-so is the great one of the people' even if he is the smallest in body, and it is said: 'So-and-so is great in religion' meaning his rank is high. If you have known this, we say: In the interpretation of al-Kabir in this sense concerning God, there are two aspects: first, that it is established that the Truth – glorified be He – is the most perfect of existents, the most noble, the most majestic, and the highest, so He – glorified be He – is great in relation to everything other than Him, and everything other than Him is small in relation to Him. Second, that He is great in the sense that He is above resembling creatures. And on these two aspects, al-Kabir is among the names of transcendence.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage discusses the divine name al-Kabir. The author first lists related names: al-Kabir, al-Mutakabbir, al-Akbar, and al-Kibriya, citing Quranic evidence. Then he focuses on al-Kabir. He explains that the word 'kabir' linguistically has two aspects: one in contrast to 'saghir' (small) in physical quantity, which does not apply to God, and another in contrast to 'saghir' in degrees of intellectual perfections, which does apply. After clarifying that God is free from physical greatness, the author gives two theological interpretations: first, that God is the most perfect and thus greatest in relation to all else; second, that He is great in the sense of being above resembling creatures. Both interpretations make al-Kabir a name of transcendence (tanzih), affirming His incomparability.

Correction: The passage explicitly rejects physical greatness for God and explains that His greatness is in terms of absolute perfection and being above resemblance to creatures.

Allahu Akbar: The Meaning and Purpose in Prayer
Author explains two aspects of al-Akbar and wisdom of starting prayer with it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two aspects of the divine name al-Akbar and why the phrase 'Allahu Akbar' is placed at the beginning of prayer.

Core Idea
'Allahu Akbar' frees the heart from everything but God.

This lesson explores the meaning of Allah's name al-Akbar. The author explains that it has two aspects, and highlights the profound spiritual purpose behind reciting 'Allahu Akbar' at the start of prayer. Understanding this transforms the prayer into a state of exclusive devotion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about al-Akbar
Read the first part of the source passage focusing on al-Akbar. Notice the two aspects mentioned.
The second aspect is a possible interpretation of the worshipper's saying.
2
Identify the two aspects
The first aspect: God is greater than all other existents. The second aspect: the worshipper's declaration 'Allahu Akbar' means God is greater than everything else.
These two are related but distinct.
3
Reflect on the placement in prayer
Why is 'Allahu Akbar' said at the beginning of prayer? Consider how it purifies intention and attachment.
The author says it prevents the heart from clinging to anything other than God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأكبر فقيه وجهان : الأول : أنه أكبر من كل ما سواه من الموجودات ، ويحتمل أن يكون قول المصلي : الله أكبر هذا . كأنه يقول : الله أكبر من كل ما سواه. وإنما قدم هذا القول أمام أفعال الصلاة ، لأن المصلي إذا عرف هذا المعنى قبل الشروع في الصلاة لم يتعلق خاطره بشيء سوى الله ، ولم يتعلق قلبه بغير الله . وأما الكبرياء فقال - عليه السلام - حاكياً عن الله : ( الكبرياء ردائي ، والعظمة إزاري ، وفي تخصيص لفظ الكبرياء بالرداء ، والعظمة بالإزار ما يدل على أن الكبرياء أعلى شأناً من العظمة ، وأبعد عن أوهام الخلق وأفهامهم .
As for al-Akbar, it has two aspects: the first is that He is greater than every existent other than Him, and it is possible that the saying of the worshipper 'Allahu Akbar' is this. It is as if he says: 'God is greater than everything other than Him.' And He only placed this saying at the beginning of the actions of prayer because when the worshipper knows this meaning before beginning the prayer, his mind does not become attached to anything other than God, nor his heart to anything other than God. And as for al-Kibriya, the Prophet – peace be upon him – said, relating from God: 'Pride is My cloak, and Grandeur is My robe.' And in the specification of the word al-Kibriya as the cloak, and al-ʿAdhamah as the robe, there is an indication that al-Kibriya is higher in station than al-ʿAdhamah, and further from the imaginations and understandings of creatures.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage examines two more divine names: al-Akbar and al-Kibriya. For al-Akbar, the author gives two aspects: first, that God is akbar (greater) than all other existents, and second, that the phrase 'Allahu Akbar' used in prayer is understood as a declaration that God is greater than everything. The placement of this phrase at the beginning of prayer is explained: it purifies the worshipper's intention so that nothing else occupies the heart. Then the author discusses al-Kibriya (Pride). He cites the sacred hadith: 'Pride is My cloak, and Grandeur is My robe.' He notes that the specification of kibriya as the cloak and adhamah as the robe indicates that kibriya is even higher in station than adhamah, and more beyond the understanding of creatures. Thus, both names affirm God's absolute supremacy and transcendence.

Key Terms
الأكبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'Allahu Akbar' means God is physically larger in size.
Correction: The passage clarifies it means greater in status, majesty, and transcendence, not physical size.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
'Allahu Akbar' is a declaration that purifies the heart for exclusive devotion to God.

Al-Kibriya: The Divine Pride Beyond Creation's Reach
Author explains al-Kibriya via sacred hadith and contrasts with al-Adhamah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine name al-Kibriya and how it signifies God's absolute pride beyond human comprehension.

Core Idea
God's pride is His exclusive cloak, beyond all creation.

This lesson focuses on the divine name al-Kibriya (Pride). The author cites a sacred hadith that links pride to God's cloak and grandeur to His robe, indicating that pride is even higher than grandeur. This teaches us about God's supreme transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about al-Kibriya
Read the second part of the source passage, starting with 'وأما الكبرياء'. Pay attention to the hadith and the comparison.
2
Understand the symbolism of cloak and robe
The Prophet said: 'Pride is My cloak, and Grandeur is My robe.' Why do you think these specific garments were chosen?
A cloak is worn over other clothing, symbolizing externality and supremacy.
3
Grasp why kibriya is higher than adhamah
The author says that specifying kibriya as the cloak and adhamah as the robe indicates kibriya is higher in station and further from created understanding.
This shows the intensity of God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأكبر فقيه وجهان : الأول : أنه أكبر من كل ما سواه من الموجودات ، ويحتمل أن يكون قول المصلي : الله أكبر هذا . كأنه يقول : الله أكبر من كل ما سواه. وإنما قدم هذا القول أمام أفعال الصلاة ، لأن المصلي إذا عرف هذا المعنى قبل الشروع في الصلاة لم يتعلق خاطره بشيء سوى الله ، ولم يتعلق قلبه بغير الله . وأما الكبرياء فقال - عليه السلام - حاكياً عن الله : ( الكبرياء ردائي ، والعظمة إزاري ، وفي تخصيص لفظ الكبرياء بالرداء ، والعظمة بالإزار ما يدل على أن الكبرياء أعلى شأناً من العظمة ، وأبعد عن أوهام الخلق وأفهامهم .
As for al-Akbar, it has two aspects: the first is that He is greater than every existent other than Him, and it is possible that the saying of the worshipper 'Allahu Akbar' is this. It is as if he says: 'God is greater than everything other than Him.' And He only placed this saying at the beginning of the actions of prayer because when the worshipper knows this meaning before beginning the prayer, his mind does not become attached to anything other than God, nor his heart to anything other than God. And as for al-Kibriya, the Prophet – peace be upon him – said, relating from God: 'Pride is My cloak, and Grandeur is My robe.' And in the specification of the word al-Kibriya as the cloak, and al-ʿAdhamah as the robe, there is an indication that al-Kibriya is higher in station than al-ʿAdhamah, and further from the imaginations and understandings of creatures.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage examines two more divine names: al-Akbar and al-Kibriya. For al-Akbar, the author gives two aspects: first, that God is akbar (greater) than all other existents, and second, that the phrase 'Allahu Akbar' used in prayer is understood as a declaration that God is greater than everything. The placement of this phrase at the beginning of prayer is explained: it purifies the worshipper's intention so that nothing else occupies the heart. Then the author discusses al-Kibriya (Pride). He cites the sacred hadith: 'Pride is My cloak, and Grandeur is My robe.' He notes that the specification of kibriya as the cloak and adhamah as the robe indicates that kibriya is even higher in station than adhamah, and more beyond the understanding of creatures. Thus, both names affirm God's absolute supremacy and transcendence.

Key Terms
الكبرياء
العظمة
رداء
إزار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's pride is similar to human arrogance.
Correction: God's pride is exclusive and denotes His incomparable greatness, not a human flaw.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the specification of 'cloak' for al-Kibriya and 'robe' for al-Adhamah indicate?
A) That al-Kibriya is higher in station than al-Adhamah
B) That both are equal
C) That al-Adhamah is higher
D) That the Prophet chose words arbitrarily

Key Takeaway
God's pride (al-Kibriya) is the highest attribute of transcendence, beyond all created understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel proud of your achievements, do you ever unconsciously claim a sliver of the cloak that belongs only to Allah? How can you actively train your heart to attribute every success to Him alone?
?
God's pride is so high it is beyond human imagination. Yet we often feel entitled to be proud of our lineage, wealth, or knowledge. What specific step can you take today to lower yourself before Allah and acknowledge that true pride belongs only to Him?
?
The Prophet ﷺ said that pride is God's cloak. Have you ever tried to wear that cloak by looking down on others? Write down one situation where you did, and resolve to apologize or make amends.

The Two Meanings of al-Kabir (The Great)
The author explains the two meanings of the divine name al-Kabir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two aspects of the divine name al-Kabir and how it emphasizes God's transcendence over any resemblance to creation.

Core Idea
God's greatness is not physical but in His perfections and transcendence.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah explores the divine name al-Kabir (the Great). The author distinguishes between physical greatness and the greatness of intellectual and moral perfections. By understanding these two interpretations, we see that al-Kabir is a name affirming God's incomparability and supreme perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: the author first discusses the general meaning of 'great' and 'small', then applies it to God.
Pay attention to the two 'aspects' (وجهان) mentioned at the beginning and again later.
2
Distinguish physical vs. intellectual greatness
The author clarifies that 'great' can refer to physical size, but God is beyond that. The relevant meaning is in degrees of intellectual perfections and rank.
This distinction is crucial for understanding God's greatness correctly.
3
Examine the two aspects for God
The first aspect: God is the most perfect being, so He is great relative to all others. The second: He is too great to resemble creatures. Both are about transcendence.
The second aspect directly refutes anthropomorphism.
4
Recognize al-Kabir as a name of tanzih
Both interpretations lead to the conclusion that al-Kabir is a name of transcendence (tanzih), affirming that God is free from any similarity to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول : أما الكبير ففيه وجهان: أحدهما : أنه الذي يقع في مقابلة الصغير، وقد يعتبر الكبير والصغير في المقادير . والحق مقدس عن المقدار والحجمية فلا يكون كبره بحسب الجثة والحجمية. وقد يعتبر الكبر والصغر في درجات الكمالات العقلية فيقال : فلان كبير القوم ، وإن كان أصغرهم في الجثة ، ويقال فلان كبير في الدين . أي أن درجته عالية إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : في تفسير الكبير بهذا المعنى في حق الله وجهان : الأول : أنه ثبت أن الحق - سبحانه - أكمل الموجودات وأشرفها وأجلها وأعلاها ، فيكون ـ سبحانه - كبيراً بالقياس إلى كل ما سواه ، وكل ما يغايره فهو صغير بالقياس إليه . والثاني : أنه كبير بمعنى أنه كبر عن مشابهة المخلوقات. وعلى هذين الوجهين فيكون الكبير من أسماء التنزيه .
And we say: As for al-Kabir (the Great), it has two aspects: One of them: that it is what falls in contrast to al-saghir (the small), and great and small may be considered in magnitudes. And al-Haqq (the Truth, God) is sanctified above magnitude and corporeality, so His greatness is not according to body and corporeality. And greatness and smallness may be considered in degrees of intellectual perfections; it is said: So-and-so is great (kabir) of the people, even if he is the smallest in body, and it is said: So-and-so is great in religion, meaning his rank is high. If you have understood this, we say: In interpreting al-Kabir in this sense regarding God, there are two aspects: The first: that it is established that al-Haqq (God) is the most perfect of existents, the most noble, the most majestic, and the highest, so He is great in relation to everything other than Him, and everything other than Him is small in relation to Him. The second: that He is great in the sense that He is too great to resemble creatures. And according to these two aspects, al-Kabir is among the names of tanzih (transcendence).
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Kabir and explains its two interpretations. The author first notes the general usage of 'great' and 'small' in physical magnitudes, but clarifies that God is transcendent above physical dimensions, so His greatness is not bodily. Then he offers two meanings of 'great' in intellectual and moral ranks. Applying this to God, he presents two aspects: first, God is the most perfect and highest in comparison to all else; second, God is far above resembling creatures. Both interpretations make al-Kabir a name of transcendence, affirming God's incomparability.

Key Terms
الكبير
الصغير
التنزيه
الكمالات العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's greatness is like physical bigness or size.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God is sanctified above magnitude and corporeality (مقدس عن المقدار والحجمية), so His greatness is not bodily. Instead, it is in perfections and in being too great to resemble creatures.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is one of the two interpretations of 'al-Kabir' as a divine name?
A) God is great in physical size.
B) God is great in the sense of being too great to resemble creatures.
C) God is great because He has a large body.
D) God is great only in comparison to other gods.

Key Takeaway
Al-Kabir is a name of transcendence, affirming God's supreme perfection and incomparability with creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allahu Akbar', do you tend to think of it as a comparative statement (He is bigger than everything) or as a declaration of His absolute transcendence (He is beyond all comparison)? Which aspect is more alive in your heart during prayer? Challenge yourself to mean both every time you say it.
?
Is there any attribute you unconsciously ascribe to God in a physical way (e.g., thinking of His 'greatness' as vast size)? What step can you take today to purify your mental image of God, based on this lesson?
?
The passage says God is too great to resemble any creature. Do you ever fall into the trap of imagining God's love, mercy, or power as merely amplified human versions? How can this understanding of al-Kabir reshape your relationship with Him?

The Meaning of al-Akbar and the Wisdom of Takbir
Author explains al-Akbar and the placement of takbir in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first aspect of the divine name al-Akbar and why the takbir is recited at the start of prayer.

Core Idea
Allahu Akbar means God is greater than all else, freeing the heart from attachment.

The author explains one of the key names of God: al-Akbar (the Greater). He then connects this name to the opening phrase of Muslim prayer, 'Allahu Akbar', showing how understanding its meaning prepares the worshiper to focus entirely on God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation to get the flow.
Note the two aspects mentioned, though only the first is detailed.
2
Understand the first aspect
The first aspect is that God is greater than everything else. This is the core meaning of al-Akbar.
3
Grasp the wisdom of takbir
Understanding that God is greater than all prevents the heart from attaching to anything else during prayer. That is why takbir is the first act.
The author uses 'tacalluq' (attachment) to explain the psychological effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأكبر ففيه وجهان : الأول : أنه أكبر من كل ما سواه من الموجودات ، ويحتمل أن يكون قول المصلي : الله أكبر هذا . كأنه يقول : الله أكبر من كل ما سواه. وإنما قدم هذا القول أمام أفعال الصلاة ، لأن المصلي إذا عرف هذا المعنى قبل الشروع في الصلاة لم يتعلق خاطره بشيء سوى الله ، ولم يتعلق قلبه بغير الله
And as for al-Akbar (the Greater), it has two aspects: The first: that He is greater than everything other than Him among existents, and it is probable that the saying of the worshiper 'Allahu Akbar' is this; as if he says: 'God is greater than everything other than Him.' And this saying is only placed at the beginning of the actions of prayer, because when the worshiper knows this meaning before embarking on prayer, his mind does not attach to anything besides God, and his heart does not cling to other than God.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage explains the name al-Akbar (the Greater). The first aspect is that God is greater than all other existents. The author then relates this to the takbir (saying 'Allahu Akbar') in prayer: the worshiper means that God is greater than everything else. This is why the takbir is placed at the start of the prayer actions: when the worshiper understands this meaning before beginning, his mind and heart become focused solely on God, detached from anything else.

Key Terms
الأكبر
الله أكبر
الصلاة
التعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: 'Allahu Akbar' means 'God is big' in a non-comparative sense.
Correction: The passage explains that it means 'God is greater than everything else.'

Checkpoint
Why is the takbir ('Allahu Akbar') placed at the beginning of the actions of prayer according to this passage?
A) To announce the start of prayer loudly.
B) So that the worshiper's mind and heart are attached only to God.
C) Because it is a mandatory part of the prayer.
D) To distinguish Muslims from non-Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Saying 'Allahu Akbar' means acknowledging that God is greater than all else, which clears the heart for worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allahu Akbar' in your next prayer, will you consciously declare that God is greater than every worry, distraction, and desire in your heart? What is one specific attachment you will release right then?
?
If your mind often wanders during prayer, could it be because you haven't truly affirmed God's greatness over those very thoughts? What would it take for you to genuinely feel 'God is greater than this' in that moment?
?
What worldly attachment are you most afraid to let go of during prayer? Will you trust that God's greatness is enough to replace it, starting today?

The Hierarchy of Divine Names: al-Kibriya' and al-Azama
The author explains the name al-Kibriya' based on a hadith qudsi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a prophetic hadith distinguishes between the divine names al-Kibriya' and al-Azama, showing that al-Kibriya' denotes a higher degree of transcendence.

Core Idea
The cloak of al-Kibriya' outranks the waist-wrapper of al-Azama.

This lesson delves into a beautiful hadith qudsi where Allah Himself describes His attributes of majesty and pride. The choice of clothing imagery — cloak and waist-wrapper — is not arbitrary; it teaches us a profound hierarchy among divine names. We will see how the author uses this to assert that al-Kibriya' is more remote from human comprehension than al-Azama.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Focus on the exact wording and the imagery of the cloak and waist-wrapper.
Notice that the hadith is narrated by the Prophet (peace be upon him) from Allah.
2
Understand the symbolism
Why a cloak and a waist-wrapper? What do they signify about rank? The cloak is worn over the waist-wrapper, implying higher status.
This symbolism is key to the author's argument.
3
Grasp the conclusion
How does the specification of these garments indicate that al-Kibriya' is higher in rank and further from human understanding?
The author explicitly states that al-Kibriya' is أعلى شأناً (higher in rank) and أبعد عن أوهام الخلق (further from imaginations).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكبرياء فقال - عليه السلام - حاكياً عن الله : ( الكبرياء ردائي ، والعظمة إزاري ، وفي تخصيص لفظ الكبرياء بالرداء ، والعظمة بالإزار ما يدل على أن الكبرياء أعلى شأناً من العظمة ، وأبعد عن أوهام الخلق وأفهامهم .
And as for al-Kibriya' (pride, magnificence), he (peace be upon him) said, narrating from God: 'Al-Kibriya' is My cloak, and al-Azama (majesty) is My waist-wrapper.' And in the specification of the word al-Kibriya' with the cloak, and al-Azama with the waist-wrapper, there is an indication that al-Kibriya' is higher in rank than al-Azama, and further from the imaginations and understandings of creatures.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage discusses the divine name al-Kibriya' based on a hadith qudsi where God says: 'Al-Kibriya' is My cloak, and al-Azama is My waist-wrapper.' The author notes that the choice of clothing items indicates that al-Kibriya' is more exalted than al-Azama, as the cloak (rida') is worn over the waist-wrapper (izar), symbolizing a higher rank. Thus al-Kibriya' is more transcendent and further from human comprehension.

Key Terms
الكبرياء
العظمة
الرداء
الإزار
الحديث القدسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Kibriya' and al-Azama are synonyms denoting the same divine attribute.
Correction: The hadith and its analysis show that al-Kibriya' is higher and more transcendent than al-Azama.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the hadith qudsi ('al-Kibriya' is My cloak, al-Azama is My waist-wrapper') indicate?
A) That God wears clothing.
B) That al-Kibriya' is higher in rank than al-Azama.
C) That al-Azama is more important than al-Kibriya'.
D) That both names are equal.

Key Takeaway
The hadith qudsi teaches that al-Kibriya' is more exalted and transcendent than al-Azama, as symbolized by the cloak over the waist-wrapper.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-Kibriya', do you truly feel that His grandeur is beyond your comprehension, or do you subtly imagine Him in a familiar, limited way? What would you need to change in your mindset to truly live in awe of His transcendence?
?
The hadith places the cloak over the waist-wrapper, symbolizing a hierarchy. Do you ever treat Allah's attributes as if they are all equal, or as if you fully grasp them? How would your worship differ if you constantly reminded yourself that al-Kibriya' is far beyond your imagination?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that you have 'figured out' who Allah is or that you understand His essence? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what specific attitude adjustment can you commit to starting today to increase your reverence for al-Kibriya'?
